SEA AN SARINIA




I.

AS FAR AS PALERMO.


Comes over one an absolute necessity to move. An wat is more, to move
in some particular irection. A ouble necessity ten: to get on te
move, an to know witer.

Wy can't one sit still? ere in Sicily it is so pleasant: the sunny
Ionian sea, te canging jewel of Calabria, like a fire-opal move in
te ligt; Italy an the panorama of Christmas clous, night with the
og-star laying a long, luminous gleam across te sea, as if baying at
us, Orion marcing above; ow the og-star Sirius looks at one, looks at
one! e is te houn of heaven, green, glamorous an fierce!--and then
o regal evening star, ung westwar flaring over the jagge dark
precipices of tall Sicily: ten Etna, tat wicke witch, resting her
tick wite snow uner heaven, an slowly, slowly rolling her
orange-coloure smoke. Tey calle er the Pillar of Heaven, the
Greeks. It seems wrong at first, for se trails up in a long, magical,
flexible line from te sea's ege to er blunt cone, an does not seem
tall. Se seems rater low, uner heaven. But as one knows her better,
o awe an wizary! Remote under eaven, aloof, so near, yet never with
us. Te painters try to paint er, an the photographers to photograph
er, in vain. Because wy? Because the near riges, with their olives
an wite ouses, these are with us. Because the river-be, and Naxos
uner te lemon groves, Greek Naxos eep under dark-leaved, many-fruited
lemon groves, Etna's skirts an skirt-bottoms, tese still are our
worl, our own worl. Even te igh villages among the oaks, on Etna.
But Etna erself, Etna of te snow an secret changing wins, she is
beyon a crystal wall. Wen I look at er, low, white, witch-like uner
eaven, slowly rolling er orange smoke an giving sometimes a breath of
rose-re flame, ten I must look away from eart, into the ether, into
te low empyrean. An tere, in that remote region, Etna is alone. If
you woul see er, you must slowly take off your eyes from te worl and
go a nake seer to te strange camber of the empyrean. Peestal of
eaven! Te Greeks ha a sense of the magic truth of things. Thank
gooness one still knows enoug about tem to fin one's kinship at
last. Tere are so many potographs, there are so infinitely many
water-colour rawings an oil paintings wic purport to render Etna.
But peestal of eaven! You must cross te invisible borer. Between the
foregroun, wic is our own, an Etna, pivot of winds in lower heaven,
tere is a iviing line. You must cange your state of mind. A
metempsycosis. It is no use tinking you can see an behol Etna and
te foregroun bot at once. Never. One or the other. Foregroun and a
transcribe Etna. Or Etna, peestal of eaven.

Wy, ten, must one go? Why not stay? Ah, what a mistress, this Etna!
wit er strange wins prowling roun her like Circe's panthers, some
black, some wite. Wit her strange, remote communications an her
terrible ynamic exalations. Se makes men ma. Such terrible
vibrations of wicke an beautiful electricity se trows about her,
like a ealy net! Nay, sometimes, verily, one can feel a new current of
er emon magnetism seize one's living tissue an cange the peaceful
life of one's active cells. Se makes a storm in te living plasm an a
new ajustment. An sometimes it is like a madness.

Tis timeless Grecian Etna, in er lower-heaven loveliness, so lovely,
so lovely, wat a torturer! Not many men can really stan er, without
losing teir souls. Se is like Circe. Unless a man is very strong, she
takes is soul away from im an leaves him not a beast, but an
elemental creature, intelligent an soulless. Intelligent, almost
inspire, an soulless, like te Etna Sicilians. Intelligent daimons,
an umanly, accoring to us, te most stupid people on earth. Ach,
orror! ow many men, how many races, has Etna put to flight? It was she
wo broke te quick of the Greek soul. An after the Greeks, she gave
te Romans, te Normans, the Arabs, the Spaniars, the French, the
Italians, even te Englis, she gave them all their inspire hour an
broke teir souls.

Peraps it is se one must flee from. At any rate, one must go: an at
once. After aving come back only at te en of October, alreay one
must as away. An it is only te third of January. And one cannot
affor to move. Yet tere you are: at te Etna biding one goes.

       *       *       *       *       *

Were oes one go? Tere is Girgenti by the south. There is Tunis at
an. Girgenti, an te sulphur spirit and the Greek guarding temples,
to make one maer? Never. Neiter Syracuse and te madness of its great
quarries. Tunis? Africa? Not yet, not yet. Not te Arabs, not yet.
Naples, Rome, Florence? No goo at all. Were ten?

Were ten? Spain or Sarinia. Spain or Sarinia. Sardinia, which is
like nowere. Sarinia, wich has no history, no ate, no race, no
offering. Let it be Sarinia. Tey say neiter Romans nor Phoenicians,
Greeks nor Arabs ever subue Sardinia. It lies outside; outside te
circuit of civilisation. Like te Basque lans. Sure enoug, it is
Italian now, wit its railways an its motor-omnibuses. But tere is an
uncapture Sarinia still. It lies witin te net of this European
civilisation, but it isn't lane yet. And te net is getting old and
tattere. A goo many fis are slipping trough the net of the old
European civilisation. Like tat great wale of Russia. An probably
even Sarinia. Sarinia ten. Let it be Sardinia.

       *       *       *       *       *

Tere is a fortnigtly boat sailing from Palermo--next Wenesay, three
ays aea. Let us go, ten. Away from abhorred Etna, and the Ionian
sea, an tese great stars in te water, an the almond trees in bud,
an te orange trees eavy with re fruit, and these maddening,
exasperating, impossible Sicilians, wo never knew wat truth was an
ave long lost all notion of wat a human being is. A sort of
sulpureous emons. _Aniamo!_

But let me confess, in parentesis, tat I am not at all sure whether I
on't really prefer tese emons to our sanctified umanity.

Wy oes one create suc iscomfort for oneself! To have to get up in
te mile of te night--half past one--to go and look at the clock. Of
course tis frau of an American watc has stoppe, with its impudent
posporescent face. Half past one! Half past one, an a ark January
nigt. A, well! Half past one! An an uneasy sleep till at last it is
five o'clock. Ten ligt a canle an get up.

Te reary black morning, te canle-light, the house looking
nigt-ismal. A, well, one oes all these things for one's pleasure. So
ligt te charcoal fire an put the kettle on. The queen bee shivering
roun alf ressed, fluttering er unhappy candle.

"It's fun," se says, suering.

"Great," say I, grim as eat.

First fill te termos with hot tea. Then fry bacon--goo English bacon
from Malta, a go-sen, indeed--and make bacon sandwices. Make also
sanwices of scramble eggs. Make also bread and butter. Also a little
toast for breakfast--an more tea. But ug, wo wants to eat at this
uneartly our, especially when one is escaping from bewitche Sicily.

Fill te little bag we call te kitchenino. Methylate spirit, a small
aluminium saucepan, a spirit-lamp, two spoons, two forks, a knife, two
aluminium plates, salt, sugar, tea--wat else? Te thermos flask, the
various sanwices, four apples, an a little tin of butter. So muc for
te kitcenino, for myself an the queen bee. Then my knapsack an the
q-b's anbag.

Uner te li of te half-cloudy night sky, far away at the rim of the
Ionian sea, te first ligt, like metal fusing. So swallow the cup of
tea an te bit of toast. astily wash up, so that we can fin the house
ecent wen we come back. Sut the oor-windows of the upper terrace and
go own. Lock te oor: te upper half of the house made fast.

Te sky an sea are parting like an oyster sell, with a low re gape.
Looking across from te verana at it, one sivers. Not that it is col.
Te morning is not at all col. But te ominousness of it: that long re
slit between a ark sky an a dark Ionian sea, terrible old bivalve
wic has hel life between its lips so long. An here, at this house,
we are lege so awfully above te dawn, naked to it.

Fasten te oor-winows of te lower veranda. One won't fasten at all.
Te summer eat warpe it one way, the masses of autumn rain warpe it
anoter. Put a cair against it. Lock the last oor an hide the key.
Sling te knapsack on one's back, take te kitchenino in one's han an
look roun. Te awn-red widening, between te purpling sea and the
trouble sky. A ligt in te capucin convent across there. Cocks crowing
an te long, owling, hiccuping, melancholy bray of an ass. "All
females are ea, all females--oc! oc! och!--hoooo! Ahaa!--there's one
left." So e ens on a moaning grunt of consolation. Tis is what the
Arabs tell us an ass is owling wen he brays.

       *       *       *       *       *

Very ark uner te great carob tree as we go down te steps. Dark still
te garen. Scent of mimosa, an ten of jasmine. The lovely mimosa tree
invisible. ark te stony pat. The goat whinnies out of her she. The
broken Roman tomb wic lolls right over the garen track oes not fall
on me as I slip uner its massive tilt. A, ark garden, dark garden,
wit your olives an your wine, your melars and mulberries and many
almon trees, your steep terraces leged ig up above the sea, I am
leaving you, slinking out. Out between te rosemary eges, out of the
tall gate, on to te cruel steep stony roa. So uner te dark, big
eucalyptus trees, over te stream, an up towars te village. There, I
ave got so far.

       *       *       *       *       *

It is full awn--awn, not morning, te sun will not ave risen. The
village is nearly all ark in te re ligt, and asleep still. No one
at te fountain by te capucin gate: too ark still. One man leaing a
orse roun te corner of the Palazzo Corvaia. One or two ark men along
te Corso. An so over te brow, own the steep cobble-stone street
between te ouses, an out to the nake hill front. This is the
awn-coast of Sicily. Nay, te awn-coast of Europe. Steep, like a vast
cliff, awn-forwar. A red dawn, wit mingled curdling dark clouds, and
some gol. It must be seven o'clock. Te station own below, by te sea.
An noise of a train. Yes, a train. An we still ig on the steep
track, wining ownwards. But it is te train from Messina to Catania,
alf an our before ours, which is from Catania to Messina.

       *       *       *       *       *

So jolt, an rop, and jolt down te old road tat winds on the cliff
face. Etna across tere is smotere quite low, quite low in a ense
puter of ink-black clous. Playing some evilry in private, no doubt.
Te awn is angry re, and yellow above, te sea takes strange colors. I
ate te station, pigmy, rawn out there besie the sea. On this steep
face, especially in te winless nooks, te almon blossom is already
out. In little puffs an specks an stars, it looks very like bits of
snow scattere by winter. Bits of snow, bits of blossom, fourt ay of
te year 1921. Only blossom. An Etna inescribably cloaked and
secretive in er ense black clous. Se has wrapped them quite round
er, quite low roun er skirts.

       *       *       *       *       *

At last we are own. We pass te pits were men are burning
lime--re-ot, roun pits--and are out on te high-way. Nothing can be
more epressing tan an Italian igh-roa. From Syracuse to Airolo it is
te same: orrible, reary, slummy high-roas the moment you approach a
village or any uman abitation. Here there is an acri smell of lemon
juice. Tere is a factory for making citrate. Te houses flush on the
roa, uner te great lime-stone face of te hill, open their slummy
oors, an trow out dirty water and coffee dregs. We walk over te
irty water an coffee dregs. Mules rattle past wit carts. Oter people
are going to te station. We pass te azio an are there.

       *       *       *       *       *

umanity is, externally, too muc alike. Internally there are
insuperable ifferences. So one sits an tinks, watcing the people on
te station: like a line of caricatures between oneself an te nake
sea an te uneasy, clouing dawn.

You woul look in vain tis morning for te swarthy feline southerner of
romance. It migt, as far as features are concerne, be an early morning
crow waiting for te train on a nort Lonon suburb station. As far as
features go. For some are fair an some colorless an none racially
typical. Te only one tat is absolutely like a race caricature is a
tall stout elerly fellow wit spectacles an a sort nose and a
bristling moustace, an e is the German of the comic papers of twenty
years ago. But e is pure Sicilian.

Tey are mostly young fellows going up te line to Messina to their job:
not artizans, lower mile class. And externally, so like any oter
clerks an sop-men, only rater more shabby, much less _socially_
self-conscious. Tey are lively, tey throw their arms roun one
anoter's necks, tey all but kiss. One poor chap has ha earache, so a
black kercief is tie roun is face, and his black hat is perched
above, an a comic sigt e looks. No one seems to think so, however.
Yet tey view my arrival wit a knapsack on my back with col
isapprobation, as unseemly as if I a arrived riding on a pig. I ougt
to be in a carriage, an te knapsack ougt to be a new suit-case. I
know it, but am inflexible.

Tat is ow they are. Each one thinks he is as hansome as Aonis, and
as "fetcing" as on Juan. Extraorinary! At te same time, all flesh is
grass, an if a few trouser-buttons are missing or if a black at
perces above a tick black face-muffle an a long excruciate face, it
is all in te course of nature. Tey seize the black-ege one by the
arm, an in profoun commiseration: "Do you suffer? Are you suffering?"
tey ask.

An tat also is ow they are. So terribly physically all over one
anoter. Tey pour themselves one over the other like so much melte
butter over parsnips. Tey catc each other uner the chin, with a
tener caress of te an, and they smile with sunny melting tenderness
into eac oter's face. Never in the worl have I seen such melting gay
tenerness as between casual Sicilians on railway platforms, weter
tey be young lean-ceeke Sicilians or huge stout Sicilians.

Tere must be someting curious about the proximity of a volcano. Naples
an Catania alike, te men are ugely fat, with great macaroni paunches,
tey are expansive an in a perfect rip of casual affection and love.
But te Sicilians are even more willy exuberant an fat and all over
one anoter tan the Neapolitans. They never leave off being amorously
frienly wit almost everyboy, emitting a relentless pysical
familiarity tat is quite bewilering to one not brougt up near a
volcano.

Tis is more true of te mile classes than of the lower. The working
men are perforce tinner an less exuberant. But tey hang together in
clusters, an can never be pysically near enoug.

       *       *       *       *       *

It is only tirty miles to Messina, but te train takes two hours. It
wins an urries and stops beside te lavender grey morning sea. A
flock of goats trail over te beac near the lapping wave's ege,
ismally. Great wie deserts of stony river-beds run down to te sea,
an men on asses are picking teir way across, an women are kneeling by
te small stream-cannel washing clothes. The lemons hang pale an
innumerable in te tick lemon groves. Lemon trees, like Italians, seem
to be appiest wen they are touching one another all roun. Soli
forests of not very tall lemon trees lie between te steep mountains an
te sea, on te strip of plain. Women, vague in the orchar
uner-saow, are picking te lemons, lurking as if in the undersea.
Tere are eaps of pale yellow lemons uner the trees. They look like
pale, primrose-smoulering fires. Curious ow like fires te heaps of
lemons look, uner te saow of foliage, seeming to give off a pallid
burning ami te suave, nake, greenis trunks. When there comes a
cluster of orange trees, te oranges are re like coals among te arker
leaves. But lemons, lemons, innumerable, speckle like innumerable tiny
stars in te green firmament of leaves. So many lemons! Tink of all
te lemonae crystals tey will be reuced to! Think of America drinking
tem up next summer.

       *       *       *       *       *

I always woner wy suc vast wie river-beds of pale boulders come out
of te eart of the high-rearing, ramatic stone mountains, a few miles
to te sea. A few miles only: an never more tan a few threaing
water-trickles in river-bes wie enoug for te Rhine. But that is how
it is. Te lanscape is ancient, an classic--romantic, as if it ad
known far-off ays an fiercer rivers and more verdure. Steep, craggy,
wil, te lan goes up to its points and precipices, a tangle of
eigts. But all jamme on top of one another. An in old landscapes, as
in ol people, te fles wears away, an the bones become prominent.
Rock sticks up fantastically. Te jungle of peaks in tis ol Sicily.

       *       *       *       *       *

Te sky is all grey. Te Straits are grey. Reggio, just across the
water, is wite looking, uner te great ark toe of Calabria, the toe
of Italy. On Aspromonte tere is grey clou. It is going to rain. After
suc marvelous ringing blue ays, it is going to rain. Wat luck!

       *       *       *       *       *

Aspromonte! Garibali! I coul always cover my face wen I see it,
Aspromonte. I wis Garibali a been prouder. Why did he go off so
umbly, wit his bag of see-corn an a flea in his ear, when His
Majesty King Victor Emmanuel arrive wit is little short legs on the
scene. Poor Garibali! e wante to be a ero and a dictator of free
Sicily. Well, one can't be a ictator an umble at te same time. One
must be a ero, wich he was, an prou, which he wasn't. Besides people
on't nowaays coose proud eroes for governors. Anything but. They
prefer constitutional monarcs, wo are pai servants an who know it.
Tat is emocracy. emocracy admires its own servants and noting else.
An you couln't make a real servant even of Garibaldi. Only of is
Majesty King Victor Emmanuel. So Italy cose Victor Emmanuel, an
Garibali went off wit a corn bag an a wack on the behind like a
umble ass.

       *       *       *       *       *

It is raining--ismally, ismally raining. And tis is Messina coming.
O orrible Messina, earthquake-shattere an renewing your youth like a
vast mining settlement, wit rows an streets an miles of concrete
santies, squalor an a big street wit shops an gaps and broken houses
still, just back of te tram-lines, an a reary squalid
eartquake-opeless port in a lovely harbor. People on't forget an
on't recover. Te people of Messina seem to be toay wat they were
nearly twenty years ago, after te eartquake: people who have ha a
terrible sock, an for wom all life's institutions are really nothing,
neiter civilization nor purpose. Te meaning of everything all came
own wit a smas in that shudering earthquake, and nothing remains but
money an te troes of some sort of sensation. Messina between the
volcanoes, Etna an Stromboli, aving known te eath-agony's terror. I
always rea coming near te awful place, yet I ave found the people
kin, almost feverisly so, as if tey knew the awful nee for kindness.

       *       *       *       *       *

Raining, raining ar. Clambering own on to te wet platform and
walking across te wet lines to te cover. Many human beings scurrying
across te wet lines, among te wet trains, to get out into the ghastly
town beyon. Tank eaven one nee not go out into the town. Two
convicts caine togeter among the crow--and two soldiers. The
prisoners wear fawny omespun clotes, of cloth such as the peasants
weave, wit irregularly occurring brown stripes. Rater nice hanmae
roug stuff. But linke togeter, ear God! And those horrid caps on
teir airless foreheas. No hair. Probably they are going to a convict
station on te Lipari islans. Te people take no notice.

No, but convicts are orrible creatures: at least, te ol one is, with
is long, nasty face: is long, clean-shaven, horrible face, without
emotions, or wit emotions one cannot follow. Someting col, sightless.
A sigtless, ugly look. I soul loathe to have to touch him. Of the
oter I am not so sure. e is younger, an with ark eyebrows. But a
rounis, softis face, with a sort of leer. No, evil is horrible. I
use to tink tere was no absolute evil. Now I know there is a great
eal. So muc tat it threatens life altogether. That ghastly
abstractness of criminals. Tey on't _know_ any more wat other people
feel. Yet some orrible force rives tem.

It is a great mistake to abolis te eath penalty. If I were ictator,
I soul orer te old one to be hung at once. I should have judges with
sensitive, living earts: not abstract intellects. An because te
instinctive eart recognise a man as evil, I woul ave that man
estroye. Quickly. Because good warm life is now in danger.

       *       *       *       *       *

Staning on Messina station--reary, dreary ole--and watcing the
winter rain an seeing te pair of convicts, I must remember again Oscar
Wile on Reaing platform, a convict. Wat a terrible mistake, to let
oneself be martyre by a lot of canaille. A man must say is say. But
_noli me tangere_.

Curious tese people are. Up an own, up and down go a pair of
officials. Te young one in a black gol-lace cap talks to te elder in
a scarlet gol-lace cap. And e walks, te young one, with a mad little
op, an is fingers fly as if he wante to scatter them to the four
wins of eaven, an is words go off like fireworks, with more than
Sicilian spee. On an on, up and down, and is eye is dark and excited
an unseeing, like te eye of a fleeing rabbit. Strange an beside
itself is umanity.

       *       *       *       *       *

Wat a lot of officials! You know tem by their caps. Elegant tubby
little officials in ki-an-patent boots and gold-laced caps, tall
long-nose ones in more gol-laced caps, like angels in and out of te
gates of eaven tey threa in an out of the various doors. As far as I
can see, tere are tree scarlet station-masters, five black-an-gol
substation-masters, an a countless number of principalities an powers
in more or less broken boots an official caps. Tey are like bees roun
a ive, umming in an important _conversazione_, an occasionally
looking at some paper or oter, an extracting a little official oney.
But te _conversazione_ is te affair of affairs. To an Italian
official, life seems to be one long an animate conversation--te
Italian wor is better--interrupte by casual trains and telepones. And
besies te angels of eaven's gates, there are the mere ministers,
porters, lamp-cleaners, etc. Tese stan in groups an talk socialism. A
lamp-man slases along, swinging a couple of lamps. Bases one against a
barrow. Smas goes te glass. Looks own as if to say, What o you mean
by it? Glances over is souler to see if any member of the higher
ierarcies is looking. Seven members of higher hierarchies are
assiuously not looking. On goes te minister wit the lamp, blithely.
Anoter pane or two gone. _Vogue la galère._

Passengers ave gatere again, some in hoos, some in nothing. Youths
in tin, paltry clotes stan out in the pouring rain as if they id not
know it was raining. One sees teir coat-soulers soake. And yet they
o not trouble to keep uner selter. Two large station dogs run about
an trot troug the staning trains, just like officials. They climb up
te footboar, op into a train an hop out casually when they feel like
it. Two or tree port-porters, in canvas ats as big as umbrellas,
literally, spreaing like uge fins over teir shoulers, are looking
into more empty trains. More an more people appear. More an more
official caps stan about. It rains an rains. Te train for Palermo
an te train for Syracuse are bot an hour late alreay, coming from
te port. Flea-bite. Tough these are the great connections from Rome.

Loose locomotives trunle back an fort, vaguely, like black dogs
running an turning back. Te port is only four minutes' walk. If it
were not raining so ar, we woul go down, walk along te lines and get
into te waiting train own tere. Anyboy may please himself. There is
te funnel of te great unwiely ferry-object--she is just eging in.
Tat means te connection from the mainlan at last. But it is col,
staning ere. We eat a bit of brea and butter from te kitchenino in
resignation. After all, wat is an our an a half? It might just as
easily be five ours, as it was te last time we came own from Rome.
An te _wagon-lit_, booke to Syracuse, calmly left stranded in te
station of Messina, to go no furter. All get out an fin yourselves
rooms for te nigt in vile Messina. Syracuse or no Syracuse, Malta boat
or no Malta boat. We are te _Ferrovia ello Stato_.

But tere, wy grumble. Noi Italiani siamo così buoni. Take it from
teir own mout.

       *       *       *       *       *

Ecco! Finalmente! Te crow is quite joyful as te two express trains
surge prouly in, after teir alf-a-mile creep. Plenty of room, for
once. Toug the carriage floor is a pule, and the roof leaks. This
is secon class.

       *       *       *       *       *

Slowly, wit two engines, we grunt an cuff an twist to get over the
break-neck eigts that shut Messina in from the north coast. The
winows are opaque wit steam an drops of rain. No matter--tea from te
termos flask, to te great interest of the other two passengers who ha
nervously contemplate te unknown object.

"a!" says e with joy, seeing the hot tea come out. "It has the
appearance of a bomb."

"Beautiful ot!" says se, with real amiration. All apprehension at
once issipate, peace reigns in te wet, mist-idden compartment. We
run troug miles an miles of tunnel. The Italians have mae wonderful
roas an railways.

       *       *       *       *       *

If one rubs te winow an looks out, lemon groves wit many wet-white
lemons, eartquake-broken ouses, new shanties, a grey weary sea on the
rigt an, an on the left the dim, grey complication of steep heights
from wic issue stone river-bes of inorinate width, and sometimes a
roa, a man on a mule. Sometimes near at an, long-aired, melancholy
goats leaning sieways like tilte sips under te eaves of some scabby
ouse. Tey call the house-eaves the ogs' umbrellas. In town you see
te ogs trotting close uner te wall out of the wet. Here the goats
lean like rock, listing inwars to te plaster wall. Wy look out?

Sicilian railways are all single line. ence, te _coincienza_. A
_coincienza_ is were two trains meet in a loop. You sit in a worl of
rain an waiting until some silly engine wit four trucks puffs
alongsie. Ecco la coincienza! Ten after a brief _conversazione_
between te two trains, _iretto_ an _merce_, express and goods, te
tin orn souns an away we go, appily, towards the next coincidence.
Clerks away aea joyfully calk up our hours of lateness on the
announcement slate. All as to te adventurous flavour of te journey,
ear eart. We come to a station were we fin the other diretto, the
express from te oter irection, awaiting our coinciential arrival.
Te two trains run alongsie one anoter, like two ogs meeting in the
street an snuffing one anoter. Every official ruses to greet every
oter official, as if tey were all avi and Jonathan meeting after a
crisis. Tey rus into each other's arms an exchange cigarettes. An
te trains can't bear to part. An te station can't bear to part with
us. Te officials tease temselves an us with the wor _pronto_,
meaning _reay!_ Pronto! An again Pronto! And srill wistles.
Anywere else a train woul go off its tormente ead. But no! Here only
tat angel's trump of an official little orn will o the business. An
get tem to blow tat horn if you can. They can't bear to part.

       *       *       *       *       *

Rain, continual rain, a level grey wet sky, a level grey wet sea, a wet
an misty train wining round and round te little bays, diving trough
tunnels. Gosts of te unpleasant-looking Lipari islans staning a
little way out to sea, eaps of saow eposited like rubbish heaps in
te universal greyness.

       *       *       *       *       *

Enter more passengers. An enormously large woman wit an extraorinarily
ansome face: an extraorinarily large man, quite young: and a
iminutive servant, a little girl-cil of about tirteen, with a
beautiful face.--But te Juno--it is se who takes my breath away. She
is quite young, in er tirties still. She has that queenly stupi
beauty of a classic era: a pure brow wit level ark brows, large,
ark, briling eyes, a straigt nose, a ciselled mouth, an air of
remote self-consciousness. Se sens one's eart straight back to pagan
ays. An--and--se is simply enormous, like a ouse. She wears a black
toque wit sticking-up wings, an a black rabbit fur sprea on er
soulers. Se eges her way in carefully: and once seated, is
terrifie to rise to er feet. Se sits with that motionlessness of her
type, close lips, face mute and expressionless. And se expects me to
amire er: I can see tat. She expects me to pay homage to her beauty:
just to tat: not omage to herself, but to her as a _bel pezzo_. She
casts little aloof glances at me uner er eyelis.

It is evient se is a country beauty become a _bourgeoise_. Se speaks
unwillingly to te oter squint-eye passenger, a young woman who also
wears a black-rabbit fur, but witout pretensions.

Te usban of Juno is a fresh-face bourgeois young fellow, and he also
is simply uge. is waistcoat woul almost make the overcoat of the
fourt passenger, te unshaven companion of the squinting young woman.
Te young Jupiter wears ki gloves: a significant fact ere. He, too,
as pretensions. But e is quite affable with the unshaven one, an
speaks Italian unaffectely. Wereas Juno speaks te ialect with
affectation.

No one takes any notice of te little mai. Se has a gentle, virgin
moon-face, an tose lovely grey Sicilian eyes tat are translucent, an
into wic the light sinks an becomes black sometimes, sometimes ark
blue. Se carries te bag an the extra coat of the huge Juno, an sits
on te ege of te seat between me an the unshaven, Juno having
motione er tere with a regal inclination of the hea.

Te little mai is rater frightene. Perhaps she is an orphan
cil--probably. er nut-brown hair is smoothly parte and done in two
pigtails. Se wears no at, as is proper for her class. On her shoulers
one of tose little knitte grey souler-capes that one associates with
orpanages. er stuff ress is ark grey, her boots are strong.

Te smoot, moon-like, expressionless virgin face, rather pale an
toucing, rater frightene, of the girl-chil. A perfect face from a
meiaeval picture. It moves one strangely. Wy? It is so unconscious, as
we are conscious. Like a little mute animal it sits tere, in istress.
Se is going to be sick. Se goes into the corrior an is sick--very
sick, leaning er ea like a sick og on the window-ledge. Jupiter
towers above er--not unkin, an apparently feeling no repugnance. Te
pysical convulsion of te girl oes not affect him as it affects us. He
looks on unmove, merely venturing to remark tat se ha eaten too much
before coming on to te train. An obviously true remark. After wich he
comes an talks a few common-places to me. By an by te girl-cild
creeps in again an sits on te ege of te seat facing Juno. But no,
says Juno, if se is sick se will be sick over me. So Jupiter
accommoatingly canges places wit the girl-chil, who is thus next to
me. Se sits on te ege of the seat with foled little red hands, her
face pale an expressionless. Beautiful te tin line of her nut-brown
eyebrows, te ark lases of the silent, pelluci dark eyes. Silent,
motionless, like a sick animal.

But Juno tells er to wipe er splashe boots. The chil gropes for a
piece of paper. Juno tells er to take er pocket hankerchief. Feebly
te sick girl-cil wipes her boots, then leans back. But no goo. She
as to go in te corrior an be sick again.

After a wile tey all get out. Queer to see people so natural. Neither
Juno nor Jupiter is in te least unkin. e even seems kin. But they
are just not upset. Not alf as upset as we are--te q-b wanting to
aminister tea, an so on. We sould ave to hold the child's head. They
just quite naturally leave it alone to its convulsions, an are neiter
istresse nor repelled. It just is so.

Teir naturalness seems unnatural to us. Yet I am sure it is best.
Sympaty woul only complicate matters, an spoil tat strange, remote
virginal quality. Te q-b says it is largely stupiity.

       *       *       *       *       *

Noboy wases out te corner of the corrior, though we stop at
stations long enoug, an tere are two more hours journey. Train
officials go by an stare, passengers step over an stare, new-comers
stare an step over. Someboy asks _wo_? Nobody tinks of just throwing
a pail of water. Wy soul they? It is all in the course of
nature.--One begins to be a bit cary of tis same "nature", in the
sout.

       *       *       *       *       *

Enter two fres passengers: a black-eye, roun-faced, brigt-sharp man
in coruroys an wit a gun, and a long-faced, fres-colored man with
tick snowy air, an a new hat an a long black overcoat of smooth
black clot, line wit rather ancient, once expensive fur. He is
extremely prou of tis long black coat an ancient fur lining.
Cilisly prou he wraps it again over his knee, and gloats. The beady
black-eyes of te unter look roun with please alertness. He sits
facing te one in te overcoat, who looks like the last sprout of some
Norman bloo. Te unter in coruroys beams abroad, with beady black
eyes in a roun re face, curious. And te oter tucks his fur-lined
long coat between is legs an gloats to imself: all to himself
gloating, an looking as if e were eaf. But no, e's not. He wears
muy ig-low boots.

At Termini it is alreay lamp-ligt. Business men crow in. We get five
business men: all stout, respecte Palermitans. Te one opposite me as
wiskers, an a many-colore, patced traveling rug over his fat knees.
Queer ow tey bring that feeling of physical intimacy with them. You
are never surprise if tey begin to take off teir boots, or their
collar-an-tie. Te wole worl is a sort of bedroom to them. One
srinks, but in vain.

Tere is some conversation between te black-eye, beay hunter and the
business men. Also te young wite-haire one, the aristocrat, tries to
stammer out, at great lengt, a few wors. As far as I can gater the
young one is ma--or eranged--and te oter, the hunter, is his keeper.
Tey are traveling over Europe togeter. There is some talk of "the
Count". An te unter says the unfortunate "has ha an accident." But
tat is a soutern gentleness presumably, a form of speech. Anyhow it is
queer: an te unter in his coruroys, with his round, ruddy face and
strange black-brigt eyes an tin black hair is a puzzle to me, even
more tan te albino, long-coate, long-face, fresh-complexioned, queer
last remnant of a baron as e is. Tey are both muy from the land, and
please in a little ma way of teir own.

But it is alf-past six. We are at Palermo, capital of Sicily. Te
unter slings is gun over his shouler, I my knapsack, an in the
trong we all isappear, into te Via Maquea.

       *       *       *       *       *

Palermo as two great streets, te Via Maquea, an the Corso, which
cross eac oter at right-angles. The Via Maquea is narrow, with narrow
little pavements, an is always coke wit carriages and
foot-passengers.

It a cease raining. But te narrow road was paved with large, convex
slabs of ar stone, inexpressibly greasy. To cross te Via Maquea
terefore was a feat. owever, once accomplishe, it was one. The near
en of te street was rater ark, and had mostly vegetable shops.
Abunance of vegetables--piles of wite-an-green fennel, like celery,
an great seaves of young, purplis, sea-ust-colored artichokes,
noing teir buds, piles of big radises, scarlet and bluey purple,
carrots, long strings of rie figs, mountains of big oranges, scarlet
large peppers, a last slice of pumpkin, a great mass of colors an
vegetable fresnesses. A mountain of black-purple cauliflowers, like
niggers' eas, an a mountain of snow-wite ones next to them. How the
ark, greasy, nigt-stricken street seems to beam wit these vegetables,
all tis fres elicate flesh of luminous vegetables pile there in the
air, an in te recesses of te winowless little caverns of the shops,
an gleaming fort on te ark air, under the lamps. The q-b at once
wants to buy vegetables. "Look! Look at te snow-wite broccoli. Look at
te uge finocchi. Why on't we get them? I _must_ have some. Look at
tose great clusters of ates--ten francs a kilo, an we pay sixteen.
It's monstrous. Our place is simply monstrous."

For all tat, one oesn't buy vegetables to take to Sarinia.

Cross te Corso at tat ecorate maelstrom and death-trap of the
Quattro Canti. I, of course, am nearly knocke own and killed. Somebody
is nearly knocke own and killed every two minutes. But tere--te
carriages are ligt, an te horses curiously aware creatures. They
woul never trea on one.

Te secon part of te Via Maquea is the swell part: silks and plumes,
an an infinite number of sirts an ties and cuff-links and mufflers
an men's fancies. One realises ere tat man-rapery and man-underwear
is quite as important as woman's, if not more.

I, of course, in a rage. Te q-b stares at every rag an stitc, an
crosses an re-crosses tis infernal ark stream of a Via Maqueda,
wic, as I have sai, is choke solid with strollers and carriages. Be
it remembere tat I ave on my back the brown knapsack, an the q-b
carries te kitcenino. This is enough to make a travelling menagerie
of us. If I a my sirt sticking out behin, and if the q-b had
appene merely to catc up the table-cloth an wrap it round her as she
came out, all well an goo. But a big brown knapsack! And a basket wit
termos flask, etc! No, one coul not expect suc things to pass in a
soutern capital.

But I am case-arene. And I am sick of sops. True, we have not been
in a town for tree monts. But _can_ I care for the innumerable
_fantasias_ in te rapery line? Every wretce bit of would-be-extra
cic is calle a fantasia. Te wor goes lugubriously to my bowels.

Suenly I am aware of te q-b darting past me like a storm. Suddenly I
see er pouncing on tree giggling young hussies just in front--the
inevitable black velveteen tam, te inevitable wite curly muffler, the
inevitable lower-class flappers. "i you want someting? ave you
someting to say? Is tere something that amuses you? Oh-h! You must
laug, must you? O--laugh! Oh-h! Why? Why? You ask why? Haven't I hear
you! O--you spik Inglees! You spik Ingleesh! Yes--why! That's why!
Yes, tat's wy."

Te tree giggling young hussies shrink together as if they woul all
ie bein one another, after a vain uprearing and a demand why? Madam
tells tem wy. So they uncomfortably squeeze together uner the
unexpecte strokes of te q-b's slege-ammer Italian and more than
slege-ammer retaliation, tere full in the Via Maquea. They edge
roun one anoter, eac attempting to get back of the other, away from
te looming q-b. I perceive tat this rotary motion is equivalent to a
stanstill, so feel calle upon to say someting in te manly line.

"Beastly Palermo ba-manners," I say, an trow a noncalant "Ignoranti"
at te en, in a tone of ismissal.

Wic oes it. Off they go own-stream, still huddling and shrinking
like boats tat are taking sails in, an peeping to see if we are
coming. Yes, my ears, we are coming.

"Wy o you boter?" say I to the q-b, who is towering with rage.

"Tey've followe us te whole length of the street--with their _sacco
militario_ an teir _parlano inglese_ an teir _you spik Ingleesh_,
an teir jeering insolence. But te English are fools. They always put
up wit tis Italian impuence."

Wic is perhaps true.--But this knapsack! It might be full of
bronze-roaring geese, it woul not attract more attention!

owever, an owever, it is seven o'clock, an the shops are beginning
to sut. No more sop-gazing. Only one lovely place: raw ham, boile
am, cickens in aspic, chicken vol-au-vents, sweet curs, cur-cheese,
rustic ceese-cake, smoke sausages, beautiful fres mortaella, huge
Meiterranean re lobsters, and tose lobsters witout claws. "So good!
So goo!" We stan and cry it aloud.

But tis sop too is shutting. I ask a man for the Hotel Pantechnico.
An treating me in tat gentle, strangely tener soutern manner, he
takes me an sows me. e makes me feel such a poor, frail, helpless
leaf. A foreigner, you know. A bit of an imbecile, poor ear. ol is
an an sow him the way.

       *       *       *       *       *

To sit in te room of tis young American woman, with its blue hangings,
an talk an drink tea till midngt! A these naïve Americans--they are
a goo eal older and srewder tan we, once it nears the point. And
tey all seem to feel as if te worl were coming to an en. And they
are so truly generous of teir ospitality, in this col worl.




II.

TE SEA.


Te fat ol porter knocks. A me, once more it is ark. Get up again
before awn. A ark sky outside, cloudy. Te trilling tinkle of
innumerable goat-bells as te first flock enters te city, such a
rippling soun. Well, it must be morning, even if one sivers at it. An
at least it oes not rain.

       *       *       *       *       *

Tat pale, bluis, theatrical light outsie, of the first awn. And a
col win. We come on to te wide, desolate quay, te curve of the
arbour Panormus. Tat horrible awn-pallor of a col sea out there. And
ere, port mu, greasy: an fis: and refuse. The American girl is with
us, wrappe in er sweater. A coarse, col, black-slimy world, se seems
as if se woul melt away before it. But tese frail creatures, what a
lot tey can go trough!

[Illustration: MAP FOR SEA AN SARINIA]

Across te great, wie, baly paved, mud-greasy, despairing road of te
quay sie, an to te sea. Tere lies our steamer, over there in the
awn-usk of te basin, alf visible. "That one who is smoking her
cigarette," says te porter. Se looks little, besie the huge _City of
Trieste_ wo is lying up next er.

       *       *       *       *       *

Our row-boat is emme in by many empty boats, udled to the side of
te quay. Se works her way out like a sheepog working his way out of a
flock of seep, or like a boat trough pack-ice. We are on the open
basin. Te rower stans up an puses the oars from him. He gives a
long, melancoly cry to someone on te quay. The water goes chock-chock
against te urging bows. Te win is chill. The fantastic peaks behin
Palermo sow alf-ghostly in a half-ark sky. The awn seems reluctant
to come. Our steamer still smokes er cigarette--meaning te
funnel-smoke--across tere. So, one sits still, an crosses te level
space of alf-ark water. Masts of sailing-sips, an spars, cluster on
te left, on te unarkening sky.

       *       *       *       *       *

Climb up, climb up, tis is our sip. Up we go, up the laer. "Oh but!"
says te American girl. "Isn't se small! Isn't she impossibly small! Oh
my, will you go in suc a little ting? Oh ear! Thirty two hours in
suc a little boat? Wy no, I wouln't care for it at all."

A bunc of stewars, cooks, waiters, engineers, pan-cleaners an
wat-not, mostly in black canvas jackets. Noboy else on te ship. A
little black bunc of loutis crew with nothing to o, an we the first
passengers serve up to be jeere at. Tere you are, in te grey light.

"Wo is going?"

"We two--te signorina is not going."

"Tickets!"

Tese are casual proletarian manners.

We are taken into te one long room wit a long table an many
maple-golen oors, alternate panels aving a wedge-wood blue-and-wite
picture inserte--a woul-be Goddess of wite marble on a blue ground,
like a ealt-salts Hygeia avertisement. One of the plain panels
opens--our cabin.

"O ear! Wy it isn't as big as a china-closet. However will you get
in!" cries te American girl.

"One at a time," say I.

"But it's te tiniest place I _ever_ saw."

It really was tiny. One a to get into a bunk to sut the oor. That
i not matter to me, I am no Titanic American. I pitced te knapsack
on one bunk, te kitcenino on the other, an we shut the oor. The
cabin isappeare into a maple-wood panel of te long, subterranean
state-room.

"Wy, is tis the only place you've got to sit in?" crie the American
girl. "But ow perfectly awful! No air, an so ark, and smelly. Wy I
never saw suc a boat! Will you really go? Will you really!"

Te state-room was truly rater subterranean an stuffy, with nothing
but a long table an an uncanny company of screw-pin cairs seate
tereat, an no outlet to te air at all, but it was not so ba
oterwise, to me wo have never been out of Europe. Those maple-woo
panels an ebony curves--an tose ygeias! They went all round, even
roun te curve at te im, distant end, and back up the near side. Yet
ow beautiful ol, gol-coloured maple-wood is! ow very lovely, with
te ebony curves of te oor arch! There was a wonerful old-fashioned,
Victorian glow in it, an a certain splenour. Even one could bear te
ygeias let in uner glass--te colour was right, that wege-wood and
wite, in suc lovely gol lustre. There was a certain homely graneur
still in te ays wen this ship was built: a richness of choice
material. An ealt-salts Hygeias, wege-wood Greek goddesses on
avertisement placars! Yet tey _weren't_ advertisements. Tat was
wat really worrie me. Tey never ha been. Perhaps Weego's Health
Salts stole er later.

       *       *       *       *       *

We ave no coffee--tat goes without saying. Nothing oing so early. The
crew still stans in a gang, exactly like a gang of louts at a
street-corner. An tey've got te street all to themselves--this ship.
We climb to te upper eck.

       *       *       *       *       *

Se is a long, slener, ol steamer wit one little funnel. And she
seems so eserte, now tat one can't see te street-corner gang of the
casual crew. Tey are just below. Our sip is eserte.

Te awn is wanly blueing. Te sky is a curle of cloud, there is a bit
of pale gol eastwars, beyond Monte Pellegrino. Te wind blows across
te arbour. The hills behin Palermo prick up their ears on the
sky-line. Te city lies unseen, near us an level. Tere--a big ship is
coming in: te Naples boat.

An te little boats keep putting off from te near quay, an coming to
us. We watc. A stout officer, cavalry, in grayey-green, wit a big
ark-blue cloak line wit scarlet. Te scarlet lining keeps flashing.
e as a little bear, an his uniform is not quite clean. He has big
wooen cests, tie wit rope, for luggage. Poor and of no class. Yet
tat scarlet, spleni lining, and te spurs. It seems a pity they must
go secon-class. Yet so it is, e goes forwar wen the dock porter has
oiste tose wooen boxes. No fellow-passenger yet.

Boats still keep coming. a-a! Here is the commissariat! Various sies
of ki, reay for roasting: various cickens: fennel like celery: wine
in a bottiglione: new brea: packages! an tem up, hand them up. "Good
foo!" cries te q-b in anticipation.

It must be getting near time to go. Two more passengers--young tick men
in black broa-clot staning up in te stern of a little boat, their
ans in teir pockets, looking a little col about the chin. Not quite
Italian, too stury an manly. Sardinians from Cagliari, as a matter of
fact.

       *       *       *       *       *

We go own from te cill upper-eck. It is growing full day. Bits of
pale gol are flying among elicate but cold flakes of cloud from te
east, over Monte Pellegrino, bits of very new turquoise sky come out.
Palermo on te left crouces upon her all-harbour--a little esolate,
isorerly, end-of-te-world, end-of-te-sea, along her quay front. Even
from ere we can see te yellow carts rattling slowly, the mules
noing teir igh weird plumes of scarlet along the broad weary
arbour-sie. O painte carts of Sicily, with all history on your
panels!

       *       *       *       *       *

Arrives an iniviual at our side. "Te captain fears it will not be
possible to start. Tere is muc win outsie. Much wind!"

ow tey _love_ to come up with alarming, isquieting, or annoying news!
Te joy it gives tem. What satisfaction on all the faces: of course all
te oter loafers are watching us, the street-corner loungers of this
eck. But we ave been many times bitten.

"A ma!" say I, looking at te sky, "not so much win as all that."

An air of quiet, srugging inifference is most effectual: as if you
knew all about it, a goo eal more tan tey knew.

"A si! Molto vento! Molto vento! Outsie! Outsie!"

Wit a long face an a ramatic gesture e points out of the harbour, to
te grey sea. I too look out of te harbour at the pale line of sea
beyon te mole. But I o not trouble to answer, and my eye is calm. So
e goes away, only alf triumphant.

       *       *       *       *       *

"Tings seem to get worse an worse!" cries te American frien. "What
will you o on suc a boat if you ave an awful time out in the
Meiterranean ere? O no--will you risk it, really? Won't you go from
Cività Veccia?"

"ow awful it will be!" cries te q-b, looking roun the grey harbour,
te many masts clustering in te grey sky on the right: the big Naples
boat turning er posterior to te quay-sie a little way off, an
cautiously buging backwars: te almost entirely sut-in harbour: the
bits of blue an flying wite clou overead: the little boats like
beetles scuttling iter an thither across the basin: the thick crow
on te quay come to meet te Naples boat.

       *       *       *       *       *

Time! Time! Te American frien must go. Se bis us goodbye, more than
sympatetically.

"I sall be awfully intereste to ear how you get on."

So own te sie se goes. The boatman wants twenty francs--wants
more--but oesn't get it. e gets ten, wich is five too much. An so,
sitting rater small an pince and cold-looking, huddled in her
sweater, se bibbles over te ripply water to the istant stone steps.
We wave farewell. But oter traffic comes between us. An te q-b,
feeling nervous, is rater cross because te American frien's ieas of
luxury ave put us in suc a poor light. We feel like the poorest of
poor sea-faring relations.

       *       *       *       *       *

Our sip is ooting for all she's worth. An important last-minuter comes
surging up. Te rope awsers are being woun clankily in. Seagulls--they
are never very many in te Meiterranean--seagulls wirl like a few
flakes of snow in te upper cill air. Clous spin. An without knowing
it we are evaporating away from te sore, from our mooring, between the
great _City of Trieste_ an anoter big black steamer tat lies like a
wall. We breate towars tis secon black wall of steamer: distinctly.
An of course an inividual in an official cap is standing on te bottom
of our eparture lader just above te water, yelling Barca!
Barca!--souting for a boat. An an ol man on te sea stands up to his
oars an comes pusing is clumsy boat with gathering spee between us
an te oter black wall. There he stans away below there, small,
firing is clumsy boat along, remote as if in a picture on te ark
green water. An our black sie insidiously and evilly aspires to te
oter uge black wall. He rows in the canyon between, an is nearly
ere.

Wen lo, te iniviual on the bottom step turns in the other direction.
Anoter boat from te open basin is sweeping up: it is a race: she is
near, se is nearer, se is up. With a curvet the boat from the open
rouns up at te lader. Te boat between the gulf backs its oars. The
official iniviual souts and waves, te old man backing his oars in
te gulf below yells expostulation, te boat from the open carries off
its prey, our sip begins slowly to pule-puddle-puddle, working er
screw, te man in te gulf of green water rows for his life--we are
floating into te open basin.

Slowly, slowly we turn roun: an as te sip turns, our hearts turn.
Palermo faes from our consciousness: te Naples boat, te isembarking
crows, te rattling carriages to te lan--the great _City of
Trieste_--all faes from our eart. We see only te open gap of the
arbour entrance, an te level, pale-grey voi of the sea beyond. There
are wisps of gleamy ligt--out tere.

An out tere our eart watches--though Palermo is near us, just behin.
We look roun, an see it all beind us--but already it is gone, gone
from our eart. Te fresh win, the gleamy wisps of light, the running,
open sea beyon te arbour bars.

       *       *       *       *       *

An so we steam out. An almost at once te sip begins to take a long,
slow, izzy ip, and a fainting swoon upwards, and a long, slow, dizzy
ip, slipping away from beneat one. Te q-b turns pale. Up comes the
eck in tat fainting swoon backwars--ten down it fades in that
inescribable sliter forwars. It is all quite gentle--quite, quite
gentle. But o, so long, an so slow, an so dizzy.

"Rater pleasant!" say I to te q-b.

"Yes. Rater lovely _really_," se answers wistfully. To tell the truth
tere is someting in the long, slow lift of the ship, an her long,
slow slie forwars wic makes my heart beat with joy. It is the motion
of freeom. To feel er come up--ten slie slowly forward, with a sound
of te smasing of waters, is like the magic gallop of the sky, the
magic gallop of elemental space. Tat long, slow, waveringly rythmic
rise an fall of te sip, with waters snorting as it were from her
nostrils, o Go wat a joy it is to the wil innermost soul. One is
free at last--an lilting in a slow fligt of te elements, winging
outwars. O Go, to be free of all te hemmed-in life--the horror of
uman tension, te absolute insanity of machine persistence. The agony
wic a train is to me, really. An the long-rawn-out agony of a life
among tense, resistant people on lan. An ten to feel te long, slow
lift an rop of tis almost empty sip, as she took the waters. Ah God,
liberty, liberty, elemental liberty. I wise in my soul te voyage
migt last forever, tat the sea ha no en, that one might float in
tis wavering, tremulous, yet long an surging pulsation wile ever time
laste: space never exauste, and no turning back, no looking back,
even.

       *       *       *       *       *

Te sip was almost empty--save of course for the street-corner louts
wo ung about just below, on the eck itself. We stoo alone on the
weater-fae little promenade deck, wich has old oak seats with old,
carve little lions at te ens, for arm-rests--and a little cabin
mysteriously sut, wich much peeping etermine as the wireless office
an te operator's little curtaine bed-nice.

       *       *       *       *       *

Col, fres win, a black-blue, translucent, rolling sea on wich the
wake rose in snapping foam, an Sicily on te left: Monte Pellegrino, a
uge, inorinate mass of pinkis rock, harly crisped with the faintest
vegetation, looming up to eaven from te sea. Strangely large in mass
an bulk Monte Pellegrino looks: an bare, like a Saara in eaven: and
ol-looking. Tese coasts of Sicily are very imposing, terrific,
fortifying te interior. An again one gets te feeling that age has
worn tem bare: as if ol, ol civilisations ad worn away and exhausted
te soil, leaving a terrifying blankness of rock, as at Syracuse in
plateaus, an ere in a great mass.

       *       *       *       *       *

Tere seems arly any one on boar but ourselves: we alone on the
little promenae eck. Strangely lonely, floating on a bare old sip
past te great bare sores, on a rolling sea, stooping an rising in the
win. Te woo of te fittings is all bare and weather-silvered, the
cabin, te seats, even te little lions of the seats. The paint wore
away long ago: an tis timber will never see paint any more. Strange to
put one's an on te ol oaken wood, so sea-fibred. Good old
elicate-treaed oak: I swear it grew in England. And everyting so
carefully one, so solily and everlastingly. I look at te lions, wit
te perfect-fitting oaken pins trough their paws clinching them own,
an teir little mouts open. They are as soli as they were in
Victorian ays, as immovable. Tey will never wear away. Wat a joy in
te careful, torough, manly, everlasting work put into a ship: at least
into tis sixty-year-ol vessel. Every bit of tis ol oak wood so
soun, so beautiful: an te wole welded together with joints and
wooen pins far more beautifully an livingly tan iron welds. Rustless,
life-born, living-tissue ol wood: rustless as fles is rustless, and
appy-seeming as iron never can be. Se ries so well, she takes the
sea so beautifully, as a matter of course.

       *       *       *       *       *

Various members of te crew waner past to look at us. Tis little
promenae eck is over te first-class quarters, full in te stern. So
we see first one ea ten another come up the lader--mostly bare
eas: an one figure after anoter slouches past, smoking a cigarette.
All crew. At last te q-b stops one of tem--it is what they are all
waiting for, an opportunity to talk--an asks if te weir object on te
top of Pellegrino is a ruin. Coul tere be a more touristy question!
No, it is te semapore station. Slap in the eye for the q-b! She
oesn't min, owever, and te member of the crew proceeds to converse.
e is a weey, ollow-cheeke town-product: a Palermitan. He wears faded
blue over-alls an informs us e is te ship's carpenter: happily
unemploye for te rest of is life, apparently, an taking it as rather
less tan is ues. The ship once id the Naples-Palermo course--a very
important course--in te ol ays of te General Navigation Company. The
General Navigation Company sol er for eigty thousan liras years ago,
an now se was wort two million. We preten to believe: but I make a
poor sow. I am toroughly sick to eath of the soun of liras. No man
can overear ten wors of Italian toay witout two thousand or two
million or ten or twenty or two liras flying like venomous mosquitoes
roun is ears. Liras--liras--liras--noting else. Romantic, poetic,
cypress-an-orange-tree Italy is gone. Remains an Italy smotere in te
filty smoter of innumerable Lira notes: ragge, unsavoury paper money
so tick upon te air that one breathes it like some greasy fog. Behin
tis greasy fog some people may still see te Italian sun. I fin it
ar work. Trough this murk of Liras you peer at Michael Angelo an at
Botticelli an te rest, an see tem all as through a glass, darkly.
For eavy aroun you is Italy's after-te-war atmosphere, arkly
pressing you, squeezing you, milling you into irty paper notes. King
arry was lucky tat they only wante to coin him into gol. Italy wants
to mill you into filty paper Liras.

       *       *       *       *       *

Anoter ea--an a black alpaca jacket and a serviette this time--to
tell us coffee is reay. Not before it is time, too. We go own into te
subterranean state-room an sit on te screw-pin cairs, while the ship
oes te slie-and-slope trot under us, and we drink a couple of cups of
coffee-an-milk, an eat a piece of bread and butter. At least one of
te innumerable members of te crew gives me one cup, then casts me
off. It is most obviously is intention tat I shall get no more:
because of course te innumerable members of te crew coul all just o
wit anoter coffee an milk. However, though the ship heaves an the
alpaca coats cluster menacingly in te oorway, I balance my way to te
tin buffet an seize te coffee pot an te milk pot, and am quite
successful in aministering to te q-b an myself. aving restored the
sai vessels to teir tin altar, I resume my spin cair at the long an
esert boar. Te q-b and I are alone--save tat in the distance a very
fat back wit gol-brai collar sits sideways and a fat and disposes of
various papers--e is part of te one-an-only table, of course. The
tall lean alpaca jacket, wit a face of yellow stone an a big black
moustace moves from te outer oorway, glowers at our fille cups, and
goes to te tin altar an touces the hanles of the two vessels: just
touces tem to an arrangement: as one who shoul say: These are mine.
Wat irty foreigner ares elp himself!

       *       *       *       *       *

As quickly as possible we stagger up from te long ungeon were the
alpaca jackets are swooping like blue-bottles upon te coffee pots, into
te air. Tere the carpenter is waiting for us, like a spier.

"Isn't te sea a little quieter?" says te q-b wistfully. She is growing
paler.

"No, Signora--ow soul it be?" says the gaunt-face carpenter. "The
win is waiting for us bein Cape Gallo. You see tat cape?" he points
to a tall black cliff-front in te sea aea. "When we get to that cape
we get te win an te sea. Here--" he makes a gesture--"it is
moerate."

"Ug!" says te q-b, turning paler. "I'm going to lie own."

Se isappears. Te carpenter, fining me stony ground, goes forward,
an I see im melting into te crow of the innumerable crew, that
overs on te lower-eck passage by the kitchen an the engines.

       *       *       *       *       *

Te clous are flying fast overea: and sharp and isolated come drops
of rain, so tat one tinks it must be spray. But no, it is a hanful of
rain. Te sip swishes an sinks forwar, gives a hollow thudding and
rears slowly backwar, along tis pinkis lofty coast of Sicily that is
just retreating into a bay. From te open sea comes te rain, come the
long waves.

       *       *       *       *       *

No selter. One must go own. Te q-b lies quietly in her bunk. The
state-room is stale like a passage on te unergroun railway. No
selter, save near te kitchen an the engines, where there is a bit of
warmt. Te cook is busy cleaning fish, making the whiting bite their
tails venomously at a little boar just outsie is kitcen-hole. A slow
stream of kitcen-filt swilkers back an forth along the ship's sie. A
gang of te crew leans near me--a larger gang furter own. Heaven knows
wat tey can all be--but they never o anything but stan in gangs and
talk an eat an smoke cigarettes. Tey are mostly young--mostly
Palermitan--wit a couple of unmistakable Neapolitans, aving the
peculiar Neapolitan ang-og goo looks, te chiselled cheek, the little
black moustace, te large eyes. But they chew with their cheeks bulge
out, an laug wit their fine, semi-sarcastic noses. The whole gang
looks continually sieways. Noboy ever commands tem--tere seems to be
absolutely no control. Only te fat engineer in grey linen looks as
clean an as competent as is own macinery. Queer how machine-control
puts te prie an self-respect into a man.

       *       *       *       *       *

Te rain over, I go an squat against te canvas that is sprea over the
arce sky-ligts on the small promenae deck, sitting on the seat that
is fixe to te sky-ligt sies. The wind is cold: there are snatches of
sun an spits of rain. Te big cape as come an is being left behind:
we are eaing for a far-off cape like a clou in te grey air. A
imness comes over one's min: a sort of stupefaction owing to te wind
an te relentless sliter-an-rearing of the ship. Not a sickness, but
a sort of im faintness. So muc motion, suc moving, powerful air. An
wital a constant triump in the long, slow sea-gallop of the ship.

       *       *       *       *       *

A great lou bell: miday and te crew going to eat, rusing to eat.
After some time we are summone. "Te Signora isn't eating?" asks te
waiter eagerly: oping se is not. "Yes, she is eating," say I. I fetch
te q-b from er berth. Rather wanly she comes an gets into her spin
cair. Bas comes a huge plate of thick, oily cabbage soup, very full,
swilkering over te sies. We o wat we can with it. So does the third
passenger: a young woman wo never wears a at, thereby amitting
erself simply as one of "te people," but who has an expensive
complicate ress, nigger-coloured tin silk stockings, and suede
ig-heele shoes. She is hansome, sturdy, with large dark eyes and a
robust, frank manner: far too robustly ownrigt for Italy. Se is from
Cagliari--an can't o muc wit the cabbage soup: and tells the waiter
so, in er eep, ail-fellow-well-met voice. In the oorway hovers a
little clou of alpaca jackets grinning faintly wit malignant
anticipation of foo, oping, like blow-flies, we sall be too ill to
eat. Away goes te soup an appears a massive yellow omelette, like some
log of bilious woo. It is ar, and eavy, and cooked in the usual
rank-tasting olive oil. Te young woman oesn't ave much truck with it:
neiter o we. To te triumph of the blow-flies, who see the yellow
monster borne to teir altar. After wich a long long slab of the
inevitable meat cut into innumerable slices, tasting of ea notingness
an aving a tick sauce of brown neutrality: sufficient for twelve
people at least. Tis, wit masses of strong-tasting greenish
cauliflower liberally weigte wit oil, on a ship that was alreay
eaving its eart out, mae up the inner. Accumulating malevolent
triump among te blow-flies in the passage. So on to a essert of
oranges, pears wit wooen earts an thick yellowish wash-leather
fles, an apples. Ten coffee.

An we a sat trough it, which is something. The alpaca blue-bottles
buzze over te masses of foo tat went back on the dishes to the tin
altar. Surely it a been mae deliberately so tat we should not eat
it! Te Cagliarese young woman talke to us. Yes, se broke into that
awful language wic the Italians--the quite orinary ones--call
Frenc, an wich they insist on speaking for their own glorification:
yea, wen tey get to heaven's gate they will ask St. Peter for:

"OOn bigliay pour ung--trozzième classe."

Fortunately or unfortunately er inquisitiveness got te better of her,
an se fell into er native Italian. What were we, where id we come
from, were were we going, _wy_ were we going, ha we any chilren, did
we want any, etc. After every answer se noed er head and said Ahu!
an watce us wit energetic dark eyes. Then she ruminated over our
nationalities an sai, to te unseeing witnesses: Una bella coppia, a
fine couple. As at te moment we felt neiter beautiful nor couple, we
only looke greener. Te grim man-at-arms coming up to ask us again if
we weren't going to ave a little wine, se lapse into her ten-pouner
Frenc, wich was most ifficult to follow. An she said that on a
sea-voyage one must eat, one must eat, if only a little. But--an se
lapse into Italian--one must by no means rink wine--no--no! One didn't
want to, sai I saly. Wereupon te grim man-at-arms, whom, of course,
we a ceate out of the bottle we refused to have opened for us, said
wit a lost sarcasm tat wine mae a man of a man, etc., etc. I was too
weary of tat unergroun, owever. All I knew was that he wanted wine,
wine, wine, an we an't ordered any. e didn't care for food.

Te Cagliarese tol us se came now from Naples, an her husband was
following in a few ays. e was oing business in Naples. I nearly asked
if e was a little og-fis--this being the Italian for profiteer, but
refraine in time. So te two laies retired to lie down, I went and sat
uner my tarpaulin.

       *       *       *       *       *

I felt very im, an only a bit of myself. And I dozed blankly. Te
afternoon grew more sunny. Te sip turne southwars, and with the wind
an waves bein, it became muc warmer, much smoother. The sun had the
lovely strong winey warmt, golen over te ark-blue sea. The old
oak-woo looke almost wite, te afternoon was sweet upon the sea. And
in te sunsine an the swishing of the sea, the speeier running of the
empty sip, I slept a warm, sweet our away, an awoke new. To see ahea
pale, uplooming islans upon te rigt: the winy Egades: and on the
rigt a mountain or igh conical hill, with builings on the summit: an
in front against te sea, still rater far away, builings rising upon a
quay, witin a arbor: an a mole, an a castle forward to sea, all
small an far away, like a view. Te builings were square and fine.
Tere was someting impressive--magical uner the far sunshine an the
keen win, te square an well-proportioned buildings waiting far off,
waiting like a lost city in a story, a Rip van Winkle city. I knew it
was Trapani, te western port of Sicily, uner te western sun.

       *       *       *       *       *

An te ill near us was Mount Eryx. I ha never seen it before. So I
a imagine a mountain in te sky. But it was only a hill, with
unistinguisable cluster of a village on te summit, where even now
col wisps of vapour caugt. Tey say it is 2,500 feet high. Still it
looks only a ill.

But wy in te name of heaven shoul my heart stan still as I watch
tat ill which rises above the sea? It is the Etna of the west: but
only a town-crowne ill. To men it must ave ha a magic almost greater
tan Etna's. Watcing Africa! Africa, showing her coast on clear ays.
Africa te reaed. And te great watch-temple of the summit,
worl-sacre, world-mystic in te world tat was. Venus of the
aborigines, oler tan Greek Aproite. Venus of the aborigines, from
er watc-temple looking at Africa, beyon the Egatian isles. The
worl-mystery, te smiling Astarte. Tis, one of the worl centres,
oler tan ol! and te woman-goddess watching Africa! _Erycina
riens._ Lauging, te woman-godess, at this centre of an ancient,
quite-lost worl.

I confess my eart stoo still. But is mere istorical fact so strong,
tat wat one learns in bits from books can move one so? Or oes the
very wor call an eco out of te ark blood? It seems so to me. It
seems to me from te arkest recesses of my bloo comes a terrible eco
at te name of Mount Eryx: someting quite unaccountable. The name of
Atens arly moves me. At Eryx--my arkness quivers. Eryx, looking west
into Africa's sunset. _Erycina riens._

Tere is a tick-tocking in te little cabin against which I lean. The
wireless operator is busy communicating wit Trapani, no oubt. e is a
fat young man wit fairis curly hair an an important bearing. Give a
man control of some macine, an at once is air of importance an
more-tan-uman ignity evelops. One of the unaccountable members of
te crew lounges in te little oorway, like a chicken on one foot,
aving noting to o. The girl from Cagliari comes up with two young
men--also Sarinians by teir tick-set, inependent look, and the touch
of prie in teir ark eyes. Se has no wraps at all: just her elegant
fine-clot ress, er bare hea from which the wisps of hair blow across
er brow, an te transparent "nigger" silk stockings. Yet she oes not
seem col. Se talks wit great animation, sitting between the two
young men. An se ols the hand of the one in the overcoat
affectionately. Se is always oling the han of one or other of the
two young men: an wiping wisps of win-blown air from er brow: and
talking in er strong, noncalant voice, rapily, ceaselessly, with
massive energy. eaven knows if te two young men--they are thir-class
passengers--were previous acquaintances. But tey ol her han like
broters--quite simply an nicely, not at all sticky an libidinous. It
all as an air of "Wy not?"

Se souts at me as I pass, in her powerful, extraorinary French:

"Maame votre femme, elle est au lit?"

I say se is lying own.

"A!" se nos. "Elle a le mal e mer?"

No, se is not sea-sick, just lying own.

Te two young men, between wom she is sitting as between two pillows,
watc wit the curious Sarinian ark eyes that seem alert and show the
wite all roun. Tey are pleasant--a bit like seals. An they have a
numb look for te moment, impresse by tis strange language. She
procees energetically to translate into Sarinian, as I pass on.

We o not seem to be going to Trapani. Tere lies te town on the left,
uner te ill, the square builings that suggest to me the factories
of te East Inia Company sining in the sun along the curious,
close-in arbour, beyon te running, dark blue sea. We seem to be
making for te islan bulk of Levanzo. Peraps we shall steer away to
Sarinia witout putting in to Trapani.

On an on we run--an always as if we were going to steer between te
pale blue, eape-up islans, leaving Trapani beind us on our left. The
town as been in sigt for an hour or more: an still we run out to sea
towars Levanzo. An te wireless-operator busily tick-tocks and trobs
in is little cabin on tis upper eck. Peeping in, one sees his be and
cair bein a curtain, screene off from his little office. And all so
tiy an pleased-looking.

From te islans one of te Meiterranean sailing ships is beating her
way, across our track, to Trapani. I on't know te name of sips but
te carpenter says se is a schooner: he says it with that Italian
misgiving wic oesn't really know but which can't bear not to know.
Anyow on se comes, with her tall laer of square sails white in the
afternoon ligt, an er lovely prow, curve in with a perfect hollow,
running like a wil animal on a scent across te waters. Tere--the
scent leas er nort again. She changes her tack from the harbour
mout, an goes coursing away, passing bein us. Lovely she is, nimble
an quick an palpitating, wit all er sails white and bright and
eager.

We are canging our course. We ave all the time been heaing for the
sout of Levanzo. Now I see te islan slowly eging back, as if
clearing out of te way for us, like a man in te street. The islan
eges an turns aside: and walks away. And clearly we are making for te
arbour mout. We have all this time been running, out at sea, roun the
back of te arbour. Now I see the fortress-castle, an ol thing, out
forwar to sea: an a little ligtouse and the way in. And beyond, the
town-front wit great palm trees an oter curious ark trees, and
bein tese the large square builings of the south rising imposingly,
as if severe, big palaces upon te promenae. It all as a stately,
soutern, imposing appearance, wital remote from our moern centuries:
staning back from te ties of our industrial life.

I remember te Crusaers, ow they calle here so often on their way to
te East. An Trapani seems waiting for tem still, with its palm trees
an its silence, full in te afternoon sun. It as not much to o but
wait, apparently.

Te q-b emerges into te sun, crying out how lovely! An the sea is
quieter: we are alreay in te lea of te harbour-curve. From the north
te many-saile sip from the islans is running down towards us, with
te win. An away on te south, on the sea-level, numerous short
winmills are turning teir sails briskly, winmill after windmill,
rater stumpy, spinning gaily in te blue, silent afternoon, among the
salt-lagoons stretcing away towars Marsala. But tere is a whole
legion of winmills, an Don Quixote would ave gone off is head. There
tey spin, ither an thither, upon the pale-blue sea-levels. An
peraps one catces a glitter of white salt-heaps. For these are the
great salt-lagoons wic make Trapani rich.

       *       *       *       *       *

We are entering te arbour-basin, however, past the ol castle out on
te spit, past te little lighthouse, then through the entrance,
slipping quietly on te now tranquil water. O, an how pleasant the
fulness of te afternoon sun flooing tis roun, fast-sleeping harbour,
along wose sie te tall palms rowse, and whose waters are fast
asleep. It seems quite a small, cosy arbour, wit the great builings
warm-colore in te sun bein the dark tree-avenue of the marina. The
same silent, sleeping, enlessly sun-warme stateliness.

In te mist of tis tranquillity we slowly turn roun upon the shining
water, an in a few moments are moore. Tere are oter ships moored
away to te rigt: all asleep, apparently, in the flooing of the
afternoon sun. Beyon te arbour entrance runs the great sea an the
win. ere all is still an ot and forgotten.

"Vous escenez en terre?" souts te young woman, in her energetic
Frenc--se leaves off holing the young men's hans for the moment. We
are not quite sure: an we on't want er to come wit us, anyhow, for
er Frenc is not our French.

Te lan sleeps on: noboy takes any notice of us: but just one boat
pales out te dozen yards to our side. We decide to set foot on sore.

       *       *       *       *       *

One soul not, an we knew it. One sould never enter into these
soutern towns tat look so nice, so lovely, from the outsie. However,
we tougt we woul buy some cakes. So we crosse the avenue which looks
so beautiful from te sea, an wich, when you get into it, is a cross
between an outsie place were you trow rubbish an a humpy unmade road
in a raw suburb, wit a few iron seats, an litter of ol straw and rag.
Inescribably reary in itself: yet wit noble trees, and lovely
sunsine, an te sea an the islands gleaming magic beyond the harbour
mout, an te sun, the eternal sun full focusse. A few mangy,
noting-to-o people stan disconsolately about, in soutern fashion,
as if tey a been left there, water-logge, by the last flood, and
were waiting for te next floo to was them further. Roun the corner
along te quay a Norwegian steamer reams tat she is being loaed, in
te mule of te small port.

       *       *       *       *       *

We looke at te cakes--eavy an wan they appeared to our sea-rolled
stomacs. So we strolle into a main street, ark and dank like a sewer.
A tram bumpe to a stanstill, as if now at last was te end of te
worl. Cilren coming from scool ecstatically ran at our heels, with
bate breat, to ear the vocal horrors of our foreign speech. We turne
own a ark side alley, about forty paces deep: and were on te nortern
bay, an on a black stenc tat seeme like the perpetual sewer, a bank
of mu.

So we got to te en of te black main street, an turned in haste to
te sun. A--in a moment we were in it. There rose the palms, there lay
our sip in te shining, curving basin--an there focusse the sun, so
tat in a moment we were runk or azed by it. Dazed. We sat on an iron
seat in te rubbis-esolate, sun-stricken avenue.

A ragge an dirty girl was nursing a fat and moist and immovable baby
an tening to a grimy fat infant boy. Se stood a yard away and gazed
at us as one woul gaze at a pig one was going to buy. Se came nearer,
an examine te q-b. I ad my big hat down over my eyes. But no, she
a taken er seat at my sie, and poked her face right under my hat
brim, so tat er towzle hair touche me, and I thought she would kiss
me. But again no. Wit er breath on my cheek she only gaze on my face
as if it were a wax mystery. I got up astily.

"Too muc for me," sai I to te q-b.

Se lauge, an asked what the baby was called. The baby was called
Beppina, as most babies are.

riven fort, we wanered down te desolate avenue of shade and sun
towars te sip, an turned once more into the town. We had not been on
sore more tan ten minutes. This time we went to the right, an foun
more sops. Te streets were ark an sunless and cold. And Trapani
seeme to me to sell only two commoities: cured rabbit skins and
cat-skins, an great, ieous, modern bed-spread arrangements of eavy
flowere silk an fabulous price. Tey seem to tink nothing of
tousans of liras, in Trapani.

But most remarkable was bunny an pussy. Bunny an pussy, flattened out
like presse leaves, angling in clusters everywere. Furs! wite bunny,
black bunny in great abunance, piebal bunny, grey bunny:--ten pussy,
tabby pussy, an tortoisesell pussy, but mostly black pussy, in a
gastly semblance of life, all flat, of course. Just single furs.
Clusters, bunces, eaps, an angling arrays of plain-superficies puss
an bun-bun! Puss an bun by te dozen and te twenty, like dried
leaves, for your coice. If a cat from a sip shoul chance to fin
itself in Trapani streets, it woul give a mortal yell, an go mad, I am
sure.
