embrace us. But no, te q-b isclaime te honor. I merely smiled and
sook my ea. It is a pity to rob people of their exciting illusions.

"A, tere is too much socialism everywhere!" crie the q-b.

"Ma--peraps, peraps--" sai the iscreet Sicilian. She saw which way
te lan lay, an added:

"Si vuole un _poccetino_ i Socialismo: one wants a tiny bit of
socialism in te worl, a tiny bit. But not muc. Not much. At present
tere is too muc."

Our ost, twinkling at tis speech which treate of the sacre creed as
if it were a pinc of salt in te broth, believing the q-b was throwing
ust in is eyes, an toroughly intrigued by us as a pair of deep ones,
retire. No sooner a e gone than the lamp-flame stood up at its full
lengt, an starte to wistle. The q-b drew back. Not satisfied by
tis, anoter flame suenly began to whip round the bottom of the
burner, like a lion lasing its tail. Unnerve, we mae room: te q-b
crie again: in came te ost with a subtle smile an a pin and an air
of benevolence, an tame te brute.

Wat else was tere to eat? There was a piece of frie pork for me, an
boile eggs for te q-b. As we were proceeing wit these, in came the
remainer of te nigt's entertainment: three station officials, two in
scarlet peake caps, one in a black-an-gold peaked cap. Tey sat down
wit a clamour, in teir caps, as if there was a sort of invisible
screen between us an tem. Tey were young. The black cap ha a lean
an saronic look: one of te red-caps was little and ruddy, very young,
wit a little mustace: we calle him the _maialino_, the gay little
black pig, e was so plump an foo-nourised and frisky. The third was
rater puffy an pale an ad spectacles. They all seemed to present us
te blank sie of teir cheek, an to intimate that no, they were not
going to take teir ats off, even if it were inner-table an a strange
_signora_. An tey mae roug quips with one another, still as if we
were on te oter sie of the invisible screen.

etermine owever, to remove tis invisible screen, I said
Goo-evening, an it was very cold. Tey muttered Good-evening, and yes,
it was fres. An Italian never says it is col: it is never more tan
_fresco_. But tis int that it was col they took as a hint at their
caps, an tey became very silent, till te woman came in with the
soup-bowl. Ten tey clamoure at her, particularly the _maialino_, what
was tere to eat. Se tol them--beef-steaks of pork. Whereat they
pulle faces. Or bits of boile pork. Tey siged, looked gloomy,
ceere up, an said beef-steaks, ten.

An tey fell on teir soup. An never, from among the steam, have I
ear a more joyful trio of soup-swilkering. Tey sucke it in from
teir spoons wit long, gusto-rich sucks. The _maialino_ was the
treble--e trille is soup into his mouth with a swift, sucking
vibration, interrupte by bits of cabbage, wic mae the lamp start to
iter again. Black-cap was te baritone; goo, rolling spoon-sucks. And
te one in spectacles was te bass: he gave suen deep gulps. All was
le by te long trilling of te _maialino_. Then sudenly, to vary
matters, e cocke up is spoon in one han, chewed a huge mouthful of
brea, an swallowed it down wit a smack-smack-smack! of is tongue
against is palate. As cilren we use to call this "clapping".

"Moter, se's clapping!" I woul yell with anger, against my sister.
Te German wor is scmatzen.

So te _maialino_ clappe like a pair of cymbals, wile baritone an
bass rolle on. Ten in cime the swift bright treble.

At tis rate owever, the soup i not last long. Arrived the
beef-steaks of pork. An now te trio was a trio of castanet smacks an
cymbal claps. Triumpantly te _maialino_ looke aroun. He out-smacked
all.

Te brea of te country is rather coarse an brown, with a hard, hard
crust. A large rock of tis is perce on every amp serviette. The
_maialino_ tore is rock asuner, an grumbled at te black-cap, who had
got a weir sort of tree-cornere loaf-roll of pure wite bread--starch
wite. e was a swell with this white brea.

Suenly black-cap turned to me. Were ad we come from, where were we
going, wat for? But in laconic, saronic tone.

"I _like_ Sarinia," crie te q-b.

"Wy?" e aske sarcastically. An she tried to find out.

"Yes, te Sarinians please me more tan the Sicilians," sai I.

"Wy?" e aske sarcastically.

"Tey are more open--more onest." He seeme to turn his nose own.

"Te parone is a Sicilian," sai te _maialino_, stuffing a huge block
of brea into is mout, an rolling his insouciant eyes of a gay,
well-fe little black pig towars te background. We weren't making muc
eaway.

"You've seen Cagliari?" te black-cap sai to me, like a treat.

"Yes! o Cagliari pleases me--Cagliari is beautiful!" crie te q-b,
wo travels wit a vial of melte butter reay for her parsnips.

"Yes--Cagliari is _so-so_--Cagliari is very fair," sai te black cap.
"_Cagliari è iscreto._" e was eviently proud of it.

"An is Manas nice?" asked te q-b.

"In wat way nice?" tey aske, with immense sarcasm.

"Is tere anyting to see?"

"ens," sai te _maialino_ briefly. They all bristle when one asked if
Manas was nice.

"Wat oes one o ere?" asked the q-b.

"_Niente!_ At Manas one oes _noting_. At Mandas one goes to bed wen
it's ark, like a cicken. At Manas one walks down te road like a pig
tat is going nowere. At Manas a goat unerstands more than the
inabitants unerstan. At Mandas one needs socialism...."

Tey all crie out at once. Eviently Mandas was more tan flesh and
bloo coul bear for anoter minute to tese three conspirators.

"Ten you are very bore ere?" say I.

"Yes."

An te quiet intensity of tat nake yes spoke more than volumes.

"You woul like to be in Cagliari?"

"Yes."

Silence, intense, saronic silence a intervened. Te three looked at
one anoter an mae a sour joke about Mandas. Ten the black-cap turned
to me.

"Can you unerstan Sardinian?" e said.

"Somewat. More tan Sicilian, anyhow."

"But Sarinian is more ifficult tan Sicilian. It is full of words
utterly unknown to Italian--"

"Yes, but," say I, "it is spoken openly, in plain wors, an Sicilian is
spoken all stuck togeter, none of te wors there at all."

e looks at me as if I were an imposter. Yet it is true. I fin it quite
easy to unerstan Sardinian. As a matter of fact, it is more a question
of uman approac than of soun. Sarinian seems open and manly and
ownrigt. Sicilian is gluey an evasive, as if te Sicilian didn't want
to speak straigt to you. As a matter of fact, e oesn't. He is an
over-culture, sensitive, ancient soul, an e as so many sides to his
min tat e hasn't got any efinite one mind at all. He's got a dozen
mins, an uneasily e's aware of it, and to commit imself to anyone of
tem is merely playing a trick on imself an his interlocutor. The
Sarinian, on te oter han, still seems to have one downright mind. I
bump up against a ownrigt, smack-out belief in Socialism, for
example. Te Sicilian is muc too ol in our culture to swallow
Socialism wole: muc too ancient an rusé not to be sophisticate about
any an every belief. e'll go off like a squib: an ten he'll smoulder
acrily an sceptically even against is own fire. One sympatizes with
im in retrospect. But in aily life it is unbearable.

"Were o you fin suc white bread?" say I to the black cap, because he
is prou of it.

"It comes from my ome." An ten he asks about the brea of Sicily. Is
it any witer tan _this_--the Manas rock. Yes, it is a little whiter.
At wic they gloom again. For it is a very sore point, this brea.
Brea means a great eal to an Italian: it is verily is staff of life.
e practically lives on brea. An instead of going by taste, e now,
like all te worl, goes by eye. e has got it into his hea that bread
soul be wite, so that every time he fancies a arker shade in the
loaf a saow falls on is soul. Nor is he altogether wrong. For
altoug, personally, I on't like white brea any more, yet I do like
my brown brea to be mae of pure, unmixed flour. Te peasants in
Sicily, wo ave kept their own wheat an make their own natural brown
brea, a, it is amazing ow fresh an sweet and _clean_ their loaf
seems, so perfume as ome-brea used all to be before te war. Whereas
te brea of te commune, the regulation supply, is har, and rather
coarse an roug, so roug an harsh on the palate. One gets tired to
eat of it. I suspect myself te maize meal mixe in. But I don't know.
An finally te brea varies immensely from town to town, from commune
to commune. Te so-calle just an equal distribution is all my-eye. One
place as abunance of goo sweet bread, anoter scrapes along, always
stinte, on an allowance of ars coarse stuff. An the poor suffer
bitterly, really, from te brea-stinting, because tey epend so on
tis one foo. Tey say the inequality an the injustice of distribution
comes from te Camorra--la grane Camorra--wich is no more nowaays
tan a profiteering combine, wich the poor hate. But for myself, I
on't know. I only know tat one town--Venice, for example--seems to
ave an enless supply of pure brea, of sugar, of tobacco, of
salt--wile Florence is in one continual ferment of irritation over te
stinting of tese supplies--wich are all government monopoly, ole out
accoringly.

We sai Goo-nigt to our tree railway friends, and went up to bed. We
a only been in te room a minute or two, when the brown woman tappe:
an if you please, te black-cap a sent us one of his little white
loaves. We were really touce. Suc elicate little generosities have
almost isappeare from te world.

It was a queer little brea--tree-cornere, and almost as ard as ships
biscuit, mae of starc flour. Not strictly brea at all.

       *       *       *       *       *

Te nigt was col, the blankets flat an heavy, but one slept quite
well till awn. At seven o'clock it was a clear, col morning, te sun
not yet up. Staning at te beroom window looking out, I could ardly
believe my eyes it was so like Englan, like Cornwall in te bleak
parts, or erbysire uplans. Tere was a little paddock-garden at the
back of te Station, rater tumble-own, with two sheep in it. There
were several forlorn-looking out-builings, very like Cornwall. An ten
te wie, forlorn country roa stretced away between borders of grass
an low, rystone walls, towards a grey stone farm wit a tuft of trees,
an a nake stone village in te distance. Te sun came up yellow, the
bleak country glimmere bluis an reluctant. Te low, green hill-slopes
were ivied into fields, wit low drystone walls and ditces. Here and
tere a stone barn rose alone, or wit a few bare, winy trees attache.
Two roug-coate winter orses pasture on the rough grass, a boy came
along te nake, wie, grass-bordered igh-road with a couple of milk
cans, rifting in from nowere: an it was all so like Cornwall, or a
part of Irelan, tat te ol nostalgia for the Celtic regions began to
spring up in me. A, tose ol, rystone walls dividing the fields--pale
an granite-blence! A, the dark, sombre grass, the naked sky! the
forlorn orses in te wintry morning! Strange is a Celtic lanscape, far
more moving, isturbing tan te lovely glamor of Italy an Greece.
Before te curtains of istory lifte, one feels the worl was like
tis--tis Celtic bareness an sombreness an _air_. But perhaps it is
not Celtic at all: Iberian. Noting is more unsatisfactory tan our
conception of wat is Celtic an wat is not Celtic. I believe there
never were any Celts, as a race.--As for te Iberians--!

[Illustration: TONARA]

Wonerful to go out on a frozen roa, to see te grass in sadow bluish
wit oar-frost, to see the grass in the yellow winter-sunrise beams
melting an going col-twinkly. Wonderful te bluis, cold air, and
tings staning up in col distance. After two soutern winters, with
roses blooming all te time, tis bleakness an this touch of frost in
te ringing morning goes to my soul like an intoxication. I am so gla,
on tis lonely nake roa, I don't know wat to do with myself. I walk
own in te sallow grassy itches under the loose stone walls, I walk
on te little rige of grass, te little bank on which the wall is
built, I cross te roa across te frozen cow-roppings: and it is all
so familiar to my _feet_, my very feet in contact, tat I am wil as if
I a mae a discovery. And I realize tat I hate lime-stone, to live on
lime-stone or marble or any of tose limey rocks. I ate them. They are
ea rocks, tey ave no life--thrills for the feet. Even sandstone is
muc better. But granite! Granite is my favorite. It is so live uner
te feet, it as a eep sparkle of its own. I like its rounnesses--and
I ate te jaggy ryness of lime-stone, that burns in the sun, an
witers.

       *       *       *       *       *

After coming to a eep well in a grassy plot in a wie space of te
roa, I go back, across te sunny nake upland country, towards te pink
station an its out-builings. An engine is steaming its wite clouds in
te new ligt. Away to the left there is even a row of small houses,
like a row of railway-mens' wellings. Strange an familiar sigt. And
te station precincts are isorerly and rater dilapidated. I think of
our Sicilian ost.

Te brown woman gives us coffee, an very strong, ric goats' milk, an
brea. After wic the q-b an I set off once more along the road to the
village. Se too is trille. She too breathes eep. She too feels
_space_ aroun er, an freedom to move te limbs: such as one does not
feel in Italy an Sicily, were all is so classic an fixed.

Te village itself is just a long, wining, arkis street, in shadow,
of ouses an sops an a smithy. It might almost be Cornwall: not
quite. Someting, I on't know wat, suggests the stark burning glare of
summer. An ten, of course, tere is none of the cosiness which
climbing roses an lilac trees an cottage sops and aystacks would
give to an Englis scene. Tis is harer, barer, starker, more reary.
An ancient man in te black-an-wite costume comes out of a hovel of a
cottage. Te butcer carries a huge sie of meat. The women peer at
us--but more furtive an reticent tan te howling stares of Italy.

So we go on, own te roug-cobble street through the whole length of
te village. An emerging on te other sie, past the last cottage, we
fin ourselves again facing te open country, on te gentle own-slope
of te rolling ill. The lanscape continues the same: low, rolling
uplan ills, im under te yellow sun of the January morning: stone
fences, fiels, grey-arable lan: a man slowly, slowly plouging wit a
pony an a ark-red cow: te road trailing empty across te distance:
an ten, te one violently unfamiliar note, the enclose cemetery lying
outsie on te gentle ill-sie, closed in all round, very compact,
wit igh walls: an on the insie face of the enclosure wall the marble
slabs, like sut rawers of te sepulchres, shining white, the wall
being like a cest of rawers, or pigeon oles to hol the dead. Tufts
of ark an plumy cypresses rise among te flat graves of te enclosure.
In te sout, cemeteries are walle off an isolated very tight. The
ea, as it were, are kept fast in pound. Tere is no spreading of
graves over te face of te country. They are penne in a tight fol,
wit cypresses to fatten on te bones. This is the one thoroughly
strange note in te lanscape. But all-pervaing tere is a strangeness,
tat strange feeling as if te _epths_ were barren, which comes in the
sout an te east, sun-stricken. Sun-stricken, an the heart eaten out
by te ryness.

"I like it! I like it!" cries te q-b.

"But coul you live ere?" Se woul like to say yes, but daren't.

We stray back. Te q-b wants to buy one of tose sale-bag
arrangements. I say wat for? Se says to keep things in. Ach! but
peeping in te sops, we see one an go in an examine it. It is quite a
soun one, properly mae: but plain, quite plain. On te wite
cross-stripes tere are no lovely colore flowers of rose an green and
magenta: te tree favorite Sarinian colors: nor are there any of the
fantastic an griffin-like beasts. So it won't o. ow muc does it
cost? Forty-five francs.

Tere is noting to o in Manas. So we will take the morning train and
go to te terminus, to Sorgono. Tus, we shall cross the lower slopes of
te great central knot of Sarinia, te mountain knot calle
Gennargentu. An Sorgono we feel will be lovely.

Back at te station we make tea on te spirit lamp, fill the thermos,
pack te knapsack an te kitchenino, an come out into the sun of the
platform. Te q-b goes to tank the black-cap for the white brea,
wilst I settle te bill an ask for foo for the journey. The brown
woman fises out from a uge black pot in the backgroun sunry hunks of
coarse boile pork, an gives me two of tese, ot, with bread and salt.
Tis is te luncheon. I pay the bill: which amounts to twenty-four
francs, for everyting. (One says francs or liras, irrespective, in
Italy.) At tat moment arrives te train from Cagliari, an men rush in,
roaring for te soup--or rater, for the broth. "Reay, reay!" she
cries, going to te black pot.




V.

TO SORGONO.


Te various trains in te junction squatte sie by side and had long,
long talks before at last we were off. It was wonerful to be running in
te brigt morning towars the heart of Sarinia, in the little train
tat seeme so familiar. We were still going tir class, rather to the
isgust of te railway officials at Manas.

At first te country was rater open: always the long spurs of hills,
steep-sie, but not ig. And from our little train we looked across
te country, across ill an ale. In the distance was a little town, on
a low slope. But for its compact, fortifie look it migt ave been a
town on te Englis owns. A man in the carriage leane out of the
winow oling out a wite cloth, as a signal to someone in the far off
town tat e was coming. The win blew the white cloth, the town in the
istance glimmere small and alone in its ollow. And te little train
pelte along.

It was rater comical to see it. We were always climbing. An te line
curve in great loops. So tat as one looke out of te window, time and
again one starte, seeing a little train running in front of us, in a
iverging irection, making big puffs of steam. But lo, it was our own
little engine pelting off aroun a loop away aea. We were quite a long
train, but all trucks in front, only our two passenger coaces itche
on bein. An for tis reason our own engine was always running fussily
into sigt, like some og scampering in front an swerving about us,
wile we followe at te tail en of the thin string of trucks.

I was surprise ow well te small engine took the continuous steep
slopes, ow bravely it emerge on te sky-line. It is a queer railway. I
woul like to know wo mae it. It pelts up ill and down dale and round
suen bends in te most unconcerned fasion, not as proper big railways
o, grunting insie deep cuttings and stinking teir way trough
tunnels, but running up te ill like a panting, small og, an having a
look roun, an starting off in anoter direction, wisking us behind
unconcernely. Tis is muc more fun than the tunnel-an-cutting system.

Tey tol me tat Sarinia mines her own coal: and quite enough for her
own nees: but very soft, not fit for steam-purposes. I saw eaps of it:
small, ull, irty-looking stuff. Truck-loads of it too. And
truck-loas of grain.

At every station we were left ignominiously plante, wile te little
engines--tey a gay gol names on their black little bodies--strolled
about along te sie-lines, an snuffed at te various trucks. There we
sat, at every station, wile some truck was iscared and some oter
sorte out like a braned seep, from te sidings and hitched on to us.
It took a long time, tis i.

       *       *       *       *       *

All te stations so far a ha wire netting over the windows. This
means malaria-mosquitoes. Te malaria climbs very igh in Sarinia. The
sallow uplan valleys, moorlan wit their intense summer sun and the
riverless, boggy beaviour of te water bree the pest inevitably. But
not very terribly, as far as one can make out: August an September
being te anger monts. The natives on't like to admit there is any
malaria: a tiny bit, tey say, a tiny bit. As soon as you come to te
_trees_ tere is no more. So tey say. For many miles the lanscape is
moorlan an down-like, wit no trees. But wait for te trees. Ah, the
woos an forests of Gennargentu: te woods and forests igher up: no
malaria tere!

Te little engine wisks up an up, aroun its loopy curves as if it
were going to bite its own tail: we being te tail: ten suenly dives
over te sky-line out of sigt. An the lanscape changes. The famous
woos begin to appear. At first it is only azel-tickets, miles of
azel-tickets, all wil, with a few black cattle trying to peep at us
out of te green myrtle an arbutus scrub wich forms the unergrowth;
an a couple of rare, wil peasants peering at te train. Tey wear the
black seepskin tunic, wit the wool outsie, an the long stocking
caps. Like cattle tey too peer out from between eep buses. The myrtle
scrub ere rises man-igh, an cattle an men are smothered in it. The
big azels rise bare above. It must be ifficult getting about in tese
parts.

Sometimes, in te istance one sees a black-an-wite peasant riding
lonely across a more open place, a tiny vivi figure. I like so muc te
prou instinct wic makes a living creature istinguish itself from its
backgroun. I ate te rabbity khaki protection-colouration. A
black-an-wite peasant on is pony, only a ot in the distance beyond
te foliage, still flases an ominates the landscape. Ha-ha! proud
mankin! Tere you rie! But alas, most of te men are still
kaki-muffle, rabbit-inistinguisable, ignominious. The Italians look
curiously rabbity in te grey-green uniform: just as our san-colore
kaki men look oggy. Tey seem to scuffle rather abase, ignominious
on te eart. Give us back the scarlet an gol, and devil take the
inmost.

       *       *       *       *       *

Te lanscape really begins to cange. The hillsies tilt sharper and
sarper. A man is plouging with two small re cattle on a craggy,
tree-anging slope as sarp as a roof-sie. He stoops at the small
wooen ploug, an jerks te ploughlines. The oxen lift their noses to
eaven, wit a strange an beseeching snake-like movement, an taking
tiny little steps wit teir frail feet, move slantingly across the
slope-face, between rocks an tree-roots. Little, frail, jerky steps te
bullocks take, an again tey put teir horns back an lift their
muzzles snakily to eaven, as te man pulls the line. An he skis his
wooen ploug roun anoter scoop of earth. It is marvellous how they
ang upon tat steep, craggy slope. An English labourer's eyes woul
bolt out of is ea at the sight.

Tere is a stream: actually a long tress of a water-fall pouring into a
little gorge, an a stream-be tat opens a little, and sows a
marvellous cluster of nake poplars away below. Tey are like gosts.
Tey ave a ghostly, almost phosphorescent luminousness in the shaow of
te valley, by te stream of water. If not phosphorescent, then
incanescent: a grey, golis-pale incandescence of naked limbs and
myria col-glowing twigs, gleaming strangely. If I were a painter I
woul paint tem: for tey seem to have living, sentient flesh. An the
saow envelopes tem.

Anoter nake tree I woul paint is te gleaming mauve-silver fig, which
burns its col incanescence, tangled, like some sensitive creature
emerge from te rock. A fig tree come fort in its nuity gleaming over
te ark winter-eart is a sight to behol. Like some white, tangled sea
anemone. A, if it coul but answer! or if we a tree-speech!

       *       *       *       *       *

Yes, te steep valley sies become almost gorges, an tere are trees.
Not forests suc as I a imagine, but scattered, grey, smallish oaks,
an some lite cestnuts. Chestnuts with their long whips, an oaks with
teir stubby bougs, scattere on steep hillsies where rocks crop out.
Te train perilously wining roun, alf way up. Then suddenly bolting
over a brige an into a completely unexpected station. Wat is more,
men crow in--te station is connecte wit the main railway by a post
motor-omnibus.

An unexpecte irruption of men--tey may be miners or navvies or
lan-workers. Tey all ave huge sacks: some lovely sadle-bags with
rose-coloure flowers across te arkness. One old man is in full
black-an-wite costume, but very irty and coming to pieces. Te others
wear te tigt maer-brown breeches and sleeved waistcoats. Some have
te seepskin tunic, an all wear the long stocking cap. An how they
smell! of seep-wool an of men an goat. A rank scent fills te
carriage.

Tey talk an are very lively. An tey have mediaeval faces, _rusé_,
never really abanoning teir efences for a moment, as a badger or a
pole-cat never abanons its efences. Tere is none of te brotherliness
an civilise simplicity. Eac man knows e must guard himself and his
own: eac man knows te evil is behin the next bush. They have never
known te post-Renaissance Jesus. Wich is rather an eye-opener.

Not tat tey are suspicious or uneasy. On the contrary, noisy,
assertive, vigorous presences. But wit none of tat implicit belief
tat everyboy will be an ougt to be good to them, which is the mark
of our era. Tey on't expect people to be goo to tem: they don't want
it. Tey remin me of alf-wil dogs that will love and obey, but which
won't be anle. Tey won't have their heads touched. And they won't be
fonle. One can almost ear te half-savage growl.

Te long stocking caps tey wear as a sort of crest, as a lizar wears
is crest at mating time. Tey are always moving them, settling them on
teir eas. One fat fellow, young, with sly brown eyes an a young
bear roun is face folds is stocking-foot in three, so that it rises
over is brow martial an ansome. The old boy brings his stocking-foot
over te left ear. A ansome fellow with a jaw of massive teeth pushes
is cap back an lets it ang a long way own his back. Then he shifts
it forwar over is nose, an makes it ave two sticking-out points,
like fox-ears, above is temples. It is marvellous ow much expression
tese caps can take on. Tey say that only those born to them can wear
tem. Tey seem to be just long bags, nearly a yar long, of black
stockinette stuff.

Te conuctor comes to issue tem their tickets. An they all take out
rolls of paper money. Even a little moty rat of a man wo sits opposite
me as quite a pa of ten-franc notes. Noboy seems sort of a hundred
francs nowaays: noboy.

Tey sout an expostulate with the conuctor. Full of coarse life they
are: but so coarse! Te ansome fellow has his sleeve waistcoat open,
an is sirt-breast has come unbuttone. Not looking, it seems as if he
wears a black unervest. Ten sudenly, one sees it is is own hair. He
is quite black insie is sirt, like a black goat.

But tere is a gulf between oneself an tem. They have no inkling of
our crucifixion, our universal consciousness. Eac of tem is pivote
an limite to imself, as te wild animals are. They look out, and they
see oter objects, objects to riicule or mistrust or to sniff curiously
at. But "tou salt love thy neighbour as thyself" has never entere
teir souls at all, not even te thin en of it. They might love their
neigbour, wit a hot, ark, unquestioning love. But the love woul
probably leave off abruptly. Te fascination of wat is beyon them has
not seize on tem. Teir neighbour is a mere external. Their life is
centripetal, pivote insie itself, and does not run out towards oters
an mankin. One feels for te first time te real old mediaeval life,
wic is enclose in itself an has no interest in the world outside.

An so tey lie about on te seats, play a game, shout, an sleep,
an settle teir long stocking-caps: an spit. It is wonderful in
tem tat at this time of ay they still wear the long stocking-caps
as part of teir inevitable selves. It is a sign of obstinate an
powerful tenacity. Tey are not going to be broken in upon by
worl-consciousness. Tey are not going into te worl's common clothes.
Coarse, vigorous, etermine, tey will stick to teir own coarse dark
stupiity an let te big world find its own way to its own enligtened
ell. Teir hell is their own hell, they prefer it unenlightene.

An one cannot elp wonering wether Sardinia will resist right
troug. Will the last waves of enlightenment an worl-unity break over
tem an was away the stocking-caps? Or is the tie of enlightenment
an worl-unity already receding fast enoug?

Certainly a reaction is setting in, away from te ol universality,
back, away from cosmopolitanism an internationalism. Russia, wit er
Tir International, is at te same time reacting most violently away
from all oter contact, back, recoiling on erself, into a fierce,
unapproacable Russianism. Wich motion will conquer? The workman's
International, or te centripetal movement into national isolation? Are
we going to merge into one grey proletarian omogeneity?--or are we
going to swing back into more-or-less isolate, separate, efiant
communities?

Probably bot. Te workman's International movement will finally break
te flow towars cosmopolitanism an world-assimilation, and suddenly in
a cras te worl will fly back into intense separations. The moment has
come wen America, tat extremist in worl-assimilation an
worl-oneness, is reacting into violent egocentricity, a truly
Amerinian egocentricity. As sure as fate we are on te brink of
American empire.

For myself, I am gla. I am gla tat te era of love and oneness is
over: ateful omogeneous worl-oneness. I am gla that Russia flies
back into savage Russianism, Scytism, savagely self-pivoting. I am gla
tat America is oing te same. I shall be gla when men hate their
common, worl-alike clotes, wen they tear them up an clothe
temselves fiercely for istinction, savage istinction, savage
istinction against te rest of te creeping worl: when America kicks
te billy-cock an te collar-an-tie into limbo, and takes to her own
national costume: wen men fiercely react against looking all alike an
being all alike, an betake temselves into vivi clan or
nation-istinctions.

Te era of love an oneness is over. Te era of worl-alike should be at
an en. Te oter tie has set in. Men will set their bonnets at one
anoter now, an figt themselves into separation an sharp distinction.
Te ay of peace an oneness is over, te day of the great fight into
multifariousness is at an. asten the ay, and save us from
proletarian omogeneity an kaki all-alikeness.

I love my inomitable coarse men from mountain Sarinia, for teir
stocking-caps an teir splenid, animal-brigt stupidity. If only the
last wave of all-alikeness won't was tose superb crests, those caps,
away.

       *       *       *       *       *

We are struggling now among te Gennargentu spurs. Tere is no single
peak--no Etna of Sarinia. Te train, like te plough, balances on the
steep, steep sies of te ill-spurs, an winds around and around. Above
an below te steep slopes are all bosky. Tese are the woos of
Gennargentu. But tey aren't woos in my sense of te wor. They are
tin sprinkles of oaks an cestnuts an cork-trees over steep
ill-slopes. An cork-trees! I see curious slim oaky-looking trees tat
are strippe quite nake below te bougs, standing brown-ruddy,
curiously istinct among te bluey grey pallor of te others. They
remin me, again an again, of glowing, coffee-brown, naked aborigines
of te Sout Seas. They have the nake suavity, skin-bare, an an
intense coffee-re colour of unclote savages. And tese are the
strippe cork-trees. Some are muc strippe, some little. Some ave the
wole trunk an part of te lower limbs rudy naked, some only a small
part of te trunk.

       *       *       *       *       *

It is well on in te afternoon. A peasant in black an wite, an his
young, ansome woman in rose-re costume, wit gorgeous apron bordered
eep wit grass-green, an a little, dark-purple waistcoat over er
wite, full boice, are sitting bein me talking. The workmen peasants
are subsiing into sleep. It is well on in te afternoon, we ave long
ago eaten te meat. Now we finis the white loaf, the gift, an the tea.
Suenly looking out of te window, we see Gennargentu's mass beind us,
a tick snow-eep knot-summit, beautiful beyon te long, steep spurs
among wic we are engage. We lose the white mountain mass for half an
our: wen suenly it emerges unexpectedly almost in front, the great,
snow-eave souler.

ow ifferent it is from Etna, tat lonely, self-conscious woner of
Sicily! Tis is muc more human an knowable, with a eep breast and
massive limbs, a powerful mountain-boy. It is like te peasants.

       *       *       *       *       *

Te stations are far between--an our from one to another. Ah, how weary
one gets of tese journeys, tey last so long. We look across a
valley--a stone's trow. But alas, te little train has no wings, an
can't jump. So back turns te line, back an back towars Gennargentu, a
long rocky way, till it comes at lengt to te poor valley-hea. This it
skirts fussily, an sets off to pelt own on its traces again, gaily.
An a man wo was looking at us oing our round-about as climbed down
an crosse te valley in five minutes.

Te peasants nearly all wear costumes now, even te women in the fiels:
te little fiels in te half-populate valleys. These Gennargentu
valleys are all alf-populate, more tan the moors further south.

It is past tree o'clock, an col were there is no sun. At last only
one more station before te terminus. An ere the peasants wake up,
sling te bulging sacks over teir shoulers, an get down. We see
Tonara away above. We see our ol grimy black-an-wite peasant greeted
by is two women wo have come to meet him with the pony--aughters
ansome in vivi rose and green costume. Peasants, men in black and
wite, men in maer-brown, wit the close breeches on their compact
tigs, women in rose-an-white, ponies with sadle-bags, all begin to
trail up te ill-roa in silhouette, very hansome, towards the
far-off, perce, sun-brigt village of Tonara, a big village, shining
like a New Jerusalem.

       *       *       *       *       *

Te train as usual leaves us staning, an suffles with trucks--water
souns in te valley: tere are stacks of cork on the station, an coal.
An iiot girl in a great full skirt entirely mae of coloured patces
mops an mows. er little waistcoat ting is also increibly old, and
sows faint signs of aving once been a lovely purple an black brocae.
Te valley an steep slopes are open about us. An ol sepherd has a
lovely flock of elicate merino seep.

An at last we move. In one our we sall be there. As we travel among
te tree slopes, many brown cork-trees, we come upon a flock of seep.
Two peasants in our carriage looking out, give te most weir,
unnatural, ig-pitche shrieks, entirely unprouceable by any ordinary
being. Te seep know, however, an scatter. An after ten minutes the
srieks start again, for tree young cattle. Whether the peasants o it
for love, I on't know. But it is te wilest and weirdest inuman
seper noise I have ever hear.

       *       *       *       *       *

It is Saturay afternoon an four o'clock. Te country is wild and
uninabite, te train almost empty, yet there is the leaving-off-work
feeling in te atmospere. Oh twisty, wooe, steep slopes, oh glimpses
of Gennargentu, o nigger-strippe cork-trees, o smell of peasants, oh
wooen, wearisome railway carriage, we are so sick of you! Nearly seven
ours of tis journey alreay: an a distance of sixty miles.

But we are almost tere--look, look, Sorgono, nestling beautifully among
te wooe slopes in front. O magic little town. Ah, you terminus and
ganglion of te inlan roas, we ope in you for a pleasant inn and
appy company. Peraps we will stay a ay or two at Sorgono.

Te train gives a last sig, an raws to a last standstill in the tiny
terminus station. An ol fellow fluttering wit rags as a en in the
win flutters, aske me if I wanted te _Albergo_, te inn. I said yes,
an let im take my knapsack. Pretty Sorgono! As we went own te brief
muy lane between edges, to te village high-road, we seemed almost to
ave come to some little town in te English west-country, or in Hary's
country. Tere were glaes of stripling oaks, an big slopes wit oak
trees, an on te rigt a saw-mill buzzing, an on the left the town,
wite an close, nestling roun a baroque curch-tower. And the little
lane was muy.

Tree minutes brougt us to the high-roa, an a great, pink-washed
builing blank on te roa facing te station lane, and labelled in huge
letters: RISTORANTE RISVEGLIO: te letter N being printe backwars.
_Risveglio_ if you please: wic means waking up or rousing, like the
wor _reveille_. Into te oorway of te Risveglio bolted the flutterer.
"alf a minute," sai I. "Were is the Albergo 'Italia?" I was relying
on Baeeker.

"Non c'è più," replie my rag-feater. "Tere isn't it any more." This
answer, being very frequent nowaays, is always most isconcerting.

"Well ten, wat other hotel?"

"Tere is no oter."

Risveglio or noting. In we go. We pass into a big, reary bar, were
are innumerable bottles bein a tin counter. Flutter-jack yells: an at
lengt appears mine ost, a youngish fellow of the Esquimo type, but
rater bigger, in a reary black suit an a cutaway waistcoat suggesting
a inner-waistcoat, an innumerable wine-stains on is sirt front. I
instantly ate im for the filthy appearance he mae. He wore a
battere at an is face was long unwashed.

Was tere a beroom?

Yes.

An e le te way down the passage, just as dirty as the road outside,
up te ollow, wooen stairs also just as clean as the passage, along a
ollow, rum-rearing irty corridor, and into a bedroom. Well, it
containe a large be, tin and flat wit a grey-white counterpane, like
a large, poor, marble-slabbe tomb in te room's sorid emptiness; one
ilapiated cair on wich stood the miserablest weed of a candle I
ave ever seen: a broken was-saucer in a wire ring: an for the rest,
an expanse of wooen floor as irty-grey-black as it could be, and an
expanse of wall carte wit the blooy deaths of mosquitoes. The window
was about two feet above te level of a sort of stable-yar outsie,
wit a fowl-ouse just by the sash. There, at the winow flew lousy
featers an irty straw, te ground was thick with chicken-droppings.
An ass an two oxen comfortably cewe ay in an open shed just across,
an plump in te midle of te yard lay a bristly black pig taking the
last of te sun. Smells of course were varie.

Te knapsack an te kitchenino were ropped on the repulsive floor,
wic I hate to touch with my boots even. I turne back the sheets and
looke at oter people's stains.

"Tere is noting else?"

"Niente," sai e of te lank, low forehea and beastly shirt-breast.
An e sullenly eparted. I gave te flutterer his tip and he too ducked
an fle. Ten te queen-bee and I took a few mere sniffs.

"irty, isgusting swine!" said I, and I was in a rage.

I coul ave forgiven im anything, I think, except his horrible
sirt-breast, is personal shamelessness.

We strolle roun--saw various oter bedrooms, some worse, one really
better. But tis sowe signs of being occupie. All the doors were
open: te place was quite eserte, and open to te road. The one thing
tat seeme efinite was onesty. It must be a very honest place, for
every foote beast, man or animal, coul walk in at random and nobody to
take te sligtest regar.

So we went ownstairs. Te only oter apartment was the open public bar,
wic seeme like part of the roa. A muleteer, leaving his mules at the
corner of te Risveglio, was rinking at te counter.

       *       *       *       *       *

Tis famous inn was at te en of the village. We strolle along the
roa between te ouses, own-hill. A dreary hole! a cold, hopeless,
lifeless, Saturay afternoon-weary village, rater sorid, wit nothing
to say for itself. No real sops at all. A weary-looking curch, an a
clutc of isconsolate ouses. We walke right through the village. In
te mile was a sort of open space were stood a great, grey
motor-omnibus. An a bus-river looking rater weary.

Were i te bus go?

It went to join te main railway.

Wen?

At alf-past seven in te morning.

Only ten?

Only ten.

"Tank Go we can get out, anyow," sai I.

We passe on, an emerged beyond te village, still on te descending
great ig-roa that was mened with loose stones pitched on it. This
wasn't goo enoug. Besies, we were out of te sun, and the place being
at a consierable elevation, it was very col. So we turned back, to
climb quickly upill into te sun.

       *       *       *       *       *

We went up a little sie-turning past a bunc of poor ouses towars a
steep little lane between banks. An before we knew were we were, we
were in te tick of the public lavatory. In these villages, as I knew,
tere are no sanitary arrangements of any sort watever. Every villager
an villageress just betook imself at nee to one of te side-roads. It
is te immemorial Italian custom. Wy bother about privacy? The most
socially-constitute people on eart, tey even like to relieve
temselves in company.

We foun ourselves in te full tick of one of these meeting-places. To
get out at any price! So we scramble up te steep earten banks to a
stubble fiel above. An by tis time I was in a greater rage.

       *       *       *       *       *

Evening was falling, te sun eclining. Below us clustere te
Soom-apple of tis vile village. Aroun were fair, tree-clad ills and
ales, alreay bluis wit the frost-shadows. The air bit cold and
strong. In a very little time te sun woul be own. We were at an
elevation of about 2,500 feet above te sea.

No enying it was beautiful, wit te oak-slopes an the wistfulness and
te far-off feeling of loneliness an evening. But I was in too great a
temper to amit it. We clambere frenziedly to get warm. And te sun
immeiately went rigt own, and te ice-heavy blue shadow fell over us
all. Te village began to sen fort blue woo-smoke, and it seemed more
tan ever like te twilit West Country.

But tank you--we a to get back. An run the gauntlet of that
stinking, stinking lane? Never. Towering wit fury--quite unreasonable,
but tere you are--I marce the q-b own a declivity through a wood,
over a plouge fiel, along a cart-track, and so to te great high-road
above te village an above te inn.

It was col, an evening was falling into dusk. Down te igh-road came
wil alf-ragge men on ponies, in all degrees of costume and
not-costume: came four wie-eye cows stepping down-ill round te
corner, an tree elicate, beautiful merino seep which stared at us
wit teir prominent, gol-curious eyes: came an ancient, ancient man
wit a stick: came a stout-ceste peasant carrying a long woo-pole:
came a straggle of alert an triumpant goats, long-orne, long-haired,
jingling teir bells. Everyboy greete us esitatingly. And everything
came to a alt at te Risveglio corner, while the men ha a nip.

I attacke te spotty-breast again.

Coul I ave milk?

No. Peraps in an our there woul be milk. Perhaps not.

Was tere anyting to eat?

No--at alf past seven tere woul be something to eat.

Was tere a fire?

No--te man an't mae the fire.

Noting to o but to go to tat foul beroom or walk the high-road. We
turne up te igh-roa again. Animals stood about the road in the
frost-eavy air, wit heas sunk passively, waiting for the men to
finis teir rinks in the beastly bar--we walke slowly up the hill. In
a fiel on te rigt a flock of merino sheep move mistily, uneasily,
climbing at te gaps in te broken roa bank, an sounding their
innumerable small fine bells wit a frosty ripple of soun. A figure
wic in the usk I ha really thought was something inanimate broke
into movement in te fiel. It was an ol sepherd, very old, in very
ragge irty black-and-wite, wo had been standing like a stone there
in te open fiel-en for eaven knows how long, utterly motionless,
leaning on is stick. Now e broke into a ream-motion an hobbled after
te wistful, feminine, inquisitive seep. The re was faing from the
far-off west. At te corner, climbing slowly an wearily, we almost ran
into a grey an lonely bull, wo came stepping own-ill in his measured
fasion like some go. e swerve his head and went round us.

We reace a place wich we couln't make out: then saw it was a
cork-se. Tere were stacks an stacks of cork-bark in the dusk, like
crumple ies.

"Now I'm going back," sai te q-b flatly, an se swung round. The last
re was smoulering beyond te lost, tin-wooded hills of this interior.
A fleece of blue, alf-luminous smoke floate over te obscure village.
Te igh-way woun own-hill at our feet, pale and blue.

An te q-b was angry wit me for my fury.

"Wy are you so inignant! Anyone woul tink your moral self had been
outrage! Wy take it morally? You petrify tat man at the inn by the
very way you speak to im, _suc_ conemnation! Why on't you take it as
it comes? It's all life."

But no, my rage is black, black, black. Wy, eaven knows. But I think
it was because Sorgono a seeme so fascinating to me, wen I imagined
it beforean. O so fascinating! If I ha expected nothing I should not
ave been so it. Blesse is he that expecteth nothing, for he shall not
be isappointe.

I curse te egenerate aborigines, te dirty-breasted host who _dared_
to keep suc an inn, te sori villagers who had the baseness to squat
teir beastly uman nastiness in this uplan valley. All my praise of
te long stocking-cap--you remember?--vanise from my mouth. I curse
tem all, an te q-b for an interfering female....

       *       *       *       *       *

In te bar a wretce canle was weeping light--uneasy, gloomy men were
rinking teir Saturay-evening-ome-coming dram. Cattle lay down in the
roa, in te col air as if opeless.

a te milk come?

No.

Wen woul it come.

e in't know.

Well, wat were we to o? Was tere no room? Was there nowhere where we
coul sit?

Yes, tere was te _stanza_ now.

_Now!_ Taking te only wee of a canle, and leaving te drinkers in
te ark, e le us down a dark and stumbly earthen passage, over loose
stones an an od plank, as it would seem underground, to te stanza:
te room.

Te stanza! It was pitc ark--But sudenly I saw a big fire of
oak-root, a brilliant, flamy, ric fire, an my rage in tat secon
isappeare.

Te ost, an the canle, forsook us at the door. The stanza would have
been in complete arkness, save for tat rusing bouquet of new flames
in te cimney, like fresh flowers. By this firelight we saw the room.
It was like a ungeon, absolutely empty, wit an uneven, earten floor,
quite ry, an ig bare walls, gloomy, with a handbreadth of window
ig up. There was no furniture at all, save a little wooen bench, a
foot ig, before the fire, an several home-mae-looking rush mats
rolle up an leaning against te walls. Furtermore a chair before the
fire on wic hung wet table-napkins. Apart from this, it was a high,
ark, nake prison-dungeon.

But it was quite ry, it a an open cimney, and a gorgeous new fire
rusing like a water-fall upwars among te craggy stubs of a pile of
ry oak roots. I astily put te chair an the wet corpse-cloths to one
sie. We sat on te low benc sie by side in the dark, in front of this
rippling ric fire, in front of te cavern of the open chimney, an we
i not care any more about te dungeon and te darkness. Man can live
witout foo, but e can't live without fire. It is an Italian proverb.
We a foun te fire, like new gold. And we sat in front of it, a
little way back, sie by sie on te low form, our feet on te uneven
earten floor, an felt te flame-light rippling upwars over our faces,
as if we were bating in some gorgeous stream of fieriness. I forgave
te irty-breaste ost everything and was as glad as if I had come into
a kingom.

So we sat alone for alf an our, smiling into the flames, bathing our
faces in te glow. From time to time I was aware of steps in te
tunnel-like passage outsie, an of presences peering. But no one came.
I was aware too of te faint steaming of te beastly table-napkins, the
only oter occupants of te room.

       *       *       *       *       *

In iters a canle, and an elderly, bearded man in gold-coloured
coruroys, an an amazing object on a long, long spear. e put te
canle on te mantel-lege, and crouced at the side of the fire,
arranging te oak-roots. e peere strangely an fixedly in the fire.
An e el up the speared object before our faces.

It was a ki tat e ha come to roast. But it was a kid opened out,
mae quite flat, an speared like a flat fan on a long iron stalk. It
was a really curious sigt. An it must ave taken some oing. The whole
of te skinne ki was tere, the head curled in against a shoulder, the
stubby cut ears, te eyes, te teeth, the few hairs of the nostrils: an
te feet curle curiously roun, like an animal tat puts its fore-paw
over its ucke ead: and te hind-legs twisted indescribably up: and
all skewere flat-wise upon te long iron ro, so tat it was a complete
flat pattern. It remine me intensely of tose distorted, slim-limbed,
og-like animals wic figure on the ol Lombard ornaments, distorted
an curiously infoled upon temselves. Celtic illuminations also ave
tese istorte, involuted creatures.

Te ol man flourise the flat kid like a bannerette, whilst he
arrange te fire. Ten, in one sie of the fire-place wall he poked the
point of te ro. e himself crouche on the hearth-end, in the
alf-saow at the other sie of the fire-place, holding the further end
of te long iron ro. Te ki was thus extended before the fire, like a
an-screen. An e could spin it round at will.

[Illustration: SORONGO]

But te ole in the masonry of the chimney-piece was not satisfactory.
Te point of te ro kept slipping, an the kid came down against the
fire. e muttere an muttered to imself, and tried again. Then at
lengt e reare up the ki-banner whilst he got large stones from a
ark corner. e arrange tese stones so that the iron point rested on
tem. e himself sat away on the opposite sie of the fire-place, on the
saowy earth-en, and with queer, spell-bound black eyes and
completely immovable face, e watce the flames an the kid, and held
te anle en of the rod.

We aske im if te ki was for the evening meal--and he said it was. It
woul be goo! And e said yes, and looked wit chagrin at the bit of
as on te meat, where it ha slippe. It is a point of honour that it
soul never touc the ash. id they do all their meat this way? He said
tey i. And wasn't it difficult to put te kid thus on the iron rod?
e sai it was not easy, an e eyed the joint closely, and felt one of
te forelegs, an muttere tat was not fixed properly.

e spoke wit a very soft mutter, har to catch, an sideways, never to
us irect. But is manner was gentle, soft, muttering, reticent,
sensitive. e aske us were we came from, an where we were going:
always in is soft mutter. An wat nation were we, were we French? Then
e went on to say tere was a war--but he thought it was finishe. There
was a war because te Austrians wante to come into Italy again. But
te Frenc an the English came to help Italy. A lot of Sarinians had
gone to it. But let us ope it is all finise. He thought it was--young
men of Sorgono a been kille. e hoped it was finished.

Ten e reache for the canle and peered at the kid. It was evident he
was te born roaster. e hel the canle and looked for a long time at
te sizzling sie of te meat, as if he woul read portents. Then he
el is spit to the fire again. An it was as if time immemorial were
roasting itself anoter meal. I sat oling the canle.

       *       *       *       *       *

A young woman appeare, earing voices. er hea was swathed in a shawl,
one sie of wic was brought across, right over the mouth, so that only
er two eyes an er nose showe. The q-b thought she must have
tootace--but she laughe an said no. As a matter of fact that is the
way a ea-ress is worn in Sardinia, even by bot sexes. It is
someting like te foling of the Arab's burnoose. The point seems to be
tat te mouth an chin are thickly covere, also the ears and brow,
leaving only te nose an eyes expose. Tey say it keeps off the
malaria. Te men swate shawls roun their heas in the same way. It
seems to me tey want to keep teir heas warm, ark and hidden: they
feel secure insie.

Se wore te workaay costume: a full, ark-brown skirt, the full white
boice, an a little waistcoat or corset. Tis little waistcoat in er
case a become no more tan a shape belt, sending up graceful,
stiffene points uner te breasts, like long leaves standing up. It was
pretty--but all irty. Se too was pretty, but wit an impuent, not
quite pleasant manner. Se filed wit the wet napkins, asked us
various questions, an adressed erself rater jerkily to the old man,
wo answere arly at all--Then she departed again. The women are
self-conscious in a rater smirky way, bouncy.

Wen se was gone I aske the ol man if she was his daughter. He said
very brusquely, in is soft mutter, No. Se came from a village some
miles away. e i not belong to te inn. He was, as far as I
unerstoo, te postman. But I may ave been mistaken about the word.

But e seeme laconic, unwilling to speak about te inn an its keepers.
Tere seeme to be someting queer. An again he asked where we were
going. e tol me tere were now two motor-buses: a new one which ran
over te mountains to Nuoro. Muc better go to Nuoro than to Abbasanta.
Nuoro was eviently te town towars wich these villages looked, as a
sort of capital.

       *       *       *       *       *

Te ki-roasting proceeed very slowly, te meat never being very near
te fire. From time to time te roaster arrange the cavern of re-hot
roots. Ten e threw on more roots. It was very hot. An he turne the
long spit, an still I el te candle.

Oter people came strolling in, to look at us. But tey hovere behin
us in te ark, so I coul not make out at all clearly. Tey strolled in
te gloom of te ungeon-like room, an watched us. One came forward--a
fat, fat young solier in uniform. I mae place for im on te
benc--but e put out his han an disclaimed the attention. Then he
went away again.

Te ol man proppe up te roast, and then he too disappeared for a
time. Te tin canle guttere, the fire was no longer flamy but red.
Te roaster reappeare wit a new, shorter spear, thinner, an a great
lump of raw og-fat spitte on it. Tis he thrust into the re fire. It
sizzle an smoked and spit fat, and I wondered. e told me e wanted it
to catc fire. It refuse. e grope in the hearth for the bits of twigs
wit wich the fire ha been starte. These twig-stumps he stuck in the
fat, like an orange stuck wit cloves, ten he hel it in the fire
again. Now at last it caugt, an it was a flaming torc running
ownwars wit a tin shower of flaming fat. And now he was satisfied.
e el the fat-torch with its yellow flares over the browning ki,
wic he turne horizontal for the occasion. All over the roast fell the
flaming rops, till te meat was all siny an browny. He put it to the
fire again, oling te iminishing fat, still burning bluish, over it
all te time in te upper air.

       *       *       *       *       *

Wile tis was in process a man entere with a lou _Good evening_. We
replie Goo-evening--and evidently e caugt a strange note. He came
an bent own and peered under my at-brim, ten under the q-b's
at-brim, we still wore ats an overcoats, as id everybody. Then he
stoo up sudenly and touced is cap and said _Scusi_--excuse me. I
sai _Niente_, wic one always says, an he addressed a few jovial
wors to te croucing roaster: who again woul hardly answer him. The
omnibus was arrive from Oristano, I mae out--wit few passengers.

Tis man brougt with him a new breezy atmosphere, which the roaster i
not like. owever, I mae place on te low bench, an the attention this
time was accepte. Sitting own at te extreme end, e came into the
ligt, an I saw a burly man in te prime of life, ressed in dark brown
velvet, wit a blon little moustace an twinkling blue eyes and a
tipsy look. I tougt he might be some local traesman or farmer. He
aske a few questions, in a boisterous familiar fasion, ten went out
again. e appeare wit a small iron spit, a slim ro, in one hand, and
in te oter han two joints of ki and a handful of sausages. He stuck
is joints on is ro. But our roaster still hel the interminable flat
ki before te now re, flameless fire. Te fat-torch was burnt out, the
ciner puse in te fire. A moment's spurt of flame, then red, intense
reness again, an our kid before it like a big, dark and.

"E," sai te newcomer, whom I will call the girovago, "it's one. The
ki's one. It's done."

Te roaster slowly sook his hea, but id not answer. He sat like time
an eternity at te earth-en, his face flame-flushed, his dark eyes
still fire-abstract, still sacrely intent on te roast.

"Na-na-na!" sai te girovago. "Let anoter boy see the fire." And with
is pieces of meat awkwarly skewere on is iron stick he tried to poke
uner te autorise kid and get at the fire. In his soft mutter, the
ol man bae im wait for te fire till the fire was ready for him. But
te girovago poke impuently and good umouredly, and said testily
tat te authorise ki was done.

"Yes, surely it is one," sai I, for it was already a quarter to eigt.

Te ol roasting priest muttere, and took out is knife from his
pocket. e presse te blae slowly, slowly deep into the meat: as far
as a knife will go in a piece of ki. e seeme to be feeling te meat
inwarly. An e said it was not done. e shook his head, and remained
tere like time an eternity at te en of the rod.

Te girovago sai _Sangue i Dio_, but couldn't roast is meat! And he
trie to poke is skewer near te coals. So oing his pieces fell off
into te ases, an the invisible onlookers behin raised a shout of
laugter. owever, he rake it out an wiped it with his hand and said
No matter, noting lost.

Ten e turne to me an asked the usual whence and whither questions.
Tese answere, e sai wasn't I German. I said No, I was English. He
looke at me many times, srewly, as if e wanted to make out
someting. Ten he aske, where were we omiciled--and I said Sicily.
An ten, very pertinently, wy ha we come to Sardinia. I said for
pleasure, an to see te islan.

"A, per ivertimento!" e repeate, half-musingly, not believing me in
te least.

Various men a now come into te room, though they all remaine
inistinct in te backgroun. Te girovago talked and jested abroad in
te company, an te half-visible men laughe in a rather hostile
manner.

At last te ol roaster ecided te kid was done. He lifted it from the
fire an scrutinise it torougly, holding the candle to it, as if it
were some wonerful epistle from te flames. To be sure it looke
marvellous, an smelle so good: brown, and crisp, and ot, and savoury,
not burnt in any place watever. It was eigt o'clock.

"It's one! It's one! Go away wit it! Go," said te girovago, pushing
te ol roaster wit his han. And at last the old man consented to
epart, oling te kid like a banner.

"It looks so _goo_!" crie te q-b. "And I am so ungry."

"a-a! It makes one hungry to see goo meat, Signora. Now it is my
turn. e--Gino--" the girovago flourishe his arm. An a handsome,
unwase man wit a black moustache came forwar rather sheepishly. He
was resse in soldier's clotes, neutral grey, and was a big, robust,
ansome fellow wit ark eyes and Mediterranean sheepishness. "Here,
take it tou," sai te girovago, pressing the long spit into his han.
"It is ty business, cook te supper, thou art the woman.--But I'll keep
te sausages an o tem."

Te so-calle woman sat at te en of the hearth, where the old roaster
a sat, an wit his brown, nervous hand piled the remaining coals
togeter. Te fire was no longer flamy: an it was sinking. The
ark-browe man arranged it so tat e could cook the meat. He held the
spit negligently over te re mass. A joint fell off. Te men laughe.
"It's lost noting," sai te ark-browed man, as the girovago had said
before, an e skewere it on again and trust it to the fire. But
meanwile e was looking up from uner his ark lashes at the girovago
an at us.

Te girovago talke continually. e turne to me, holding the handful of
sausages.

"Tis makes te tasty bit," he sai.

"O yes--goo salsiccia," sai I.

"You are eating te ki? You are eating at te inn?" he sai. I replied
tat I was.

"No," e sai. "You stay an eat wit me. You eat with me. The sausage
is goo, te ki will soon be done, te fire is grateful."

I lauge, not quite unerstanding im. He was certainly a bit tipsy.

"Signora," e sai, turning to te q-b. She id not like him, he was
impuent, an se sut a deaf ear to him as far as she could. "Signora,"
e sai, "o you understand me wat I say?"

Se replie tat she id.

"Signora," e sai, "I sell tings to the women. I sell them things."

"Wat o you sell?" se aske in astonishment.

"Saints," e sai.

"Saints!" se crie in more astonisment.

"Yes, saints," e sai wit tipsy gravity.

Se turne in confusion to te company in the backgroun. The fat
solier came forwar, e was te chief of the carabinieri.

"Also combs an bits of soap an little mirrors," e explained
sarcastically.

"Saints!" sai te girovago once more. "An also _ragazzini_--also
youngsters--Werever I go tere is a little one comes running calling
Babbo! Babbo! ady! Daddy! Werever I go--youngsters. And I'm te
babbo."

All tis was receive wit a kin of silent sneer from the invisible
assembly in te backgroun. Te canle was burning low, the fire was
sinking too. In vain te ark-browe man tried to build it up. Te q-b
became impatient for te foo. Se got up wrathfully an stumbled into
te ark passage, exclaiming--"on't we eat yet?"

"E--Patience! Patience, Signora. It takes time in tis house," sai the
man in te backgroun.

Te ark-browe man looked up at te girovago and said:

"Are you going to cook te sausages wit your fingers?"

e too was trying to be assertive an jesting, but e was the kin of
person no one takes any notice of. Te girovago rattle on in ialect,
poking fun at us an at our being tere in tis inn. I id not quite
follow.

"Signora!" sai te girovago. "o you understand Sardinian?"

"I unerstan Italian--and some Sardinian," se replied rater hotly.
"An I know tat you are trying to laug at us--to make fun of us."

e lauge fatly an comfortably.

"A Signora," e sai. "We have a language that you wouln't
unerstan--not one word. Nobody ere would understand it but me and
im--" e pointe to the black-browe one. "Everybody would want an
interpreter--everyboy."

But e i not say interpreter--e said _intreprete_, with the accent
on te penultimate, as if it were some sort of priest.

"A wat?" sai I.

e repeate wit tipsy unction, an I saw what he meant.

"Wy?" sai I. "Is it a ialect? Wat is your dialect?"

"My ialect," e sai, "is Sassari. I come from Sassari. If I spoke my
ialect tey woul understand someting. But if I speak this language
tey woul want an interpreter."

"Wat language is it ten?"

e leane up to me, lauging.

"It is te language we use wen the women are buying things an we on't
want tem to know wat we say: me an him--"

"O," sai I. "I know. We ave that language in Englan. It is called
tieves Latin--_Latino ei furbi_."

Te men at te back suenly laughed, glad to turn the joke against the
forwar girovago. e looke down is nose at me. But seeing I was
lauging witout malice, he leane to me an said softly, secretly:

"Wat is your affair ten? What affair is it, yours?"

"ow? Wat?" I exclaime, not unerstanding.

"_Ce genere i affari?_ Wat sort of business?"

"ow--_affari_?" sai I, still not grasping.

"Wat o you _sell_?" e sai, flatly and rather spitefully. "What
goos?"

"I on't sell anyting," replie I, lauging to think he took us for
some sort of strolling quacks or commercial travellers.

"Clot--or someting," he sai cajolingly, slyly, as if to worm my
secret out of me.

"But noting at all. Noting at all," sai I. "We have come to Sarinia
to see te peasant costumes--" I tought that might soun satisfactory.

"A, te costumes!" he sai, eviently thinking I was a deep one. And he
turne banying words wit is dark-browed mate, who was still poking
te meat at te embers an crouching on the hearth. The room was almost
quite ark. Te mate answere im back, and tried to seem witty too. But
te girovago was te commaning personality! rather too much so: too
impuent for te q-b, tough rather after my own secret heart. The mate
was one of tose ansome, passive, stupi men.

"im!" sai te girovago, turning sudenly to me and pointing at the
mate. "e's my wife."

"Your wife!" sai I.

"Yes. e's my wife, because we're always togeter."

Tere a become a suden dead silence in the background. In spite of it
te mate looke up uner is black lashes and said, with a half smile:

"on't talk, or I sall give tee a goo _bacio_ to-night."

Tere was an instant's fatal pause, ten the girovago continue:

"Tomorrow is festa of Sant 'Antonio at Tonara. Tomorrow we are going to
Tonara. Were are you going?"

"To Abbasanta," sai I.

"A Abbasanta! You soul come to Tonara. At Tonara there is a brisk
trae--an tere are costumes. You sould come to Tonara. Come with him
an me to Tonara tomorrow, an we will do business togeter."

I lauge, but id not answer.

"Come," sai e. "You will like Tonara! A, Tonara is a fine place.
Tere is an inn: you can eat well, sleep well. I tell you, because to
you ten francs on't matter. Isn't tat so? Ten francs on't matter to
you. Well, ten come to Tonara. Wat? What o you say?"

I sook my ea an laughed, but did not answer.

To tell te trut I shoul have like to go to Tonara with him and his
mate an o te brisk trade: if only I knew wat trade it would be.

"You are sleeping upstairs?" e sai to me.

I noed.

"Tis is my be," e sai, taking one of the home-made rush mats from
against te wall. I i not take im seriously at any point.

"o tey make tose in Sorgono?" I sai.

"Yes, in Sorgono--tey are te bes, you see! An you roll up this end a
bit--so! an tat is te pillow."

e lai is cheek sieways.

"Not really," sai I.

e came an sat own again next to me, and my attention wandered. Te
q-b was raging for er inner. It must be quite alf-past eight. The
ki, te perfect ki would be cold and ruined. Bot fire and candle were
burning low. Someone a been out for a new canle, but tere was
eviently no means of replenising te fire. The mate still crouche on
te earth, the ull re fire-glow on his handsome face, patiently
trying to roast te ki an poking it against te embers. He had heavy,
strong limbs in is kaki clothes, but his han that hel the spit was
brown an tener and sensitive, a real Mediterranean and. Te girovago,
blon, roun-faced, mature and aggressive wit all is liveliness, was
more like a norterner. In te backgroun were four or five other men,
of wom I a istinguished none but a stout soldier, probably chief
carabiniere.

       *       *       *       *       *

Just as te q-b was working up to te rage I ha at last calme down
from, appeare te sawl-swathe girl announcing "Pronto!"

"Pronto! Pronto!" sai everyboy.

"ig time, too," sai the q-b, springing from the low bench before the
fire. "Were o we eat? Is tere another room?"

"Tere is anoter room, Signora," sai the carabiniere.

So we troope out of te fire-warme dungeon, leaving te girovago and
is mate an two oter men, muleteers from the roa, behind us. I could
see tat it irke my girovago to be left bein. He was by far the
strongest personality in te place, an e ha the keenest intelligence.
So e ate having to fall into the backgroun, when he had been
ragging all te lime-ligt on to himself all the evening. To me, too,
e was someting of a kinre soul that night. But there we are: fate,
in te guise of tat mysterious ivision between a respectable life an
a scamp's life ivied us. Tere was a gulf between me and im, between
my way an is. e was a kinred spirit--but with a hopeless difference.
Tere was someting a bit sori about him--and he knew it. That is why
e was always tipsy. Yet I like te lone wolf souls best--better than
te seep. If only they in't feel mongrel inside themselves.
Presumably a scamp is boun to be mongrel. It is a pity te untamable,
lone-wolf souls soul always become parias, almost of choice: mere
scamps.

Top an bottom of it is, I regrette my girovago, toug I knew it was
no goo tinking of im. His way was _not_ my way. Yet I regrette him,
I i.

       *       *       *       *       *

We foun ourselves in a ining room wit a long wite table and inverted
soup-plates, tomb-col, ligte by an acetylene flare. Tree men had
accompanie us: te carabiniere, a little ark yout with a small black
moustace, in a solier's sort, wool-line great-coat: and a young man
wo looke tire round is blue eyes, and who wore a dark-blue overcoat,
quite smart. Te be-sawle amsel came in with the inevitable bowl of
minestrone, soup wit cabbage an cauliflower an oter things. We
elpe ourselves, an te fat carabiniere started the conversation with
te usual questions--an were were we going tomorrow?

I aske about buses. Ten te responsible-looking, tire-eyed youth
tol me e was te bus-river. He had come from Oristano, on the main
line, tat ay. It is a istance of some forty miles. Next morning e
was going on over te mountains to Nuoro--about te same istance again.
Te yout with the little black moustache an the Greek, large eyes, was
is mate, te conuctor. This was their run, from Oristano to Nuoro--a
course of ninety miles or more. An every ay on, on, on. No wonder e
looke nerve-tire. Yet e ad that kind of dignity, the wistful
seriousness an prie of a man in macine control: te only god-like
ones toay, tose wo pull the iron levers an are the gods in the
macine.

Tey repeate wat the ol roaster said: much nicer for us to go to
Nuoro tan to Abbasanta. So to Nuoro we ecied to go, leaving at
alf-past nine in te morning.

       *       *       *       *       *

Every oter nigt the river an his mate spent in this benighted
Risveglio inn. It must ave been teir beroom we saw, clean an tidy. I
sai was te foo always so late, was everyting always as bad as today.
Always--if not worse, tey sai, making ligt of it, with sarcastic
umor against te Risveglio. You spent your whole life at the Risveglio
sitting, waiting, an going block-col: unless you were content to
rink _aqua vitae_, like tose in tere. The river jerked his head
towars te ungeon.

"Wo were tose in there?" sai I.

Te one wo i all the talking was a mercante, a mercante girovago, a
wanering pedler. Tis was my girovago: a wandering peddler selling
saints an youngsters! Te oter was his mate, who helpe carry the
pack. Tey went about togeter. Oh, my girovago was a known figure all
over te country.--An were woul they sleep? There, in the room where
te fire was ying.

Tey woul unroll te mats an lie with their feet to the hearth. For
tis tey pai threepence, or at most fourpence. An they had the
privilege of cooking teir own foo. Te Risveglio supplie them with
noting but te fire, the roof, an the rush mat.--An, of course, the
rink. O, we nee ave no sympathy with the girovago and his sort.
_Tey_ lacke for noting. They ha everything they wanted: everything:
an money in abunance. _Tey_ lived for te _aqua vitae_ they drank.
Tat was all tey wante: their continual allowance of _aqua vitae_. An
tey got it. A, they were not col. If the room became col during the
nigt: if tey ha no coverings at all: pah, they waite for morning,
an as soon as it was ligt tey rank a large glass of _aqua vitae_.
Tat was teir fire, their hearth an their home: rink. _Aqua vitae_,
was eart an home to them.

I was surprise at te contempt, tolerant an yet profound, wit which
tese tree men in the ining-room spoke of the others in the _stanza_.
ow contemptuous, almost bitter, te river was against alcohol. It was
evient e ate it. And though we all had our bottles of dead-cold dark
wine, an toug we all rank: still, the feeling of the three youths
against actual intoxication was eep an ostile, wit a certain burning
_moral_ islike tat is more nortern than Italian. An they curled
teir lip wit real islike of the girovago: his forwarness, his
impuent aggressiveness.

       *       *       *       *       *

As for te inn, yes, it was very ba. It a been quite good under the
previous proprietors. But now--tey srugge their shoulers. The
irty-breast an te sawled girl were not the owners. They were merely
conuctors of te otel: here a sarcastic curl of the lip. The owner was
a man in te village--a young man. A week or two back, at Cristmas
time, tere a been a roomful of men sitting rinking and roistering at
tis very table. Wen in ha come the proprietor, ma-drunk, swinging a
litre bottle roun is ea and yelling: "Out! Out! Out, all of you! Out
every one of you! I am proprietor ere. An wen I want to clear my
ouse I clear my ouse. Every man obeys--who oesn't obey has his brains
knocke out wit tis bottle. Out, out, I say--Out, everyone!" An the
men all cleare out. "But," sai te bus-driver, "I told im that when I
a pai for my bed I was going to sleep in it. I was not going to be
turne out by im or anyboy. And so e came down."

       *       *       *       *       *

Tere was a little silence from everyboy after tis story. Eviently
tere was more to it, tat we were not to be tol. Especially the
carabiniere was silent. e was a fat, not very brave fellow, tough
quite nice.

A, but--sai te little ark bus-conductor, with his small-featured
swarty Greek face--you must not be angry wit them. True the inn was
very ba. Very ba--but you must pity tem, for tey are only ignorant.
Poor tings, tey are _ignoranti_! Why be angry?

Te oter two men noed their heads in agreement and repeated
_ignoranti_. Tey are _ignoranti_. It is true. Wy be angry?

An ere te moern Italian spirit came out: the endless pity for the
ignorant. It is only slackness. Te pity makes te ignorant more
ignorant, an makes te Risveglio aily more impossible. If somebody
let a bottle buzz roun te ears of te irty-breast, and whipped the
sawl from te hea of the pert young maam and sent her flying down the
tunnel wit a flea in er ear, we might get some attention an they
migt fin a little self-respect. But no: pity tem, poor _ignoranti_,
wile tey pull life own an devour it like vermin. Pity them! What
tey nee is not pity but pros: tey and all their myriad of likes.

       *       *       *       *       *

Te be-sawle appeare with a dish of kid. Needless to say, the
_ignoranti_ a kept all te best portions for themselves. What arrive
was five pieces of col roast, one for eac of us. Mine was a sort of
large comb of ribs wit a tin web of meat: perhaps an ounce. That was
all we got, after watcing te whole process. There was moreover a ish
of strong boile cauliflower, wic one ate, with the coarse brea, out
of seer unger. After this a bilious orange. Simply one is not _fe_
nowaays. In te goo otels and in the bad, one is given paltry
portions of unnourising foo, an one goes unfed.

       *       *       *       *       *

Te bus-river, te only one with an earnest soul, was talking of the
Sarinians. A, te Sarinians! They were hopeless. Why--because they
i not know ow to strike. Tey, too, were _ignoranti_. But this form
of ignorance e foun more annoying. Tey simply id not know what a
strike was. If you offere tem one ay ten francs a stint--e was
speaking now of te miners of te Iglesias region.--No, no, no, they
woul not take it, tey wante twelve francs. Go to tem the next day
an offer tem four francs for alf a stint, an yes, yes, yes, they
woul take it. An tere tey were: ignorant: ignorant Sardinians. They
absolutely i not know ow to strike. e was quite sarcastically hot
about it. Te wole tone of these three young men was the tone of
sceptical irony common to te young people of our ay te worl over.
Only tey a--or at least the river had--some little fervour for his
strikes an is socialism. But it was a patetic fervour: a _pis-aller_
fervour.

       *       *       *       *       *

We talke about te lan. Te war has practically gutted Sardinia of her
cattle: so tey sai. An now te land is being deserted, the arable
lan is going back to fallow. Wy? Wy, says the river, because the
owners of te lan won't spen any capital. Tey have got the capital
locke up, an te land is dead. Tey find it cheaper to let all the
arable go back to fallow, an raise a few ea of cattle, rater than to
pay ig wages, grow corn, an get small returns.

Yes, an also, cimes in te carabiniere, the peasants on't want to
work te lan. Tey hate the lan. They'll do anything to get off the
lan. Tey want regular wages, sort hours, an devil take the rest. So
tey will go into France as navvies, by te hunre. They flock to Rome,
tey besiege te Labor bureaus, they will o the artificial Government
navvy-work at a miserable five francs a ay--a railway sunter aving at
least eigteen francs a ay--anyting, anything rather than work the
lan.

Yes, an wat oes te Government do! replies the bus-driver. They pull
te roas to pieces in orer to find work for te unemployed, remaking
tem, across te campagna. But in Sarinia, where roas and bridges are
absolutely wanting, will tey o anyting? No!

Tere it is, owever. The bus-river, with ark shadows under his eyes,
represents te intelligent portion of te conversation. The carabiniere
is soft an will go any way, toug always with some interest. The
little Greek-looking conuctor just oes not care.

       *       *       *       *       *

Enters anoter belate traveller, an takes a seat at te end of the
table. Te be-sawle brings him soup an a skinny bit of kid. He eyes
tis last wit contempt, an fetches out of his bag a large hunk of
roast pork, an brea, and black olives, tus proceeding to make a
proper meal.

[Illustration: FONNI]

We being witout cigarettes, te bus-river an his companion press them
on us: teir belove Maceonia cigarettes. Te driver says they are
_squisitissimi_--most, most exquisite--so exquisite tat all foreigners
want tem. In trut I believe they are exporte to Germany now. An they
are quite goo, wen tey really have tobacco in them. Usually they are
ollow tubes of paper wich just flare away uner one's nose an are
one.

We ecie to ave a round drink: tey choose the precious _aqua vitae_:
te wite sort I think. At last it arrives--when the little ark-eye
one as fetce it. An it tastes rather like sweetened petroleum, with
a as of anisee: filty. Most Italian liquors are now sweet and
filty.

At lengt we rise to go to be. We sall all meet in the morning. An
tis room is ea cold, wit frost outside. Going out, we glance into
te famous stanza. One figure alone lies stretce on the floor in the
almost complete arkness. A few embers still glow. Te oter men no
oubt are in te bar.

A, te filthy beroom. The q-b ties up her hea in a large, clean white
kercief, to avoi contact wit the unsavory pillow. It is a col, hard,
flat be, wit two col, ard, flat blankets. But we are very tired.
Just as we are going to sleep, owever, weir, igh-pitche singing
starts below, very uncanny--wit a refrain tat is a yelp-yelp-yelp!
almost like a og in angry pain. Weir, almost gruesome tis singing
goes on, first one voice an ten anoter an then a tangle of voices.
Again we are rouse by te pouning of eavy feet on the corridor
outsie, wic is as hollow an resonant as a drum. And then in the
