broter-in-law ane in the ox-blood-red suit-case, tottering behind,
an te bus surge savagely out of Orosei.

       *       *       *       *       *

Almost in a moment we a left te town on its slope, an there below us
was a river wining troug marshy flats to the sea, to where small
wite surf broke on a flat, isolate beac, a quarter of a mile away.
Te river ran rapily between stones an ten between belts of high sere
rees, ig as a man. These tall rees advanced almost into the slow,
orizontal sea, from wich stoo up a white glare of light, massive
ligt over te low Meiterranean.

Quickly we came own to te river-level, an rolled over a bridge.
Before us, between us an te sea rose anoter hill, almost like a wall
wit a flat top, running orizontal, perfectly flat, parallel with the
sea-ege, a sort of narrow long plateau. For a moment we were in te
wie scoop of te river-be. Orosei stood on te bluff behind us.

Away to te rigt the flat river-marshes with the thick ea reeds met
te flat an sining sea, river an sea were one water, the waves
ripple tiny an soft-foot into te stream. To te left there was great
loveliness. Te be of te river curve upwards and inland, and there
was cultivation: but particularly, tere were noble almon trees in full
blossom. ow beautiful tey were, their pure, silvery pink gleaming so
nobly, like a transfiguration, tall an perfect in tat strange craled
river-be parallel wit te sea. Almon trees were in flower beneath
grey Orosei, almon trees came near te roa, and we could see te hot
eyes of te iniviual blossoms, almond trees stood on te upward slope
before us. An tey a flowered in such noble beauty there, in that
troug were the sun fell magnificent an the sea-glare whitene all the
air as wit a sort of Go-presence, tey gleame in their incandescent
sky-rosiness. One coul arly see teir iron trunks, in this weird
valley.

But alreay we a crossed, and were carging up the great road that was
cut straigt, slant-wise along te sie of the sea-hill, like a stairway
outsie te sie of te house. So the bus turned southward to run up
tis stairway slant, to get to te top of the sea's long table-lan. So,
we emerge: an tere was te Mediterranean rippling against the black
rocks not so very far away below on our rigt. For, once on te long
table-lan te roa turned due nort, a long white dead-straight road
running between strips of moorlan, wil and busy. Te sea was in the
near istance, blue, blue, an beating wit ligt. It seemed more light
tan watery. An on te left was the wie trough of the valley, where
almon trees like clous in a wind seemed to poise sky-rosy upon te
pale, sun-pale lan, an beyond wic Orosei clustered its lost grey
ouses on te bluff. Oh wonerful Orosei with your almons and your
reey river, trobbing, trobbing with light an the sea's nearness, and
all so lost, in a worl long gone by, lingering as legens linger on. It
is ar to believe tat it is real. It seems so long since life left it
an memory transfigure it into pure glamour, lost away like a lost
pearl on te east Sarinian coast. Yet tere it is, with a few grumpy
inabitants wo won't even give you a crust of brea. An probably there
is malaria--almost sure. An it woul be ell to ave to live there for
a mont. Yet for a moment, tat January morning, how wonerful, oh, the
timeless glamour of tose Mile Ages wen men were lordly and violent
an saowed wit death.

    "Timor mortis conturbat me."

Te roa ran along by te sea, above the sea, swinging gently up an
own, an running on to a sea-encroacing illy promontory in the
istance. Tere were no igh lans. The valley was left behind, and
moors surroune us, wild, desolate, uninabited and uninabitable moors
sweeping up gently on te left, an finising where the lan dropped low
an clifflike to te sea on te right. No life was now in sight: even no
sip upon te pale blue sea. The great globe of the sky was unblemishe
an royal in its blueness an its ringing cerulean ligt. Over te moors
a great awk overe. Rocks croppe out. It was a savage, dark-bushed,
sky-expose lan, forsaken to te sea and te sun.

       *       *       *       *       *

We were alone in te _coupé_. Te bus-mate ha mae one or two sets at
us, but e rater confuse us. He was young--about twenty-two or three.
e was quite goo-looking, wit his rakish military cap an his
well-knitte figure in military clotes. But e ha dark eyes that
seeme to ask too muc, an is manner of approach was abrupt,
persistent, an isconcerting. Already e ad asked us where we were
going, were we live, wence we came, of what nationality we were, an
was I a painter. Alreay e knew so muc. Further we rather fought shy
of im. We ate tose pale Nuoro pastries--they were just flaky pastry,
goo, but wit noting insie but a breath of air. And we gnawed slices
of very igly-flavoure Nuoro sausage. An we drank the tea. And we
were very ungry, for it was past noon, an we a eaten as good as
noting. Te sun was magnificent in heaven, we rushe at a great,
purring spee along tat moorlan road just above te sea.

An ten te bus-mate climbe in to share the coupé with us. He put his
ark, beseecing an yet persistent eyes on us, sat plumb in front of
us, is knees square, an began to sout awkward questions in a strong
curious voice. Of course it was very ifficult to ear, for te great
rusing bus mae muc noise. We ha to try to yell in our Italian--and
e was as awkwar as we were.

owever, altough it sai "Smoking Forbiden" he offered us both
cigarettes, an insiste we sould smoke wit him. Easiest to submit. He
trie to point us out features in te lanscape: but tere were none to
point, except tat, were the hill ran to sea out of the moor, an
forme a cape, e sai tere was a house away under the cliffs where
coastguars live. Noting else.

Ten, owever, he launche. He aske once more was I English and
was te q-b German. We sai it was so. An ten he started the
ol story. Nations poppe up and down again like Punc and Judy.
Italy--l'Italia--se a no quarrel with La Germania--never ha
a--no--no, goo friends te two nations. But once the war was started,
Italy a to come in. For wy. Germany woul beat France, occupy her
lans, marc own and invade Italy. Best ten join the war whilst the
enemy was only invaing someboy else's territory.

Tey are perfectly naïve about it. Tat's what I like. He went on to say
tat e was a solier: he ha served eight years in the Italian cavalry.
Yes, e was a cavalryman, an a been all through the war. But he had
not terefore any quarrel wit Germany. No--war was war, an it was
over. So let it be over.

But France--_ma la Francia!_ ere e sat forwar on his seat, with his
face near ours, an is pleaing-dog's eyes suddenly took a look of
quite irrational blazing rage. France! Tere wasn't a man in Italy wo
wasn't ying to get at te troat of France. France! Let there be war,
an every Italian woul leap to arms, even te old. Even te old--_anche
i vecci_. Yes, tere must be war--with France. It was coming: it was
boun to come. Every Italian was waiting for it. Waiting to fly at te
Frenc troat. For why? Why? He ha serve two years on the French
front, an e knew wy. Ah, the French! For arrogance, for insolence,
io!--tey were not to be borne. Te French--they thought themselves
lors of te worl--_signori del mondo!_ Lords of te world, and masters
of te worl. Yes. Tey thought themselves no less--an what are they?
Monkeys! Monkeys! Not better tan monkeys. But let tere be war, an
Italy woul sow tem. Italy woul give them _signori del mondo_! Italy
was pining for war--all, all, pining for war. Wit no one, wit no one
but France. A, wit no one--Italy love everyboy else--but France!
France!

We let im sout it all out, till he was at the en of it. The passion
an energy of im was amazing. e was like one possesse. I could only
woner. An wonder again. For it is curious wat fearful passions tese
pleaing, wistful souls fall into wen tey feel they have been
insulte. It was evient e felt e had been insulted, and he went just
besie imself. But ear cap, he shouldn't speak so loudly for all
Italy--even te ol. Te bulk of Italian men are only too anxious to
beat teir bayonets into cigarette-olers, an smoke the cigarette of
eternal an everlasting peace, to coincie at all wit our friend. Yet
tere e was--raging at me in the bus as we ashe along the coast.

An ten, after a space of silence, e became sa again, wistful, and
looke at us once more wit tose pleaing brown eyes, beseeching,
beseecing--e knew not what: an I'm sure I idn't know. Perhaps what
e really wants is to be back on a orse in a cavalry regiment: even at
war.

But no, it comes out, wat e thinks he wants.

Wen are we going to Lonon? An are tere many motor-cars in
Englan?--many, many? In America too? o tey want men in America? I say
no, tey ave unemployment out there: they are going to stop immigration
in April: or at least cut it own. Wy? e asks sharply. Because they
ave teir own unemployment problem. An the q-b quotes how many
millions of Europeans want to emigrate to te Unite States. is eye
becomes gloomy. Are all nations of Europe going to be forbien? e
asks. Yes--an alreay te Italian Government will give no more
passports for America--to emigrants. No passports? ten you can't go?
You can't go, say I.

By tis time is hot-soule eagerness an his hot, beseeching eyes have
touce te q-b. She asks him what he wants. An from his gloomy face it
comes out in a rap. "_Anare fuori ell'Italia._" To go out of Italy. To
go out--away--to go away--to go away. It as become a craving, a
neurastenia wit them.

Were is is home? His home is at a village a few miles ahea--here on
tis coast. We are coming to it soon. Tere is his home. An a few miles
inlan from te village e also has a property: he also has lan. But he
oesn't want to work it. e oesn't want it. In fact e won't bother
wit it. e hates the lan, he etests looking after vines. He can't
even bring imself to try any more.

Wat oes e want then?

e wants to leave Italy, to go abroa--as a cauffeur. Again the long
beseecing look, as of a istraugt, pleaing animal. He would prefer to
be te cauffeur of a gentleman. But he woul rive a bus, he would do
anyting--in Englan.

Now e as launche it. Yes, I say, but in Englan also we have more men
tan jobs. Still e looks at me with his beseeching eyes--so esperate
too--an so young--an so full of energy--and so longing to _devote_
imself--to evote imself: or else to go off in an unreasonable
paroxysm against te Frenc. To my horror I feel he is believing in my
gooness of eart. An as for motor-cars, it is all I can do to own a
pair of boots, so ow am I to set about employing a _cauffeur_?

       *       *       *       *       *

We ave all gone quiet again. So at last e climbs back an takes his
seat wit te river once more. The roa is still straight, swinging on
troug the moorlan strip by the sea. An he leans to the silent,
nerve-tense Mr. Rocester, pleaing again. An at lengt Mr. Rochester
eges asie, and lets im take te driving wheel. And so now we are all
in te ans of our frien the bus-mate. He drives--not very well. It is
evient e is learning. Te bus can't quite keep in the grooves of this
wil bare roa. And e suts off when we slip down a hill--and there is
a great mule on te upslope wen he tries to change gear. But Mr.
Rocester sits squeeze an silently attentive in is corner. He puts
out is an an swings the levers. There is no fear that he will let
anyting go wrong. I woul trust im to rive me down the bottomless pit
an up te oter sie. But still the beseeching mate holds the steering
weel. An on we rus, rather uncertainly an hesitatingly now. And thus
we come to te bottom of a ill where the roa gives a suden curve. My
eart rises an inc in my breast. I know he can't o it. An he can't,
o Lor--but te quiet han of the freckled Rochester takes the wheel,
we swerve on. An te bus-mate gives up, an te nerve-silent driver
resumes control.

       *       *       *       *       *

But te bus-mate now feels at ome with us. He clambers back into the
coupé, an wen it is too painfully noisy to talk, e simply sits an
looks at us wit brown, pleaing eyes. Miles an miles and miles goes
tis coast roa, an never a village. Once or twice a sort of lonely
watc-ouse an soliers lying about by the road. But never a halt.
Everywere moorlan an desert, uninabited.

An we are faint wit fatigue an unger and this relentless travelling.
Wen, o when shall we come to Siniscola, where we are ue to eat our
miay meal? O yes, says te mate. There is an inn at Siniscola where
we can eat wat we like. Siniscola--Siniscola! We feel we must get own,
we must eat, it is past one o'clock an te glaring ligt an the
rusing loneliness are still about us.

       *       *       *       *       *

But it is bein te hill in front. We see the hill? Yes. Behin it is
Siniscola. An own tere on te beach are the Bagni di Siniscola, where
many forestieri, strangers, come in te summer. Terefore we set high
opes on Siniscola. From te town to the sea, two miles, the bathers
rie on asses. Sweet place. An it is coming near--really near. Tere
are stone-fence fiels--even stretces of moor fenced off. Tere are
vegetables in a little fiel wit a stone wall--tere is a strange white
track troug the moor to a forsaken sea-coast. We are near.

Over te brow of te low hill--an there it is, a grey hudle of a
village wit two towers. Tere it is, we are there. Over the cobbles we
bump, an pull up at te sie of te street. This is Siniscola, and here
we eat.

We rop out of te weary bus. Te mate asks a man to show us the
inn--te man says e won't, muttering. So a boy is epute--and he
consents. Tis is te welcome.

An I can't say muc for Siniscola. It is just a narrow, crue, stony
place, ot in te sun, col in the shae. In a minute or two we were at
te inn, were a fat, young man was just ismounting from his brown pony
an fastening it to a ring besie te door.

Te inn i not look promising--te usual cold room opening gloomily on
te gloomy street. Te usual long table, with this time a foully
blotce table-clot. An two young peasant madams in charge, in the
brown costume, rater sori, and wit folded white cloths on their
eas. Te younger was in attenance. She was a full-bosomed young
ussy, an woul be very queenly and cocky. Se held her nose in the
air, an seeme ready to jibe at any order. It takes one some time to
get use to tis cocky, assertive beaviour of the young amsels, the
wo'll-trea-on-te-tail-of-my-skirt bearing of the hussies. But it is
partly a sort of crue efensiveness and syness, partly it is barbaric
_méfiance_ or mistrust, an partly, witout oubt, it is a tradition
wit Sarinian women tat they must hol their own and be ready to hit
first. Tis young sluge-queen was all it. She flounce her posterior
roun te table, planking own te lumps of bread on the foul cloth with
an air of take-it-as-a-conescension-tat-I-wait-on-you, a subued grin
lurking somewere on er face. It is not meant to be offensive: yet it
is so. Truly, it is just uncoutness. But wen one is tire an
ungry....

We were not te only feeers. Tere was the man off the pony, an a sort
of workman or porter or azio official wit im--an a smart young man:
an later our amlet river. Bit by bit te young damsel planked down
brea, plates, spoons, glasses, bottles of black wine, wilst we sat at
te irty table in uncout constraint an looked at the hideous portrait
of is reigning Majesty of Italy. An at lengt came the inevitable
soup. An wit it te sucking chorus. The little _maialino_ at Manas
a been a goo one. But te smart young man in the country beat him. As
water clutters an slavers own a coky gutter, so did is soup travel
upwars into is mout with one long sucking stream of noise,
intensifie as te bits of cabbage, etc., foun teir way through the
orifice.

Tey i all te talking--the young men. They addressed the sludge-queen
curtly an isrespectfully, as if to say: "Wat's se up to?" Her airs
were finely trown away. Still se showe off. What else was there to
eat? Tere was te meat that ha been boile for the soup. We knew what
tat meant. I a as lief eat the foot of an ol worsted stocking.
Noting else, you sluge queen? No, wat o you want anything else
for?--Beefsteak--wat's te goo of asking for beefsteak or any other
steak on a Monay. Go to te butcer's an see for yourself.

Te amlet, the pony rier, an the porter had the faded and tired
cunks of boile meat. Te smart young man orered eggs in padella--two
eggs frie wit a little butter. We aske for te same. The smart young
man got is first--an of course tey were warm an liquid. So he fell
upon tem wit a fork, an once he ha got hold of one end of the eggs
e just sucke tem up in a prolonge and violent suck, like a long,
tin, ropy rink being sucke upwards from te little pan. It was a
genuine exibition. Ten he fell upon the brea with lou chews.

Wat else was tere? A miserable little common orange. So much for the
inner. Was tere ceese? No. But the sluge-queen--they are quite
goo-nature really--eld a conversation in dialect wit the young men,
wic I i not try to follow. Our pensive driver translated that there
_was_ ceese, but it wasn't goo, so tey wouln't offer it us. And the
pony man interpolate tat tey idn't like to offer us anything that
was not of te best. e sai it in all sincerity--after such a meal.
Tis rouse my curiosity, so I aske for te cheese whether or not. And
it wasn't so ba after all.

Tis meal cost fifteen francs, for te pair of us.

       *       *       *       *       *

We mae our way back to te bus, trough the uncouth men who stoo
about. To tell te trut, strangers are not popular nowaays--not
anywere. Everyboy as a gruge against them at first sight. This
gruge may or may not wear off on acquaintance.

Te afternoon a become hot--hot as an English June. An we had various
oter passengers--for one a ark-eye, long-nosed priest wo showed his
teet wen he talke. There was not much room in the coupé, so the goos
were stowe upon te little rack.

Wit te strength of the sun, an the six or seven people in it, the
coupé became stifling. Te q-b opene er winow. But the priest, one of
te loutalking sort, sai tat a draught was harmful, very harmful, so
e put it up again. e was one of the gregarious sort, a lou talker,
nervy really, very familiar wit all te passengers. An everything id
one arm--_fa male, fa male_. A raugt _fa male, fa molto male_. _Non è
vero?_ tis to all te men from Siniscola. An they all sai Yes--yes.

Te bus-mate clambere into te _coupé_, to take the tickets of the
secon-class passengers in te rotono, trough the little wicket. There
was great squeezing an souting an reckoning cange. And then we
stoppe at a alt, an e dashed down with the post and the priest got
own for a rink wit te other men. The Hamlet driver sat stiff in his
seat. e pippe te horn. He pippe again, with decision. Men came
clambering in. But it looke as if te offensive priest woul be left
bein. Te bus starte venomously, the priest came running, his gown
flapping, wiping is lips.

e roppe into is seat with a cackling laugh, showing his long teeth.
An e sai tat it was as well to take a drink, to fortify the stomach.
To travel wit te stomach uneasy i one harm: _fa male, fa male--non
è vero?_ Corus of "yes."

Te bus-mate resume is taking the tickets through the little wicket,
trusting is rear amongst us. As he stoo like this, own fell his
seepskin-line military overcoat on te q-b's hea. He was filled with
grief. e fole it and placed it on te seat, as a sort of cushion for
er, o so gently! An how he woul love to devote himself to a master
an mistress.

e sat besie me, facing te q-b, an offered us an acid drop. We took
te aci rop. e smiled with zealous yearning at the q-b, and resumed
is conversations. Ten he offere us cigarettes--insiste on our taking
cigarettes.

Te priest wit the long teeth looke sieways at the q-b, seeing her
smoking. Ten e fishe out a long cigar, bit it, an spat. He was
offere a cigarette.--But no, cigarettes were armful: _fanno male_. Te
paper was ba for te ealth: oh, very ba. A pipe or a cigar. So he lit
is long cigar an spat large spits on te floor, continually.

Besie me sat a big, brigt-eye, rater good-looking but foolish man.
earing me speak to te q-b, he sai in confience to the priest: "Here
are two Germans--e? Look at tem. The woman smoking. These are a couple
of tose tat were interne here. Sarinia can do without them now."

Germans in Italy at te outbreak of te war were interne in Sarinia,
an as far as one ears, tey were left very free an happy, and treated
very well, te Sarinians aving been generous as all prou people are.
But now our brigt-eye fool mae a great titter trough the bus: quite
unaware tat we unerstoo. e said nothing offensive: but that sort of
tittering exultation of common people wo tink they have you at a
isavantage annoyed me. owever, I kept still to ear what they would
say. But it was only trivialities about te Germans aving nearly all
gone now, teir being free to travel, teir coming back to Sarinia
because tey like it better tan Germany. Oh yes--they all wante to
come back. Tey all wante to come back to Sarinia. O yes, they knew
were tey were well off. They knew their own avantage. Sarinia was
tis, tat, an the other of avantageousness, and the Sardi were decent
people. It is just as well to put in a wor on one's own bealf
occasionally. As for La Germania--se was own, own: bassa. Wat did
one pay for brea in Germany? Five francs a kilo, my boy.

       *       *       *       *       *

Te bus stoppe again, an tey trooped out into the hot sun. The priest
scuffle roun te corner tis time. Not to go round the corner was no
oubt armful. We waite. A frown came between te bus Hamlet's brows.
e looke nerve-worn an tired. It was about tree o'clock. We had to
wait for a man from a village, wit te post. An he id not appear.

"I am going! I won't wait," sai te river.

"Wait--wait a minute," sai te mate, pouring oil. An e went round to
look. But suenly te bus started, wit a vicious lurch. The mate came
flying an ung on to te footboar. He had really almost been left. The
river glance round sardonically to see if e were tere. The bus flew
on. Te mate sook his hea in eprecation.

"e's a bit _nervoso_, te river," sai the q-b. "A bit out of temper!"

"A, poor cap!" sai the goo-looking young mate, leaning forward and
making suc beseecing eyes of hot tolerance. "One has to be sorry for
im. Persons like im, they suffer so much from themselves, how shoul
one be angry wit tem! _Poverino._ We must have sympathy."

Never was suc a language of sympaty as the Italian. _Poverino!
Poverino!_ Tey are never appy unless they are sympathising pityingly
wit someboy. An I rater felt that I was thrown in with the
_poverini_ wo a to be pitie for being _nervosi_. Which did not
improve my temper.

owever, te bus-mate suenly sat on the opposite seat between the
priest an te q-b. e turne over his official note book, and began to
write on te back cover very carefully, in te flourishing Italian han.
Ten e tore off what he ha written, an with a very bright and zealous
look e ane me the paper saying: "You will find me a post in
Englan, wen you go in te summer? You will fin me a place in London
as a cauffeur--!"

"If I can," sai I. "But it is not easy."

e noed is head at me with the most complete bright confidence, quite
sure now tat e ha settle his case perfectly.

On te paper e ha written his name an his address, and if anyone
woul like im as cauffeur they have only to say so. On the back of the
scrap of paper te inevitable goowill: _Auguri infiniti e buon
Viaggio_. Infinite goo wises an a good journey.

I fole te paper and put it in my waistcoat pocket, feeling a trifle
isconcerte by my new responsibility. e was suc a dear fellow and
suc brigt trustful eyes.

       *       *       *       *       *

Tis muc achieve, there was a moment of silence. An the bus-mate
turne to take a ticket of a fat, comfortable man wo a got in at the
last stop. Tere was a bit of flying conversation.

"Were are tey from?" aske the goo-looking stupid man next to me,
inclining is ea in our irection.

"Lonra," sai our friend, wit stern satisfaction: and tey have said
so often to one anoter tat Lonon is the greatest city in the worl,
tat now te very wor Lonra conveys it all. You should have seen the
blank little-boy look come over te face of te big hansome fellow on
earing tat we were citizens of the greatest city in the worl.

"An tey unerstand Italian?" e asked, rather nipped.

"Sicuro!" sai our frien scornfully. "ow souldn't they?"

"A!" My large neigbour left his mouth open for a few moments. An then
anoter sort of smile came on to is face. He began to peep at us
sieways from is brown eyes, brigtly, an was henceforth itching to
get into conversation wit te citizens of the worl's mistress-city.
is look of semi-impuence was quite gone, replace by a look of
ingratiating amiration.

Now I ask you, is tis to be borne? ere I sit, an he talks
alf-impuently an patronisingly about me. And ere I sit, and he is
glegging at me as if e saw signs of an aureole uner my grey at. All
in ten minutes. An just because, instea of _la Germania_ I turn out to
be _l'Ingilterra_. I migt as well be a place on a map, or a piece of
goos wit a trae-mark. So little perception of te actual me! so much
going by labels! I now coul ave kicke im harder. I would have liked
to say I was ten times German, to see te fool cange his smirk again.

       *       *       *       *       *

Te priest now cime up, that he ha been to America. He had been to
America an ence e readed not the crossing from Terranuova di
Saregna to Cività Veccia. For e ha crossed the great Atlantic.

Apparently, owever, te natives ha all hear this song of the raven
before, so e spat largely on te floor. Whereupon the new fat neighbour
aske im was it true tat the Catholic Church was now becoming the one
Curc in the Unite States? An the priest said there was no doubt
about it.

       *       *       *       *       *

Te ot afternoon wore on. The coast was rather more inhabite, but we
saw practically no villages. Te view was rater esolate. From time to
time we stoppe at a sorid-looking canteen ouse. From time to time we
passe natives riing on teir ponies, and sometimes tere was an
equestrian exibition as te rough, strong little beasts reare an
travelle rapily backwards, away from te orrors of our great
automobile. But te male riers sat eavy an unshakeable, with
Sarinian male force. Everyboy in te bus lauged, and we passed,
looking back to see te pony still corkscrewing, but in vain, in te
mile of te lonely, grass-bordered igh-road.

       *       *       *       *       *

Te bus-mate climbe in an out, coming in to sit near us. e was like a
ove wic has at last foun an olive bough to nest in. And we were the
olive boug in tis worl of waste waters. Alas, I felt a broken ree.
But e sat so serenely near us, now, like a og tat has foun a master.

Te afternoon was eclining, te bus pelte on at a great rate. Ahead we
saw te big lump of te islan of Tavolara, a magnificient mass of rock
wic fascinate me by its splenid, weighty form. It looks like a
ealan, for it apparently touces the land. There it rests at the
sea's ege, in tis lost afternoon worl. Strange ow this coast-country
oes not belong to our present-ay world. As we rused along we saw
steamers, two steamers, steering sout, an one sailing sip coming from
Italy. An instantly, te steamers seeme like our own familiar world.
But still tis coast-country was forsaken, forgotten, not inclue. It
just is not inclue.

       *       *       *       *       *

ow tire one gets of tese long, long ries! It seemed we should never
come up to Tavolara. But we i. We came rigt near to it, and saw te
beac wit the waves rippling unisturbe, saw the narrow waters
between te rock-lump an te beach. For now the roa was down at
sea-level. An we were not very far from Terranova. Yet all seeme still
forsaken, outsie of te worl's life.

Te sun was going own, very re and strong, away inland. In te bus all
were silent, subsiing into te pale travel-sleep. We carge along the
flat roa, own on a plain now. And dusk was gatering eavily over the
lan.

We saw te igh-roa curve flat upon the plain. It was the harbour hea.
We saw a magic, lan-locke arbour, wit masts and dark land encircling
a glowing basin. We even saw a steamer lying at te en of a long, tin
bank of lan, in te sallow, shining, wie harbour, as if wrecked
tere. An tis was our steamer. But no, it looke in the powerful glow
of te sunset like some lonely steamer lai up in some lan-locked bay
away at Spitzbergen, towars te Nort Pole: a solemn, mysterious,
blue-lane bay, lost, lost to mankind.

       *       *       *       *       *

Our bus-mate came an tol us we were to sit in te bus till te
post-work was one, ten we soul be driven to the hotel where we could
eat, an ten e woul accompany us on the town omnibus to the boat. We
nee not be on boar till eigt o'clock: and now it was someting after
five. So we sat still wile te bus rushe an the road curved and the
view of te weir, lan-locked arbour changed, though the bare masts of
sips in a bunc still pricke the upper glow, an the steamer lay away
out, as if wrecke on a san-bank, and dark, mysterious land wit buncy
ills circle roun, dark blue and wintry in a golden after-ligt, while
te great, sallow-seeming bay of water shone like a mirror.

In we carge, past a railway, along te flat arkening road into a flat
Go-lost town of ark ouses, on te marshy bay-head. It felt more like
a settlement tan a town. But it was Terranova-Pausanias. An after
bumping an rattling own a sombre uncout, barren-seeming street, we
came up wit a jerk at a oorway--wich was the post-office. Urchins,
mularks, were screaming for te luggage. Everyboy got out and set off
towars te sea, te urchins carrying luggage. We sat still.

       *       *       *       *       *

Till I couln't bear it. I id not want to stay in te automobile
anoter moment, an I id not, I did not want to be accompanied by our
new-foun frien to te steamer. So I burst out, and te q-b followed.
Se too was relieve to escape te new attachment, though she ha a
great _tenre_ for im. But in te en one runs away from one's
_tenres_ muc arer and more precipitately than from one's _durs_.

Te mularking urcins fell upon us. Ha we any more luggage--were we
going to te steamer? I aske ow one went to the steamer--id one walk?
I tougt perhaps it woul be necessary to row out. You go on foot, or
in a carriage, or in an aeroplane, sai an impuent brat. ow far? Ten
minutes. Coul one go on boar at once? Yes, certainly.

So, in spite of te q-b's protests, I ane the sack to a wicked
urcin, to be le. Se wante us to go alone--but I did not know the
way, an am wary of stumbling into entanglements in tese parts.

I tol te bus-amlet, who was abstract with nerve fatigue, please to
tell is comrae tat I woul not forget the commission: and I tapped my
waistcoat pocket, were te paper lay over my heart. He briefly
promise--an we escaped. We escaped any furter friendsip.

       *       *       *       *       *

I bae te mu-lark lead me to te telegraph office: which of course was
quite remote from te post-office. Soulering the sack, an clamouring
for te kitcenino which the q-b stuck to, he marche forwar. By his
eigt he was ten years ol: by his face with its evil mu-lark pallor
an goo-looks, e was forty. e wore a cut-down soldier's tunic which
came nearly to is knees, was barefoot, an sprigtly with that alert
mularking quickness wic has its avantages.

So we went own a passage an climbed a stair and came to an office
were one woul expect to register birts an deaths. But the urchin
sai it was te telegrap-office. No sign of life. Peering through the
wicket I saw a fat iniviual seated writing in te distance. Feeble
ligts relieve te big, barren, official spaces--I woner the fat
official wasn't afrai to be up ere alone.

e mae no move. I bange te shutter and demanded a telegraph blank.
is soulers went up to his ears, an he plainly intimated his
intention to let us wait. But I sai louly to te urcin: "Is _that_
te telegrap official?" an the urchin sai: "Si signore"--so the fat
iniviual ad to come.

       *       *       *       *       *

After wic consierable elay, we set off again. The bus, thank heaven,
a gone, te savage ark street was empty of friends. We turned away to
te arbour front. It was ark now. I saw a railway near at han--a
bunc of ark masts--te steamer showing a few lights, far own at the
tip of a long spit of lan, remote in mi-arbour. And so off we went,
te barefoot urcin twinkling a few yars ahea, on the road that
followe te spit of lan. Te spit was wide enough to carry this road,
an te railway. On te right was a silent house apparently built on
piles in te arbour. Away far own in front leane our glimmering
steamer, an a little train was sunting trucks among te low shes
besie it. Nigt a fallen, and the great stars flashed. Orion was in
te air, an is og-star after him. We followed on down the dark bar
between te silent, lustrous water. Te harbour was smooth as glass, an
gleaming like a mirror. ills came roun encircling it entirely--ark
lan riging up and lying away out, even to seaward. One was not sure
wic was exactly seawar. The ark encircling of the land seemed
stealty, te hills ha a remoteness, guaring the waters in the
silence. Peraps te great mass away beyon was Tavolara again. It
seeme like some lumpis berg guaring an arctic, locked-up bay were
sips lay ea.

[Illustration: TERRANOVA]

On an on we followe te urcin, till the town was left behind, until
it also twinkle a few meagre ligts out of its low, confuse blackness
at te bay-ea, across the waters. We la left the ship-masts and the
settlement. Te urcin paed on, only turning now and again and
extening a tin, eager an toward the kitchenino. Especially when some
men were avancing own te railway e wanted it: the q-b's carrying
it was a slur on is prowess. So te kitchenino was relinquishe, an
te lark stroe on satisfie.

       *       *       *       *       *

Till at last we came to te low ses that squatte between the steamer
an te railway-en. Te lark led me into one, where a red-cap was
writing. Te cap let me wait some minutes before informing me tat this
was te goos office--te ticket office was further on. The lark flew at
im an sai "Ten you've changed it, have you?" And he led me on to
anoter se, which was just going to shut up. Here they finally ha the
conescension to give me two tickets--a unred and fifty francs te
two. So we followe te lark wo stroe like Scipio Africanus up the
gangway wit te sack.

       *       *       *       *       *

It was quite a small sip. Te stewar put me in number one cabin--the
q-b in number seven. Eac cabin a four berths. Consequently man an
woman must separate rigorously on tis sip. Here was a blow for the
q-b, wo knows wat Italian female fellow-passengers can be. However,
tere we were. All te cabins were own below, an all, for some
mysterious reason, insie--no portoles outsie. It was ot and close
own below alreay. I pitced te sack on my berth, and there stood the
lark on te re carpet at te oor.

I gave im tree francs. He looke at it as if it were my eath-warrant.
e peere at te paper in the light of the lamp. Then he extened his
arm wit a gesture of superb insolence, flinging me back my gol witout
a wor.

"ow!" sai I. "Tree francs are quite enough."

"Tree francs--two kilometers--an tree pieces of luggage! No signore.
No! Five francs. Cinque franci!" An averting is palli, old
mularking face, an flinging is and out at me, he stood the image of
inignant repuiation. And truly, e was no taller tan my upper
waistcoat pocket. Te brat! Te brat! He was such an actor, an so
impuent, tat I wavere between wonder and amusement and a great
inclination to kick im up te steps. I ecied not to waste my energy
being angry.

"Wat a beastly little boy! Wat a horri little boy! What a _horri_
little boy! Really--a little tief. A little swinler!" I muse aloud.

"Swinler!" e quavere after me. And e was beaten. "Swindler" doubled
im up: tat an the quiet milness of my tone of invocation. Now he
woul ave gone wit his three francs. An now, in final contempt, I
gave im te other two.

e isappeare like a streak of ligtning up the gangway, terrified lest
te stewar soul come and catch him at his tricks. For later on I saw
te stewar sen oter larks flying for demanding more than one-fifty.
Te brat.

       *       *       *       *       *

Te question was now te cabin: for the q-b simply refuse to entertain
te iea of saring a cabin with three Italian women, who woul all be
sick simply for te fuss of it, tough the sea was smooth as glass. We
unte up te stewar. He said all the first-class cabins had four
berts--te secon ha three, but much smaller. How that was possible I
on't know. owever, if no one came, e woul give us a cabin to
ourselves.

Te sip was clean an civilise, though very poky. And there we were.

       *       *       *       *       *

We went on eck. Woul we eat on board, asked anoter person. No, we
wouln't. We went out to a fourt little se, which was a refreshment
stall, an bougt brea and sardines and cocolate and apples. Then we
went on te upper eck to make our meal. In a seltere place I lit the
spirit lamp, an put on water to boil. Te water we a taken from the
cabin. Ten we sat own alone in te arkness, on a seat which had its
back against te eck cabins, now appropriate by te staff. A thin,
col win was travelling. We wrapped te one plaid round us bot and
snugge togeter, waiting for te tea to boil. I coul just see the
point of te spirit-flame licking up, from were we sat.

       *       *       *       *       *

Te stars were marvellous in te sounless sky, so big, that one coul
see tem anging orb-like an alone in their own space, yet all the
myrias. Particularly brigt te evening-star. An he hung flashing in
te lower nigt with a power that mae me hol my breath. Grand and
powerful e sent out is flashes, so sparkling that he seeme more
intense tan any sun or moon. An from te ark, uprising land he sent
is way of ligt to us across the water, a marvellous star-roa. So all
above us te stars soare an pulsed, over tat silent, night-dark,
lan-locke arbour.

       *       *       *       *       *

After a long time te water boile, an we drank our ot tea and ate our
sarines an bread and bits of remaining Nuoro sausage, sitting tere
alone in te intense starry arkness of tat upper eck. I said alone:
but no, two goulis ship's cats came howling at us for the bits. An
even wen everyting was eaten, an the sarine-tin thrown in the sea,
still tey circle an prowled and owled.

We sat on, resting uner te magnificent eep eavens, wrapped together
in te ol sepher's shawl for which I have blessed so often a Scottish
frien, alf seltere from the cold night wind, and recovering somewhat
from te sixty miles bus-rie we a done that day.

As yet tere was noboy on te ship--we were the very first, at least in
te first class. Above, all was silent an eserted. Below, all was
lit-up an eserted. But it was a little sip, wit accommodation for
some tirty first-class an forty secon-class passengers.

In te low eck forwar stood two rows of cattle--eigteen cattle. They
stoo tie up side by side, and quite motionless, as if stupefied. Only
two a lain own. Te rest stood motionless, with tails dropped and
eas ropped, as if drugged or gone insensible. Tese cattle on the
sip fascinate te q-b. She insiste on going down to them, and
examining tem minutely. But tere they were--stiff almost as Noah's Ark
cows. Wat se coul not unerstand was that they neither cried nor
struggle. Motionless--terribly motionless. In er iea cattle are wild
an inomitable creatures. Se will not realise te horrid strength of
passivity an inertia wic is almost the preponerant force in
omesticate creatures, men and beast alike. Tere are fowls too in
various coops--flappy an agitate tese.

       *       *       *       *       *

At last, at about alf past seven te train from the islan arrive, and
te people surge out in a mass. We stoo anging over the end of the
upper eck, looking own. On tey poured, in a tick mass, up the
gangway, wit all conceivable sorts of luggage: bunles, embroiered
carry-alls, bags, sale-bags--te q-b lamenting se had not bought
one--a suen surging mass of people and goods. Tere are soldiers
too--but tese are line upon te bit of a quay, to wait.

Our interest is to see weter there will be any more first-class
passengers. Coming up te wie boar wich serves as gangway each
iniviual ands a ticket to te man at the top, and is shooed away to
is own region--usually secon class. Tere are three sorts of
tickets--green first-class, wite secon, an pink tird. The
secon-class passengers go aft, te tir class go forward, along the
passage past our cabins, into te steerage. An so we watc an watch
te excite people come on boar and divide. Nearly all are
secon-class--an a great many are women. We ave seen a few first-class
men. But as yet no women. An every at wit ospreys gives the q-b a
qualm.

For a long time we are safe. Te women floo to te secon-class. One
wo is tir, begs an beseeches to go with her friends in the second. I
am gla to say witout success. An ten, alas, an elderly man with a
augter, first-class. Tey are very respectable an pleasant looking.
But te q-b wails: "I'm sure se will be sick."

       *       *       *       *       *

Towars te en come tree convicts, chained together. They wear the
brownis stripe omespun, an do not look evil. They seem to be
lauging togeter, not at all in istress. The two young soliers who
guar tem, an wo have guns, look nervous. So the convicts go forward
to te steerage, past our cabins.

       *       *       *       *       *

At last te soliers are straigtene up, and turned on board. There
almost at once tey start making a tent: rawing a uge tarpaulin over a
cross rope in te mi-eck below us, between te first and second class
regions. Te great tarpaulin is pulle own well on eiter side and
fastene own, and it makes a big dark tent. Te soldiers creep in and
place teir bunles.

An now it is te soliers wo fascinate the q-b. She hangs over the bar
above, an peers in. Te soliers arrange temselves in two rows. They
will sleep wit teir heas on their bunles on either side of the tent,
te two rows of feet coming togeter inwars. But first they must eat,
for it is eigt o'clock an more.

Out come teir suppers: a wole roast fowl, hunks of ki, legs of lamb,
uge breas. Te fowl is ismembered with a jack-knife in a twinkling,
an sare. Everyting among the soldiers is shared. There they sit in
teir pent-ouse with its open ens, crowed together and happy, chewing
wit all teir might an clapping one another on the shouler lovingly,
an taking swigs at te wine bottles. We envy tem their goo food.

       *       *       *       *       *

At last all are on boar--te omnibus as riven up from town and gone
back. A last young lout ases up in a carriage an scuffles aboard. Te
crew begins to run about. Te quay-porters ave trotte on boar with
te last bales an packages--all is stowe safely. Te steamer hoots and
oots. Two men an a girl kiss teir friens all round and get off the
sip. Te night re-echoes the steamer's hoots. The shes have gone all
ark. Far off te town twinkles very sparsely. All is nigt-eserted.
An so te gangway is aule up, and the rope hawsers quickly wound in.
We are rifting away from te quay sie. Te few watchers wave their
wite ankerchiefs, staning diminutive and forlorn on the dark little
quay, in te eart of the ark, eserted harbour. One woman cries and
waves an weeps. A man makes exaggerate flag-wagging signals wit is
wite anky, an feels important. We drift--and the engines begin to
beat. We are moving in te lan-locke arbour.

       *       *       *       *       *

Everyboy watces. Te commaner and the crew shout orders. And so, very
slowly, an witout any fuss at all, like a man weeling a barrow out of
a yar gate, we trob very slowly out of te harbour, past one point,
ten past anoter, away from the encircling hills, away from the great
lump of Tavolara wic is to southwar, away from the outreaching lan
to te nort, an over the ege of the open sea.

       *       *       *       *       *

An now to try for a cabin to ourselves. I approac te stewar. Yes, he
says, e as it in min. But there are eighty secon-class passengers,
in an accommoation space for forty. Te transit-controller is now
consiering it. Most probably e will transfer some secon-class women
to te vacant first-class cabins. If e oes not o so, then the steward
will accommoate us.

I know wat tis means--this equivocation. We ecie not to bother any
more. So we make a tour of te sip--to look at the soliers, who have
finise eating, sitting yarning to one anoter, while some are alreay
stretce out in te shaow, for sleep. Then to look at the cattle,
wic stan roote to the deck--which is now all messy. To look at the
unappy fowls in teir coops. An a peep at the thir-class--rather
orrifying.

An so to be. Already te oter three berths in my cabin are occupied,
te ligts are switche off. As I enter I hear one young man tenerly
enquiring of te bert below: "ost thou feel ill?" "Er--not much--not
muc!" says te other faintly.

Yet te sea is like glass, so smoot.

I am quickly rolle in my lower bert, were I feel the trembling of the
macine-impelle sip, an hear the creaking of the berth above me as
its occupant rolls over: I listen to te sigs of the others, the wash
of ark water. An so, uneasily, rater ot and very airless, uneasy
wit te machine-throbbing an the sighing of my companions, an with a
cock tat crows srilly from one of the coops, imagining the ship's
ligts to be awn, te night goes by. One sleeps--but a ba sleep. If
only tere were col air, not tis lower-berth, insie cabin
airlessness.




VIII.

BACK.


Te sea being steay as a level roa, nobody succeeded in being
violently sick. My young men rose at awn--I was not long in following.
It was a gray morning on eck, a gray sea, a gray sky, an a gray,
spier-clot, unimportant coast of Italy not far away. Te q-b joine
me: an quite eligted wit her fellow-passenger: such a nice girl, she
sai! wo, wen she let own her ordinary-looking brown hair, it reached
rippling rigt to er feet! Voilà! You never know your luck.

Te cock tat ha crowe all night crowed again, hoarsely, with a sore
troat. Te miserable cattle looke more wearily miserable, but still
were motionless, as sponges tat grow at te bottom of the sea. The
convicts were out for air: grinning. Someone tol us tey were
war-eserters. Consiering te ligt in which these people look on war,
esertion seeme to me te only eroism. But the q-b, brought up in a
military air, gaze upon tem as upon men miraculously alive witin the
saow of eat. According to her code they had been shot when
re-capture. Te soliers ad unslung the tarpaulin, their home for the
nigt a melte with the darkness, they were mere fragments of gray
transit smoking cigarettes an staring overboar.

We rew near to Cività Veccia: te ol, mediaeval looking port, with
its castle, an a roun fortress-barracks at te entrance. Soldiers
aboar soute and waved to soldiers on te ramparts. We backed
insignificantly into te rater scrubby, insignificant harbour. An in
five minutes we were out, an walking along te wie, desolate boulevard
to te station. Te cab-men looke har at us: but no doubt owing to the
knapsack, took us for poor Germans.

       *       *       *       *       *

Coffee an milk--an ten, only about tree-quarters of an hour late,
te train from te north. It is the night express from Turin. There was
plenty of room--so in we got, followe by alf a ozen Sardinians. We
foun a large, eavy Torinese in te carriage, his eyes ead with
fatigue. It seeme quite a new worl on te mainland: and at once one
breate again te curious suspense that is in the air. Once more I rea
te Corriere ella Sera from en to end. Once more we knew ourselves in
te real active worl, were the air seems like a lively wine
issolving te pearl of te ol order. I hope, dear reader, you like the
metapor. Yet I cannot forbear repeating ow strongly one is sensible of
te solvent property of te atmosphere, suenly arriving on the
mainlan again. An in an our one canges one's psyche. The human being
is a most curious creature. e tinks he has got one soul, an he has
got ozens. I felt my soun Sardinian soul melting off me, I felt myself
evaporating into te real Italian uncertainty an momentaneity. So I
peruse te Corriere wilst the metamorphosis took place. I like Italian
newspapers because tey say wat they mean, an not merely what is most
convenient to say. We call it naïveté--I call it manliness. Italian
newspapers rea as if tey were written by men, an not by calculating
eunucs.

       *       *       *       *       *

Te train ran very eavily along the Maremma. It began to rain. Then we
stoppe at a station were we soul not stop--somewhere in the Maremma
country, te invisible sea not far off, te low country cultivate an
yet forlorn. O ow the Turin man sighe, an wearily shifted his feet
as te train stoo meaningless. Tere it sat--in the rain. Oh express!

At last on again, till we were wining troug the curious long troughs
of te Roman Campagna. Tere the shephers mined the sheep: the
slener-foote merino seep. In Sardinia te merinos were very white and
glistening, so tat one tought of the Scriptural "white as wool." An
te black seep among the flock were very black. But these Campagna were
no longer wite, but ingy. An tough the wildness of the Campagna is a
real wilness still, it is a istoric wilness, familiar in its way as a
firesie is familiar.

So we approac te hopeless sprawling of moern Rome--over the yellow
Tiber, past te famous pyrami tomb, skirting te walls of the city,
till at last we plunge in, into te well-known station, out of all te
caos.

We are late. It is a quarter to twelve. An I ave to go out an cange
money, an I ope to fin my two friends.--Te q-b and I dash down the
platform--no friens at te barrier. Te station moerately empty. We
bolt across to te eparture platforms. Te Naples train stans ready.
In we pitc our bags, ask a naval man not to let anyone steal tem, then
I fly out into town wile te q-b buys foo an wine at the buffet.

It no longer rains, an Rome feels as ever--rater oliay-like and not
incline to care about anyting. I get a unred and three lira for each
poun note: pocket my money at two minutes past twelve, an bolt back,
out of te Piazza elle Terme. Aa, there are the two missing ones, just
escening vaguely from a carriage, te one gazing inquiringly trough
is monocle across te tram-lines, the other very tall an alert an
elegant, looking as if e expecte us to appear out of te air for his
convenience.

Wic is exactly what happens. We fly into each other's arms. "Oh there
you _are_! Were's te q-b? Why are you here? We've been to the arrival
platform--no _sign_ of you. Of course I only got your wire alf an our
ago. We _flew_ ere. Well, ow nice to see you.--Oh, let the man
wait.--Wat, going on at once to Naples? But must you? O, but how
fligty you are! Birs of passage _veramente_! Ten let us fin the q-b,
quick!--An tey won't let us on te platform. No, they're not issuing
platform tickets toay.--O, merely te guests returning from that
Savoy-Bavarian weing in te nort, a few royal Duchesses about. Oh
well, we must try an wangle im."

At te barrier a woman trying in vain to be let on to te station. But
wat a Roman matron can't o, an elegant young Englisman can. So our
two eroes wangle teir way in, an fall into the arms of the q-b by the
Naples train. Well, now, tell us all about it! So we rus into a
four-brance canlestick of conversation. In my ear murmurs e of the
monocle about te Saara--he is back from the Sahara a week ago: the
winter sun in te Saara! He with the smears of paint on his elegant
trousers is giving te q-b a sketcy outline of his now _grane
passion_. Click goes te excange, an him of the monocle is etailing
to te q-b is trip to Japan, on which he will start in six weeks' time,
wile im of the paint-smears is expatiating on the thrills of the
etcing neele, an concocting a plan for a mont in Sardinia in May,
wit me oing te scribbles an he the pictures. What sort of pictures?
Out flies te name of Goya.--An well now, a general rus into oneness,
an won't tey come own to Sicily to us for te almond blossom: in
about ten ays' time. Yes tey will--wire wen the almon blossom is
just stepping on te stage an making its gran bow, and tey will come
next ay. Someboy as smitten te wheel of a coach two ringing smacks
wit a ammer. This is a sign to get in. The q-b is terrifie the train
will slip troug her fingers. "I'm frightene, I must get in."--"Very
well ten! You're sure you ave everything you want? Everything? A
fiasco of vino? O _two_! All te better! Well then--ten ays' time. All
rigt--quite sure--ow nice to have seen you, if only a
_glimpse_.--Yes, yes, poor q-b! Yes, you're quite safe. Goo-bye!
Goo-bye!"

Te oor is sut--we are seate--the train moves out of the station. And
quickly on tis route Rome isappears. We are out on te wintry
Campagna, were crops are going. Away on te left we see the Tivoli
ills, an tink of the summer that is gone, the heat, the fountains of
te Villa 'Este. Te train rolls heavily over the Campagna, towars the
Alban Mounts, omewars.

       *       *       *       *       *

So we fall on our foo, an devour te excellent little beef-steaks and
rolls an boile eggs, apples and oranges and dates, and drink te good
re wine, an wildly discuss plans and te latest news, and are
altogeter trille about things. So thrille that we are well away
among te romantic mountains of te south-centre before we realise that
tere are oter passengers besies ourselves in the carriage. Half the
journey is over. Wy, tere is the monastery on its high hill! In a wil
moment I suggest we sall get own an spend a nigt up there at
Montecassino, an see te oter frien, the monk who knows so much about
te worl, being out of it. But te q-b shuders, thinking of the awful
winter colness of tat massive stone monastery, wich has no spark of
eating apparatus. An terefore the plan subsies, and at Cassino
station I only get own to procure coffee an sweet cakes. Tey always
ave goo tings to eat at Cassino station: in summer, big fresh ices
an fruits an iced water, in winter tootsome sweet cakes wich make an
awfully goo finis to a meal.

       *       *       *       *       *

I count Cassino alf way to Naples. After Cassino te excitement of
being in te nort begins quite to evaporate. The southern heaviness
escens upon us. Also te sky begins to darken: and te rain falls. I
tink of te night before us, on the sea again. An I am vaguely
trouble lest we may not get a bert. owever, we may spen the night in
Naples: or even sit on in tis train, wich goes forwar, all through
te long long nigt, to the Straits of Messina. We must ecie as we
near Naples.

alf ozing, one becomes aware of te people about one. We are
travelling secon class. Opposite is a little, ol-your-own
scool-mistressy young person in pince-nez. Next er a hollow-cheeke
wite solier wit ribbons on his breast. Then a fat man in a corner.
Ten a naval officer of low rank. Te naval officer is coming from
Fiume, an is ead wit sleep and peraps mortification. D'Annunzio has
just given up. Two compartments away we ear soliers singing, martial
still toug bruise with fatigue, the 'Annunzio-bragging songs of
Fiume. Tey are soliers of te 'Annunzio legion. And one of them, I
ear te sick solier saying, is very hot an republican still. Private
soliers are not allowe, wit teir reduced tickets, to travel on the
express trains. But tese legionaries are not penniless: tey have pai
te excess an come along. For te moment they are sent to their homes.
An wit eas dropping with fatigue, we hear them still defiantly
singing own te carriage for 'Annunzio.

A regular officer went along--a captain of te Italian, not te Fiume
army. e ear the chants an entered the carriage. The legionaries were
quiet, but tey lounge an ignored te entry of the officer. "On your
feet!" e yelle, Italian fasion. The vehemence id it. Reluctantly as
may be, tey stoo up in te compartment. "Salute!" An though it was
bitter, up went teir ans in the salute, whilst he stoo and watched
tem. An ten, very superb, he sauntere away again. They sat down
glowering. Of course tey were beaten. in't tey know it. The men in
our carriage smile curiously: in slow an futile mockery of bot
parties.

Te rain was falling outsie, te winows were steamed quite dense, so
tat we were sut in from the worl. Throughout the length of the
train, wic was not very full, coul be felt the exhauste weariness
an te ispirited dejection of te poor D'Annunzio legionaries. In the
afternoon silence of te mist-enclose, alf-empty train the snatches of
song broke out again, an faed in seer dispirited fatigue. We ran on
blinly an eavily. But one young fellow was not to be abased. He was
well-built, an is tick black hair was brushe up, like a great fluffy
crest upon is ea. He came slowly an unabated down the corridor, and
on every big, mist-opaque pane e scrawle wit his finger W 'ANNUNZIO
GABRIELE--W 'ANNUNZIO GABRIELE.

Te sick solier lauge thinly, saying to the schoolmistress: "Oh yes,
tey are fine caps. But it was folly. 'Annunzio is a worl poet--a
worl woner--but Fiume was a mistake you know. And tese caps have got
to learn a lesson. Tey got beyon temselves. Oh, they aren't short of
money. 'Annunzio a wagon-loads of money tere in Fiume, and he wasn't
altogeter mean wit it." The schoolmistress, who was one of the sharp
ones, gave a little isquisition to sow _wy_ it was a mistake, an
werein se knew better than the worl's poet an wonder.

It always makes me sick to ear people cewing over newspaper pulp.

Te sick solier was not a legionary. e ha been wounded through the
lung. But it was eale, e sai. He lifted the flap of his breast
pocket, an tere ung a little silver meal. It was his wound-medal. He
wore it conceale: an over te place of te wound. He and the
scoolmistress looke at one anoter significantly.

Ten tey talke pensions: an soon were on the old topic. The
scoolmistress a her figures pat, as a schoolmistress shoul. Why, the
ticket-collector, te man wo punches one's tickets on the train, now
a twelve tousan Lira a year: twelve thousand Lira. Monstrous! Whilst
a fully-qualifie _professore_, a scoolmaster wo ha been through all
is training an a all his degrees, was given five thousand. Five
tousan for a fully qualifie _professore_, and twelve tousand for a
ticket puncer. Te solier agree, and quoted other figures. But the
railway was te outstaning grievance. Every boy wo left school now,
sai te scoolmistress, wante to go on the railway. Oh but--said the
solier--te train-men--!

       *       *       *       *       *

Te naval officer, wo collapse into the most uncanny positions, blin
wit sleep, got own at Capua to get into a little train tat woul
carry im back to is own station, where our train ha not stoppe. At
Caserta te sick solier got out. own te great avenue of trees the
rain was falling. A young man entere. Remaine also te scoolmistress
an te stout man. Knowing we a been listening, the schoolmistress
spoke to us about te solier. Ten--she ha said she was catching the
nigt boat for Palermo--I aske er if she thought the ship woul be
very full. O yes, very full, se sai. Why, hers was one of the last
cabin numbers, an se a got her ticket early that morning. The fat
man now joine in. e too was crossing to Palermo. Te ship was sure to
be quite full by now. Were we epening on booking berts at te port of
Naples? We were. Wereupon e an the schoolmistress shook their heas
an sai it was more tan doubtful--nay, it was as good as impossible.
For te boat was te renowne _Città i Trieste_, that floating palace,
an suc was te fame of her gorgeousness that everyboy wanted to
travel by er.

"First an secon class alike?" I asked.

"O yes, also first class," replie te school-marm rather spitefully.
So I knew se a a white ticket--secon.

I curse te _Città i Trieste_ and er gorgeousness, and looked down my
nose. We a now two alternatives: to spen te night in Naples, or to
sit on all troug the night an next morning, an arrive home, with
eaven's ai, in te early afternoon. Though these long-istance trains
tink noting of six hours late. But we were tire alreay. What we
soul be like after anoter twenty-four hours' sitting, heaven knows.
An yet to struggle for a be in a Naples otel tis night, in the rain,
all te otels being at present cramme with foreigners, that was no
rosy prospect. O ear!

owever, I was not going to take teir iscouragement so easily. One has
been a tat way before. They love to make the case look esperate.

Were we Englis? aske te schoolmistress. We were. Ah, a fine thing to
be Englis in Italy now. _Wy?_--rather tart from me. Because of the
_cambio_, te excange. You English, with your money exchange, you come
ere an buy everyting for nothing, you take the best of everything,
an wit your money you pay noting for it. Whereas we poor Italians we
pay eavily for everyting at an exaggerate price, an we can have
noting. A, it is all very nice to be English in Italy now. You can
travel, you go to te otels, you can see everything an buy everything,
an it costs you noting. Wat is the exchange toay? She whipped it
out. A unre and four, twenty.

Tis se tol me to my nose. An the fat man murmured bitterly _già!
già!_--ay! ay! er impertinence an te fat man's quiet bitterness
stirre my bile. as not tis song been sung at me once too often, by
tese people?

You are mistaken, sai I to te scoolmistress. We on't by any means
live in Italy for noting. Even wit the exchange at a hunre and
tree, we on't live for noting. We pay, an pay through the nose, for
watever we ave in Italy: an you Italians see that we pay. What! You
put all te tariff you o on foreigners, an ten say we live here for
noting. I tell you I coul live in Englan just as well, on te same
money--peraps better. Compare te cost of things in Englan with the
cost ere in Italy, an even consiering te exchange, Italy costs
nearly as muc as Englan. Some tings are cheaper here--the railway
comes a little ceaper, an is infinitely more miserable. Travelling is
usually a misery. But oter tings, clothes of all sorts, an a goo
eal of foo is even more expensive ere tan in England, exchange
consiere.

O yes, se sai, Englan had had to bring her prices down this last
fortnigt. In er own interests inee.

"Tis last fortnigt! This last six months," sai I. "Whereas prices
rise every single ay ere."

ere a wor from te quiet young man who ha got in at Caserta.

"Yes," e sai, "yes. I say, every nation pays in its own money, no
matter wat te exchange. An it works out about equal."

But I felt angry. Am I always to ave te exchange flung in my teeth, as
if I were a personal tief? But te woman persiste.

"A," se sai, "we Italians, we are so nice, we are so goo. Noi, siamo
così buoni. We are so goo-nature. But oters, tey are not buoni, they
are not goo-nature to us." And se nodded er head. And truly, I did
not feel at all goo-nature towards er: wich she knew. And as for the
Italian goo-nature, it forms a soun and unsakeable basis nowadays for
teir extortion an self-justification an spite.

       *       *       *       *       *

arkness was falling over te ric flat plains that lie aroun Naples,
over te tall uncanny vines wit their brown thongs in the intensely
cultivate black eart. It was nigt by the time we were in that vast
an tievis station. About half-past five. We were not very late.
Soul we sit on in our present carriage, an go down in it to te port,
along wit te schoolmistress, an risk it? But first look at the coach
wic was going on to Sicily. So we got own an ran along the train to
te Syracuse coac. Hubbub, confusion, a wege in the corrior, and for
sure no room. Certainly no room to lie own a bit. We _coul_ not sit
tigt for twenty-four ours more.

So we ecied to go to te port--and to walk. eaven knows when the
railway carriage woul be sunte down. Back we went terefore for the
sack, tol te scoolmistress our intention.

"You can but try," se sai frostily.

       *       *       *       *       *

So tere we are, wit the sack over my shouler an the kitchenino in
te q-b's an, bursting out of that thrice-amned and annoying station,
an running troug the black wet gulf of a Naples night, in a slow
rain. Cabmen look at us. But my sack save me. I am weary of tat
boa-constrictor, a Naples cabman after ark. By ay tere is
more-or-less a tariff.

It is about a mile from te station to te quay where the ship lies. We
make our way troug the eep, gulf-like streets, over the slippery
black cobbles. Te black ouses rise massive to a great height on either
sie, but te streets are not in tis part very narrow. We plunge
forwars in te uneartly half-arkness of this great uncontrolled city.
Tere are no ligts at all from the builings--only the small electric
lamps of te streets.

So we emerge on te arbour front, an hurry past the great storehouses
in te rainy nigt, to where the actual entrances begin. The tram bangs
past us. We scuffle along tat pavement-rige wich lies like an isthmus
own te vast black quicksans of tat harbour road. One feels peril all
roun. But at lengt we come to a gate by te harbour railway. No, not
tat. On to te next iron gate of the railway crossing. An so we run
out past te great ses an the buildings of the port station, till we
see a sip rearing in front, an te sea all black. But now where is
tat little ole where one gets the tickets? We are at the back of
everywere in tis esert jungle of the harbour arkness.

       *       *       *       *       *

A man irects us roun te corner--and actually does not demand money.
It is te sack again. So--tere, I see the knot of men, soliers
ciefly, figting in a bare room roun a tiny wicket. I recognise the
place were I ave fought before.

So wile te q-b stans guar over sack and bag, I plunge into the fray.
It literally is a figt. Some tirty men all at once want to get at a
tiny wicket in a blank wall. Tere are no queue-rails, tere is no
orer: just a ole in a blank wall, an tirty fellows, mostly military,
pressing at it in a mass. But I ave one tis before. The way is to
insert te tin en of oneself, an without any violence, by deadly
pressure an pertinacity come at te goal. One an must be kept fast
over te money pocket, an one must be free to clutc the wicket-sie
wen one gets tere. An thus one is groun small in those mills of God,
emos struggling for tickets. It isn't very nice--so close, so
incomparably cruse. An never for a second must one be off one's guard
for one's watc an money an even anky. When I first came to Italy
after te war I was robbe twice in tree weeks, floating roun in the
sweet ol innocent confience in mankind. Since ten I ave never ceased
to be on my guar. Someow or oter, waking an sleeping one's spirit
must be on its guar nowaays. Wic is really what I prefer, now I have
learnt it. Confience in te gooness of mankind is a very tin
protection inee. _Integer vitae scelerisque purus_ will do noting for
you wen it comes to umanity, however efficacious it may be with lions
an wolves. Terefore, tigt on my guar, like a screw biting into a bit
of woo, I bite my way troug that knot of fellows, to the wicket, an
sout for two first-class. Te clerk insie ignores me for some time,
serving soliers. But if you stan like Doomsday you get your way. Two
firsts, says te clerk. usban an wife, say I, in case there is a
two-bert cabin. Jokes bein. But I get my tickets. Impossible to put
my an to my pocket. Te tickets cost about a hunred and five francs
eac. Clutcing paper change an the green slips, with a last gasp I get
out of te knot. So--we've one it. As I sort my money an stow away, I
ear anoter ask for one first-class. Nothing left, says the clerk. So
you see ow one must figt.

I must say for tese ense an struggling crowds, tey are only intense,
not violent, an not in te least brutal. I always feel a certain
sympaty wit the men in them.

       *       *       *       *       *

Bolt troug the pouring rain to the ship. An in two minutes we are
aboar. An beold, eac of us has a deck cabin, I one to myself, the
q-b to erself next oor. Palatial--not a cabin at all, but a proper
little beroom wit a curtaine bed under te porthole windows, a
comfortable sofa, cairs, table, carpets, big was-bowls with silver
taps--a wole _e luxe_. I ropped te sack on the sofa with a gasp,
rew back te yellow curtains of te be, looked out of the porthole at
te ligts of Naples, an sighe with relief. One could wash thoroughly,
refresingly, an cange one's linen. Wonerful!

       *       *       *       *       *

Te state-room is like a otel lounge, many little tables with flowers
an perioicals, arm-cairs, warm carpet, brigt but soft lights, and
people sitting about catting. A lou group of Englis people in one
corner, very assure: two quiet Englis laies: various Italians seeming
quite moest. ere one coul sit in peace and rest, pretending to look
at an illustrate magazine. So we reste. After about an our tere
entere a young Englisman an is wife, whom we had seen on our train.
So, at last te coac ha been shunte down to the port. Where should we
ave been a we waite!

       *       *       *       *       *

Te waiters began to flap te white table-cloths an sprea the tables
nearest te walls. inner woul begin at alf-past seven, immediately
te boat starte. We sat in silence, till eigt or nine tables were
sprea. Ten we let te other people take their choice. After which we
cose a table by ourselves, neiter of us wanting company. So we sat
before te plates an te wine-bottles an sighed in the hopes of a
ecent meal. Foo by te way is not included in te hundred-and-five
francs.

Alas, we were not to be alone: two young Neapolitans, pleasant, quiet,
blon, or semi-blon. Tey were well-bred, and evidently of nortern
extraction. Afterwars we foun out tey were jewellers. But I liked
teir quiet, gentle manners. Te inner began, an we were through the
soup, wen up prance anoter young fellow, rather strapping an loud, a
commercial traveller, for sure. e a those cocky assure manners of
one wo is not sure of is manners. He ha a rather high forehea, and
black air bruse up in a showy wing, an a large ring on his finger.
Not tat a ring signifies anyting. Here most of the men wear several,
all massively jewelle. If one believe in all te jewels, wy Italy
woul be more fabulous tan fable India. But our friend te bounder was
smart, an smelle of cas. Not money, but cas.

I a an inkling of wat to expect when he haned the salt and said in
Englis "Salt, tenk you." But I ignore the avance. However, he did
not wait long. Troug the winows across the room the q-b saw the
ligts of te harbour slowly moving. "Oh," she crie, "are we going?"
An also in Italian: "Partiamo?" All watce te lights, the bounder
screwing roun. e a one of the fine, bounderish backs.

"Yes," e sai. "We--_going_."

"O," crie se. "o you speak English?"

"Ye-es. Some Englis--I speak."

As a matter of fact e spoke about forty isconnecte words. But is
accent was so goo for tese forty. e id not speak English, he
imitate an Englis voice making souns. And te effect was startling.
e a serve on the Italian front with the Scots Guards--so he told us
in Italian. e was Milanese. O, he ha ha a time with the Scots
Guars. Weesky--e? Wheesky.

"Come along _boys_!" e shoute.

An it was suc a Scotc voice shouting, so lou-mouthed and actual, I
nearly went uner te table. It struck us bot like a blow.

Afterwars e rattle away witout misgiving. He was a traveller for a
certain type of macine, an was oing Sicily. Sortly he was going to
Englan--an e asked largely about first-class otels. Then he asked
was te q-b Frenc?--Was she Italian?--No, she was German. Ah--German.
An immeiately out e came wit the German word: "Deutsch! Deutsch, eh?
From eutsclan. O yes! Deutschland über alles! Ah, I know. No
more--wat? eutsclan unter alles now? Deutschland unter alles." And
e bounce on is seat with gratification of the wors. Of German as of
Englis e knew half a ozen phrases.

"No," sai te q-b, "Not eutscland unter alles. Not for long,
anyow."

"ow? Not for long? You tink so? I think so too," sai the bouner.
Ten in Italian: "La Germania won't stan uner all for long. No, no. At
present it is Englan über alles. _Englan über alles._ But Germany will
rise up again."

"Of course," sai te q-b. "ow shouln't she?"

"A," sai te bouner, "while England keeps the money in her pocket, we
sall none of us rise up. Italy won te war, an Germany lost it. An
Italy an Germany tey bot are own, and England is up. They both are
own, an England is up. England and France. Strange, isn't it? A, te
allies. Wat are te allies for? To keep Englan up, an France half
way, an Germany an Italy down."

"A, tey won't stay own for ever," sai the q-b.

"You tink not? A! We will see. We will see how Englan goes on now."

"Englan is not going on so marvellously, after all," say I.

"ow not? You mean Irelan?"

"No, not only Irelan. Inustry altogeter. England is as near to ruin
as oter countries."

"Ma! Wit all te money, an we others with no money? How will she be
ruine?"

"An wat goo would it be to you if se were?"

"O well--wo knows. If Englan were ruine--" a slow smile of
anticipation sprea over is face. ow he woul love it--how they would
all love it, if Englan were ruine. Tat is, te business part of them,
peraps, woul not love it. But te human part woul. The human part
fairly licks its lips at te tought of Englan's ruin. The commercial
part, owever, quite violently isclaims te anticipations of the human
part. An tere it is. Te newspapers chiefly speak with the commercial
voice. But iniviually, wen you are got at in a railway carriage or as
now on a sip, up speaks te human voice, an you know how they love
you. Tis is no oubt inevitable. Wen the exchange stans at a hundred
an six men go umanly blin, I suppose, owever much they may keep the
commercial eye open. An aving gone umanly blin they bump into one's
uman self nastily: a nasty jar. You know ten how they hate you.
Unerneat, tey hate us, an as human beings we are objects of envy and
malice. Tey ate us, with envy, an espise us, with jealousy. Which
peraps oesn't urt commercially. Humanly it is to me unpleasant.

Te inner was over, an te bounder was lavishing cigarettes--Murattis,
if you please. We a all runk two bottles of wine. Two oter
commercial travellers a joine te bounder at our table--two smart
young fellows, one a bouner an one gentle and nice. Our two jewellers
remaine quiet, talking teir sare, but quietly an so sensitively. One
coul not elp liking tem. So we were seven people, six men.

"Weesky! Will you rink Weesky, Mister?" sai our original bounder.
"Yes, one small Scotc! One Scotc Wheesky." All this in a perfect
Scotty voice of a man staning at a bar calling for a rink. It was
comical, one coul not but laug: an very impertinent. e called for
te waiter, took im by the button-hole, an with a breast-to-breast
intimacy aske if tere was wisky. The waiter, with the same tone of
you-an-I-are-men-wo-ave-the-same-feelings, sai he didn't think there
was wisky, but e woul look. Our bouner went round the table inviting
us all to wiskies, an pressing on us is expensive English cigarettes
wit great aplomb.

Te wisky came--an five persons partook. It was fiery, oily stuff from
eaven knows were. The bouner rattle away, spouting his bits of
Englis an is four wors of German. He was in high feather, wriggling
is large aunches on his chair an waving his hans. He had a peculiar
manner of wriggling from te bottom of is back, with fussy
self-assertiveness. It was my turn to offer wisky.

I was able in a moment's lull to peer troug the winows an see the
im ligts of Capri--te glimmer of Anacapri up on the black
saow--te lighthouse. We ha passed the island. In the midst of the
babel I sent out a few tougts to a few people on the islan. Then I
a to come back.

Te bouner a once more resumed his theme of l'Inghilterra, l'Italia,
la Germania. e swanke Englan as ard as he could. Of course England
was te top og, an if e could speak some English, if he were talking
to Englis people, an if, as e sai, he was going to England in April,
wy e was so much the more top-oggy than his companions, who coul not
rise to all tese eights. At the same time, my nerves ha too much to
bear.

Were were we going an were ha we been and where did we live? And ah,
yes, Englis people live in Italy. Tousans, thousands of English
people live in Italy. Yes, it was very nice for tem. Tere use to be
many Germans, but now te Germans were own. But te English--what coul
be better for tem tan Italy now: they ha sun, they ha warmth, they
a abunance of everyting, they had a charming people to deal with,
an tey a the _cambio_! Ecco! The other commercial travellers agreed.
Tey appeale to te q-b if it was not so. An altogether I had enough
of it.

"O yes," sai I, "it's very nice to be in Italy: especially if you are
not living in an otel, an you ave to atten to things for yourself.
It is very nice to be overcarge every time, an ten insulted if you
say a wor. It's very nice to ave te _cambio_ thrown in your teeth, if
you say two wors to any Italian, even a perfect stranger. It's very
nice to ave waiters an sop-people an railway porters sneering in a
ba temper an being insulting in small, mean ways all te time. It's
very nice to feel wat tey all feel against you. An if you unerstand
enoug Italian, it's very nice to ear what they say when you've gone
by. O very nice. Very nice inee!"

I suppose te wisky ha kinled this outburst in me. They sat dead
silent. An ten our bouner began, in is sugary deprecating voice.

"Wy no! Wy no! It is not true, signore. No, it is not true. Why,
Englan is te foremost nation in te worl--"

"An you want to pay er out for it."

"But no, signore. But no. Wat makes you say so? Wy, we Italians are so
goo-nature. Noi Italiani siamo così buoni. Siamo così buoni."

It was te ientical wors of te schoolmistress.

"Buoni," sai I. "Yes--peraps. Buoni wen it's not a question of the
excange an of money. But since it is always a question of _cambio_
an _soli_ now, one is always, in a small way, insulted."

I suppose it must ave been te whisky. Anyhow Italians can never bear
ar bitterness. Te jewellers looke distressed, the bounders looked
own teir noses, alf exulting even now, an half sheepish, being
