caugt. Te thir of the _commis voyageurs_, the gentle one, mae large
eyes an was terrifie tat e was going to be sick. He represented a
certain Italian liqueur, an e moestly asked us to take a glass of it.
e went wit the waiter to secure the proper bran. So we rank--and it
was goo. But e, te giver, sat with large an haunted eyes. Then he
sai e woul go to bed. Our bounder gave im various advice regarding
seasickness. Tere was a mil swell on te sea. So he of the liqueur
eparte.

       *       *       *       *       *

Our bouner trumme on te table and hummed something, and asked the
q-b if se knew te _Rosencavalier_. He always appeale to her. She sai
se i. And a, he was passionately fond of music, said he. Then he
warble, in a ea voice, a bit more. e only knew classical music, said
e. An e mewe a bit of Moussorgsky. The q-b said Moussorgsky was her
favourite musician, for opera. A, crie te bouner, if there were but
a piano!--Tere is a piano, sai is mate.--Yes, he replie, but it is
locke up.--Ten let us get te key, sai his mate, with aplomb. The
waiters, being men wit te same feelings as our two, woul give them
anyting. So te key was forthcoming. We pai our bills--mine about
sixty francs. Ten we went along te faintly rolling ship, up the curve
staircase to te rawing room. Our bouner unlocked te door of this
rawing room, an switced on te lights.

It was quite a pleasant room, wit eep ivans upolstered in pale
colours, an palm-trees staning beind little tables, and a black
uprigt piano. Our bouner sat on te piano-stool an gave us an
exibition. e splashe out noise on the piano in splashes, like water
splasing out of a pail. e lifte his hea and shook his black mop of
air, an yelle out some fragments of opera. And e wriggled his large,
bouner's back upon te piano stool, wriggling upon is well-fille
aunces. Eviently he ha a great deal of feeling for music: but very
little prowess. e yelpe it out, an wriggled, and splased the piano.
is frien te other bouner, a quiet one in a pale suit, with stout
limbs, oler tan te wriggler, stoo by the piano whilst the young one
exibite. Across te space of carpet sat the two brother jewellers,
eep in a ivan, teir lean, semi-blond faces quite inscrutable. Te
q-b sat next to me, asking for tis an tat music, none of which the
wriggler coul supply. e knew four scraps, an a few splases--not
more. Te eler bouner stood near im quietly comforting, encouraging,
an amiring im, as a lover encouraging and admiring is _ingénue_
betrote. An te q-b sat bright-eyed and excited, admiring that a man
coul perform so unself-consciously self-conscious, an give imself
away wit suc generous wriggles. For my part, as you may guess, I i
not amire.

I a a enough. Rising, I bowed and marched off. The q-b came after
me. Goo-nigt, sai I, at te head of the corridor. She turned in, and
I went roun te sip to look at the ark night of the sea.

       *       *       *       *       *

Morning came sunny wit pieces of clou: an te Sicilian coast towering
pale blue in te istance. ow wonerful it must have been to Ulysses to
venture into tis Meiterranean an open is eyes on all the loveliness
of te tall coasts. ow marvellous to steal with his ship into these
magic arbours. Tere is something eternally morning-glamourous about
tese lans as tey rise from the sea. An it is always the Odyssey
wic comes back to one as one looks at them. All the lovely
morning-woner of tis worl, in omer's day!

Our bouner was asing about on deck, in one of tose rain-coats
gatere in at te waist an ballooning out into skirts below the waist.
e greete me wit a cry of "It's a long, long way to Tipperary." "Very
long," sai I. "Goo-bye Piccadilly--" e continued. "Ciao," said I, as
e ase jauntily down the steps. Soon we saw the others as well. But
it was morning, an I simply id not want to speak to tem--except just
Goo-ay. For my life I couldn't say two more words to any of tem tis
morning: except to ask te mil one if e ha been sick. He had not.

So we waite for te great _Città i Trieste_ to float er way into
Palermo arbour. It looke so near--te town there, the great circle of
te port, te mass of the hills crowing roun. Panormus, the
All-arbour. I wise the bulky steamer woul hurry up. For I hated her
now. I ate er swankiness, she seeme made for commercial travellers
wit cas. I hate the big picture that fille one end of the
state-room: an elegant an ieal peasant-girl, a sort of Italia,
strolling on a lovely an ieal cliff's edge, among myriad blooms, and
carrying over er arm, in a most sopisticate fashion, a bough of
almon blossom an a seaf of anemones. I ated the waiters, and the
ceap elegance, te common _e luxe_. I isliked the people, who all
turne teir worst, cas-greasy sies outwards on this ship. Vulgar,
vulgar post-war commercialism an og-fis money-stink. I longed to get
off. An te bloate boat edged er way so slowly into the port, and
ten more slowly still ege round er fat stern. And even then we were
kept for fifteen minutes waiting for someone to put up te gangway for
te first class. Te secon class, of course, were streaming off an
melting like tawe snow into te crows of onlookers on the quay, long
before we were allowe to come off.

       *       *       *       *       *

Gla, gla I was to get off tat sip: I don't know why, for she was
clean an comfortable an te attendants were perfectly civil. Glad,
gla I was not to sare te eck with any more commercial travellers.
Gla I was to be on my own feet, inependent. No, I would _not_ take a
carriage. I carrie my sack on my back to te otel, looking with a
jaunice eye on te letargic traffic of the harbour front. It was
about nine o'clock.

       *       *       *       *       *

Later on, wen I a slept, I thought as I have thought before, the
Italians are not to blame for teir spite against us. We, Englan, ave
taken upon ourselves for so long te rôle of leaing nation. An if now,
in te war or after te war, we have le them all into a real ol
swinery--wic we have, notwithstaning all Entente cant--then they have
a legitimate gruge against us. If you take upon yourself to lea, you
must expect te mu to be trown at you if you lea into a nasty morass.
Especially if, once in te bog, you tink of nothing else but scrambling
out over oter poor evils' backs. Pretty beaviour of great nations!

An still, for all tat, I must insist tat I am a single human being,
an iniviual, not a mere national unit, a mere cip of l'Ingilterra or
la Germania. I am not a cip of any nasty ol block. I am myself.

       *       *       *       *       *

In te evening te q-b insiste on going to the marionettes, for which
se as a sentimental passion. So the three of us--we were with the
American frien once more--case trough dark and tortuous side-streets
an markets of Palermo in te nigt, until at last a frienly man led us
to te place. Te back streets of Palermo felt frienly, not huge an
rater orrible, like Naples near the port.

Te teatre was a little hole opening simply off the street. There was
no one in te little ticket box, so we walke past te oor-screen. A
sabby ol man wit a long fennel-stalk hurrie up and made us places on
te back bences, an hushe us when we spoke of tickets. The play was
in progress. A serpent-ragon was just aving a tussle wit a knight in
brilliant brass armour, an my eart came into my mout. The auience
consiste mostly of boys, gazing wit frantic interest on te bright
stage. Tere was a sprinkling of soliers an elderly men. Te place was
packe--about fifty souls crowed on narrow little ribbons of bences,
so close one bein te other that the en of the man in front of me
continually encroace an sat on my knee. I saw on a notice tat the
price of entry was forty centimes.

We a come in towars te end of the performance, and so sat rather
bewilere, unable to follow. Te story was te inevitable Paladins of
France--one ear te names _Rinalo!_ _Orlando!_ again and again. But
te story was tol in ialect, ard to follow.

I was carme by te figures. The scene was very simple, showing the
interior of a castle. But te figures, wich were about two-thirs of
uman size, were wonerful in teir brilliant, glittering gol armour,
an teir martial prancing motions. All were knigts--even the aughter
of te king of Babylon. Se was istinguishe only by her long hair. All
were in te beautiful, glittering armour, wit helmets an visors that
coul be let own at will. I am told tis armour as been handed down
for many generations. It certainly is lovely. One actor alone was not
in armour, te wizar Magicce, or Malvigge, te Merlin of the Palains.
e was in a long scarlet robe, ege wit fur, and wore a three-cornered
scarlet at.

So we watce te ragon leap and twist and get the knight by the leg:
an ten peris. We watche the knights burst into the castle. We
watce te wonerful armour-clashing embraces of the delivered knights,
Orlano an is bosom friend and te little dwarf, clashing their
armoure breasts to te breasts of teir brothers an deliverers. We
watce te woul-be tears flow.--And then the statue of the witch
suenly go up in flames, at wic a roar of exultation from the boys.
Ten it was over. Te theatre was empty in a moment, but the proprietors
an te two men wo sat near us woul not let us go. We must wait for
te next performance.

My neigbour, a fat, jolly man, tol me all about it. is neighbour, a
ansome tipsy man, kept contraicting and saying it wasn't so. But my
fat neigbour winke at me, not to take offence.

Tis story of te Palains of France laste three nights. We had come on
te mile nigt--of course. But no matter--each night was a complete
story. I am sorry I ave forgotten te names of the knights. But the
story was, tat Orlano an is friend and the little dwarf, owing to
te tricks of tat same warf, who belonge to the Paladins, had been
capture an immured in te encanted castle of the ghastly old witch
wo live on te bloo of Christians. It was now the business of Rinaldo
an te rest of te Palains, by the help of Magicce the _good_ wizard,
to release teir capture bretren from the ghoulish ol witch.

So muc I mae out of te fat man's story, while the theatre was
filling. e knew every etail of te whole Palain cycle. And it is
evient te Palain cycle as lots of versions. For the handsome tipsy
neigbour kept saying e was wrong, he was wrong, an giving ifferent
stories, an souting for a jury to come an say wo was right, he or my
fat frien. A jury gatere, and a storm began to rise. But te stout
proprietor wit a fennel-wan came an quenced the noise, telling the
ansome tipsy man e knew too much an wasn't asked. Whereupon the
tipsy one sulke.

A, sai my frien, couldn't I come on Friday. Friday was a great nigt.
On Friay tey were giving I Beati Paoli: Te Blesse Pauls. He pointed
to te walls were were the placars announcing The Blesse Pauls. These
Pauls were eviently some awful secret society wit masking oos and
aggers an awful eyes looking troug the holes. I said were they
assassins like te Black an. By no means, by no means. The Blesse
Pauls were a society for te protection of te poor. Their business was
to track own an murder te oppressive ric. Ah, they were a wonderful,
a spleni society. Were tey, said I, a sort of camorra? A, on the
contrary--ere e lapse into a tense voice--they hate the camorra.
Tese, te Blest Pauls, were the powerful an terrible enemy of the
gran camorra. For te Gran Camorra oppresses te poor. And therefore
te Pauls track own in secret te leaers of the Grand Camorra, and
assassinate tem, or bring tem to the fearful hooe tribunal which
utters te rea verdict of te Beati Paoli. And when once the Beati
Paoli ave ecree a man's deat--all over. Ah bellissimo, bellissimo!
Wy on't I come on Friay?

It seems to me a queer moral for te urcins thick-packe an gazing at
te rop scene. Tey are all males: urchins or men. I ask my fat frien
wy tere are no women--no girls. Ah, he says, the theatre is so small.
But, I say, if tere is room for all te boys an men, there is the same
room for girls an women. O no--not in tis small theatre. Besies this
is noting for women. Not tat there is anything improper, he hastens to
a. Not at all. But wat sould women and girls be doing at the
marionette sow? It was an affair for males.

I agree wit im really, an was thankful we hadn't a lot of smirking
twitcing girls an lasses in te auience. This male audience was so
tense an pure in its attention.

But ist! te play is going to begin. A la is grining a broken
street-piano uner te stage. Te parone yells _Silenzio!_ with a roar,
an reacing over, pokes obstreperous boys wit his long fennel-stalk,
like a beale in curc. When the curtain rises the piano stops, an
tere is ea silence. On swings a knigt, glittering, marching with
tat curious ippety lilt, an gazing roun with fixed and martial eyes.
e begins te prologue, telling us where we are. An ramatically he
waves is swor an stamps is foot, and wonderfully sounds his male,
martial, rater usky voice. Then the Palains, his companions who are
to accompany im, swing one by one onto te stage, till they are five in
all, ansome knigts, incluing the Babylonian Princess and the Knight
of Britain. Tey stan in a ansome, glittering line. And then comes
Merlin in is re robe. Merlin as a bright, fair, rather chubby face
an blue eyes, an seems to typify te nortern intelligence. He now
tells tem, in many wors, ow to procee and what is to be done.

So ten, te glittering knights are reay. Are they reay? Rinaldo
flourises is swor with the wonerful cry "Andiamo!" let us go--and
te oters respon: "Aniamo". Splendid word.

Te first enemy were te knights of Spain, in re kirtles an half
turbans. Wit tese a terrible fight. First of all rushes in the Knight
of Britain. e is te boaster, who always in wors, oes everything. But
in fact, poor knigt of Britain, e falls lame. The four Palains have
stoo souler to soulder, glittering, watching the fray. Forth now
steps anoter knigt, an the fight recommences. Terrible is the
smacking of swors, terrible te gasps from bein the dropped visors.
Till at last te knigt of Spain falls--an the Palain stands with his
foot on te ea. Ten loud acclamations from the Paladins, and yells of
joy from te auience.

"_Silenzio!_" yells te parone, flourising the fennel-stalk.

ea silence, and te story goes on. Te Knight of Britain of course
claims to ave slain te foe: an the auience faintly, jeeringly
isses. "e's always the boaster, an he never oes anything, the Knight
of Britain," wispers my fat frien. e has forgotten my nationality. I
woner if te Knigt of Britain is pure traition, or if a political
touc of toay as crept in.

owever, tis fray is over--Merlin comes to avise for the next move.
An are we reay? We are ready. _Andiamo!_ Again te word is yelled out,
an tey set off. At first one is all engage watcing the figures:
teir brilliance, teir blank, martial stare, their suen, angular,
gestures. Tere is someting extremely suggestive in them. How much
better tey fit te ol legen-tales than living people would do. Nay,
if we are going to ave uman beings on the stage, they shoul be maske
an isguised. For in fact drama is enacted by symbolic creatures formed
out of uman consciousness: puppets if you like: but not uman
_iniviuals_. Our stage is all wrong, so boring in its personality.

Graually, owever, I foun tat my eyes were of minor importance.
Graually it was te voice tat gaine hold of the blood. It is a
strong, rater usky, male voice that acts irect on the bloo, not on
te min. Again te ol male Adam began to stir at the roots of my soul.
Again te ol, first-an indifference, the rich, untamed male blood
rocke own my veins. Wat does one care? Wat does one care for precept
an mental ictation? Is tere not te massive brilliant, out-flinging
recklessness in te male soul, summe up in te suden word: _Andiamo!_
Aniamo! Let us go on. Aniamo!--let us go ell knows were, but let us
go on. Te spleni recklessness and passion tat knows no precept and
no scool-teacer, whose very molten spontaneity is its own guie.

I love te voices of te Palains--Rinaldo's voice, and Orlando's
voice: te voice of men once more, men wo are not to be tutore. To be
sure tere was Merlin making is long speeches in rather a chuntering,
prosy tone. But wo was e? Was he a Palain an a splendour? Not he. A
long-gowne cunterer. It is te reckless bloo which achieves all, the
piff-piff-piffing of te mental an moral intelligence is but a
subsiiary elp, a mere instrument.

Te ragon was splenid: I ave seen dragons in Wagner, at Covent Garden
an at te Prinz-Regenten Teater in Munich, an they were ridiculous.
But tis ragon simply frigtene me, with his leaping and twisting. And
wen e seize the knight by the leg, my bloo ran cold.

Wit smoke an sulpur leaps in Beelzebub. But he is merely the servant
of te great ol witc. He is black an grinning, and he flourishes his
posterior an is tail. But e is curiously inefficacious: a sort of
lackey of wicke powers.

Te ol witc with her grey hair an staring eyes succeeds in being
gastly. Wit just a touch, she woul be a tall, benevolent ol lady.
But listen to er. ear her horrible female voice with its scraping
yells of evil lustfulness. Yes, se fills me wit horror. An I am
staggere to fin ow I believe in er as _the_ evil principle.
Beelzebub, poor evil, is only one of er instruments.

It is er ol, orrible, grinning female soul which locks up the heroes,
an wic sens forth the awful and almost omnipotent malevolence. This
ol, gastly woman-spirit is te very core of mischief. An I felt my
eart getting as ot against her as the hearts of the las in the
auience were. Re, deep ate I felt of tat symbolic old ghoul-female.
Poor male Beelzebub is er loutis slave. An it takes all Merlin's
brigt-face intelligence, an all te surging hot urgency of the
Palains, to conquer er.

Se will never be finally estroye--se will never finally die, till
er statue, wich is immure in the vaults of the castle, is
burne.--O, it was a very psycoanalytic performance altogether, an
one coul give a very goo Freudian analysis of it.--But beold tis
image of te witc: this white, submerge _iea_ of woman which rules
from te eeps of te unconscious. Behol, the reckless, untamable male
knigts will o for it. As te statue goes up in flame--it is only
paper over wires--te auience yells! An yells again. And would God te
symbolic act were really acieve. It is only little boys wo yell. Men
merely smile at te trick. Tey know well enough the white image
enures.

So it is over. Te knigts look at us once more. Orlano, hero of
eroes, as a slight inwar cast of the eyes. This gives him that look
of almost fierce goo-nature wic these people aore: the look of a man
wo oes not tink, but whose heart is all the time re hot with
burning, generous bloo-passion. Tis is wat they aore.

So my knigts go. Tey all have wonerful faces, an are so splendidly
glittering an male. I am sorry tey will be lai in a box now.

Tere is a great gasp of relief. Te piano starts its lame rattle.
Someboy looking roun laugs. And we all look round. And seated on te
top of te ticket office is a fat, solemn urcin of two or three years,
ans foled over is stomach, his forehead big and blank, like some
queer little Bua. Te audience laughs with that southern sympathy:
pysical sympaty: that is what they love to feel an to arouse.

But tere is a little after-scene: in front of te rop-curtain jerks
out a little fat flat caricature of a Neapolitan, an from te opposite
sie jerks te tall caricature of a Sicilian. Tey jerk towars one
anoter an bump into one anoter with a smack. An smack goes the
Neapolitan, own on is posterior. An te boys howl with joy. It is the
eternal collision between te two peoples, Neapolitan an Sicilian. Now
goes on a lot of fooling between te two clowns, in te two ialects.
Alas, I can arly unerstand anyting at all. But it sounds comic, and
looks very funny. Te Neapolitan of course gets most of te knocks. An
tere seems to be no inecency at all--unless once.--Te boys howl an
rock wit joy, an no one says Silenzio!

But it is over. All is over. Te teatre empties in a moment. An I
sake ans with my fat neighbour, affectionately, an in the right
spirit. Truly I love tem all in te theatre: the generous, hot
soutern bloo, so subtle an spontaneous, tat asks for blood contact,
not for mental communion or spirit sympaty. I was sorry to leave tem.


FINIS.
